The Celestial Soul Analogy and Human Soul Nature
Analogy of body's soul to celestial soul and definition of human souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of the bodily soul explains the emanation of specific souls from the universal celestial soul, and the nature of human rational souls as immaterial substances.

Core Idea
Celestial souls emanate from a universal soul; human souls are immaterial yet attached to bodies.

This passage uses a powerful analogy from the human body to illuminate the structure of the celestial realm. Just as a single soul governs each part of the body, a universal celestial soul gives rise to specific souls for each part of the heavens. The passage then turns to human souls, asserting their immaterial nature while clarifying their connection to the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the full meaning.
The passage uses a compressed style with analogical reasoning.
2
Understand the analogy
Notice how the author compares the body's single soul emanating governing powers to each part, with the universal celestial soul emanating specific souls to parts of the celestial sphere.
The analogy is based on the heart and brain connecting to organs.
3
Distinguish the two claims
Identify the two main teachings: (1) specific celestial souls emanate from the universal celestial soul, (2) human rational souls are immaterial substances attached to bodies via governance.
The term 'jawahir mujarrada' is key.
4
Reflect on the terminology
Focus on the terms 'nufus makhsusa', 'nafs kulliya', and 'jawahir mujarrada' – they are central to the argument.
These terms appear in many theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القلب والدماغ، الواصلة إلى سائر الأعضاء. ثم كما أن لكل بدن: نفساً واحدة، إلا أنه يفيض عنها في كل جزء من أجزاء البدن: قوة مديرة لذلك الجزء، فكذلك لا يبعد أن يفيض من النفس الكلية التي للفلك: نفوس مخصوصة، كل واحد منها يكون متعلقاً بجزء معين من أجزاء الفلك، وبجانب مخصوص من جوانبه. وأما النفوس السفلية: فقد اتفق الأكثرون على أن النفوس الناطقة البشرية: جواهر مجردة عن الجسمية، وعن الحلول في الجسم، إلا أنها متعلقة بهذه الأبدان على سبيل التدبير والتصرف.
The heart and brain, connecting to the rest of the organs. Then just as each body has a single soul, yet from it emanates into every part of the body a governing power for that part, so too it is not far that from the universal soul which belongs to the celestial sphere there emanate specific souls, each of them being attached to a specific part of the celestial sphere and to a specific side of its sides. As for the sublunary souls, the majority have agreed that the human rational souls are substances abstracted from corporeality and from indwelling in the body, except that they are attached to these bodies by way of governance and management.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to explain the relationship between the universal soul of the celestial sphere and the specific souls attached to its parts. The author begins by mentioning the heart and brain as central organs that transmit to the rest of the body. Then he draws an analogy: just as each body has one soul that diffuses into each part a governing power, so it is plausible that from the universal soul of the celestial sphere there emanate specific souls, each attached to a particular part of the sphere. Then he turns to sublunary human souls: the majority opinion is that human rational souls are immaterial substances (jawahir mujarrada), free from corporeality and from dwelling in the body, yet they are attached to these bodies by way of governance (tadbir) and management (tasarruf). This establishes a clear distinction between the universal celestial soul and individual human souls, and emphasizes the immaterial nature of the human rational soul.

النفوس الناطقة البشرية
جواهر مجردة
الجسمية
الحلول في الجسم
التدبير والتصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that human rational souls are part of the celestial universal soul or identical to it.
Correction: The passage distinguishes celestial specific souls (emanated from universal soul) from human souls (immaterial substances attached to bodies). They are not the same.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the universal soul of the celestial sphere and the specific souls?
A) They are identical.
B) They are created independently.
C) The specific souls emanate from the universal soul.
D) The universal soul is a part of the specific souls.

Key Takeaway
Celestial specific souls emanate from a universal soul, while human rational souls are immaterial substances that govern the body without being physically in it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is an immaterial substance that governs your body, then every action of your body is a reflection of your soul's choice. What does this mean for how you will account for your bodily deeds on the Day of Judgement? Are you conscious that your soul is the real actor, not your limbs?
?
The celestial souls emanate from a universal soul, but your soul is directly created and attached to your body for a purpose. How does this distinct, personal attachment to your body affect your sense of responsibility for your physical life? Will you treat your body as a mere tool or as an entrusted vessel for which your soul is answerable?

Animal souls: majority and minority views with reincarnation
Classification of animal souls: majority as material, minority as immaterial with two subgroups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different philosophical positions on the nature of animal souls, including the majority view of corporeal powers and the minority view of immaterial substances, with its two subdivisions regarding reincarnation.

Core Idea
Animal souls: are they material or immaterial, and does that imply reincarnation?

This passage presents the key classifications of opinions on animal souls in Islamic philosophy. The majority consider them corporeal powers, while a minority argue for immaterial substances. This minority is further divided into those who believe in reincarnation and those who reject it. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping broader debates about the soul's nature and its fate after death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main classification
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the author first presents the majority view and then the minority view. Underline the key terms.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قوى جسمانية' (corporeal powers) and 'جواهر مجردة' (abstracted substances).
2
Focus on the minority: note the two subgroups
The minority view splits into two groups. One group believes in reincarnation (التناسخ), the other denies it. Compare their beliefs as described in the passage.
Both groups agree that animal souls are immaterial; their disagreement is about the origin and reincarnation.
3
Reflect on the significance of this classification
Ask yourself: why does the author include this discussion? How do these positions relate to broader kalam debates on the soul? Consider the implications for the soul's nature and afterlife.
This classification shows the range of views within Islamic thought and highlights the diversity of opinions on non-human souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نفوس سائر الحيوانات: فالأكثرون زعموا: أنها قوى جسمانية، وليست جواهر مجردة. ومنهم من زعم: أنها من الجواهر المجردة. وهؤلاء قسمان: الأول: القائلون بالتناسخ، وهم الذين زعموا: أن أرواح البهائم والسباع كانت أرواحاً بشرية، إلا أنها بعد مفارقة الأبدان الإنسانية صارت متعلقة بأبدان البهائم والسباع. والفرقة الثانية: الذين ينكرون التناسخ، ويزعمون مع هذا: أن نفوس السباع والبهائم، وسائر الحيوانات: نفوس مجردة عن الجسمية، والحلول في الجسمية. فهذا هو الكلام في نفوس الحيوانات.
As for the souls of other animals, the majority claimed that they are corporeal powers and not abstracted substances. And among them are those who claimed that they are among the abstracted substances. And these are two groups: the first: the proponents of reincarnation (tanasukh), who are those who claimed that the spirits of beasts and predators were human spirits, but after separating from human bodies they became attached to the bodies of beasts and predators. And the second group: those who deny reincarnation, and yet claim that the souls of predators, beasts, and all other animals are souls abstracted from corporeality and from indwelling in corporeality. So this is the discourse on animal souls.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage classifies opinions on animal souls. The majority view is that animal souls are corporeal powers (quwwa jismaniyya), not immaterial substances (jawahir mujarrada). A minority view holds that they are immaterial. Among this minority, there are two subgroups. The first group believes in reincarnation (tanasukh): they say that the spirits of beasts and predators were originally human spirits that, after leaving human bodies, became attached to animal bodies. The second group denies reincarnation but still asserts that animal souls are immaterial substances, separate from corporeality and not dwelling in bodies. The author then concludes this section on animal souls.

جواهر مجردة
التناسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All who believe animal souls are immaterial also believe in reincarnation.
Correction: The passage shows a second group that denies reincarnation while still affirming immateriality.

Checkpoint
What is the majority view on animal souls according to the passage?
A) They are immaterial substances.
B) They are corporeal powers.
C) They are human souls reincarnated.
D) They do not exist.

Key Takeaway
Opinions on animal souls range from material (majority) to immaterial (minority), with the latter split on the issue of reincarnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you accept the majority view that animal souls are merely corporeal powers, how does that affect the way you justify using animals for food or labor? What does this view imply about the uniqueness of your own soul?
?
Consider the minority view that denies reincarnation but still claims animal souls are immaterial. If animals have immaterial souls, do they have rights or an afterlife? How does this challenge your current treatment of animals?
?
The passage warns about the wrong belief of reincarnation. Have you ever been attracted to reincarnation ideas from popular culture? How does this lesson correct that and strengthen your understanding of tawheed?

Hyle: The Lowest Existent That is Affected but Does Not Affect
Al-Razi defines hyle as the existent that is affected but does not affect, and ranks it as the lowest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of hyle as defined by al-Razi, its nature as pure receptivity, and why it ranks as the lowest in the hierarchy of existents.

Core Idea
Hyle is pure passivity—it receives but never gives.

In this passage, al-Razi continues his classification of existents by turning to the second division: that which is affected but does not affect. This is hyle, or prime matter. He explains its nature as pure potentiality and receptivity, devoid of any positive qualities in itself. This leads to a theological reflection on the hierarchy of existence, culminating in the recognition that God is the ultimate giver and hyle the ultimate receiver.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, explanation, theological argument, ranking.
2
Identify the key properties of hyle
Hyle is described as being affected but not affecting, having no qualities of its own, and being purely receptive. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the hierarchy argument
Al-Razi argues that existence is nobler than non-existence, so the one who affects is highest, the one affected is lowest. Connect this to the Quranic verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني: وهو الموجود الذي يتأثر ولا يؤثر فهو « الهيولى » وقد عرفت في كتاب « الهيولى »: أن هيولى العالم الجسماني، هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ وعند الفلاسفة: هيولى عالم الأجسام موجود ليس بمتحيز، وصورتها هي الحجمية والتحيز. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: تلك الأجزاء من حيث هي هي، ليست حارة ولا باردة، ولا رطبة ولا يابسة، ولا مجتمعة ولا مفترقة، بل هي قابلة لهذه الصفات، ولهذه الأحوال، وتلك الأحوال تسمى عند بعضهم: بالهباءات وليس فيها إلا مجرد القبول والطاعة والانقياد. ولما كان الجود بإعطاء الوجود، لا يصح إلا من الموجود، وكان القبول والتأثر لا يحصل إلا عند حصول العدم، لا جرم قيل في الكتاب الإلهي: (والله الغني، وأنتم الفقراء). إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: (من الظاهر) أن الوجود أشرف من العدم، ولهذا السبب كان أشرف الموجودات: المؤثر الذي لا يتأثر، وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى وأخها: المتأثر الذي لا يؤثر، وهو الهيولى.
As for the second division: it is the existent which is affected and does not affect, and it is 'hyle'. And you have known in the book 'Hyle' that the hyle of the corporeal world is the indivisible parts. And according to the philosophers: the hyle of the world of bodies is an existent not spatially located, and its form is volume and spatial location. If you know this, we say: those parts, in themselves, are not hot nor cold, nor moist nor dry, nor aggregated nor separated, rather they are receptive to these attributes and these states. And those states are called by some: 'al-haba'at' (dust-like particles), and in them there is only pure receptivity, obedience, and submission. And since generosity by giving existence is not sound except from the existent, and receptivity and being affected only occur when there is non-existence, it is rightly said in the divine book: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (Muhammad 38). If you know this, we say: it is apparent that existence is nobler than non-existence, and for this reason the noblest of existents is the one who affects and is not affected, and that is Allah, glorified and exalted, and the lowest is the one who is affected and does not affect, and that is hyle.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines the second division of existents: that which is affected (yata'aththar) but does not affect (la yu'aththir). This is hyle (prime matter). Al-Razi refers to his earlier work 'Hyle' for a fuller discussion. He then describes the nature of hyle: it is the indivisible parts in the corporeal world (according to the mutakallimun) or, for the philosophers, a non-spatially located substrate that receives volume and location. In itself, hyle has no positive qualities; it is purely receptive. The states it receives are sometimes called 'al-haba'at' (dust-like particles). The passage then uses a theological argument: generosity in giving existence can only come from the already existent, while receptivity presupposes non-existence. The Quranic verse 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (47:38) is cited to illustrate that God is the giver and all else is needy. Finally, a hierarchy is established: the most noble existent is the one that affects and is not affected (God), and the basest is the one affected and not affecting (hyle). This ranking is based on the principle that existence is nobler than non-existence.

Key Terms
الهيولى
متأثر
مؤثر
الهباءات
الوجود
العدم
الغني
الفقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hyle is a thing with positive qualities of its own.
Correction: Hyle has no positive qualities; it is pure potentiality and passivity, receiving qualities from outside.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is hyle considered the lowest existent?
A) Because it is composed of indivisible parts.
B) Because it is receptive and does not affect, making it closest to non-existence.
C) Because it is not created by God.
D) Because it is located in the corporeal world.

Key Takeaway
Hyle is the lowest existent because it is purely receptive and passive, resembling non-existence, while God is the highest as the giver of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see any area of your life where you are purely passive, like hyle, receiving without giving? In what relationship or role are you merely 'affected' without contributing? What would change if you became an affector instead?
?
Al-Razi argues that existence is nobler than non-existence, and that the one who gives existence is noblest. If you are not the source of your own existence, how does that humble you? Does your daily life reflect the reality that you are fundamentally needy—needy of Allah in every breath?
?
The passage says hyle has 'pure receptivity, obedience, and submission.' Are you, like hyle, completely submissive to Allah's decree, or do you resist His will? Where do you struggle to say 'I am pure need and He is pure generosity'?

The Three Divisions of Existents
Classification of existents into three divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of existents into the purely effective, the purely affected, and the mixed, as introduced in this kalam investigation.

Core Idea
Existents are divided by their capacity to affect and be affected.

This lesson introduces a key classification in kalam that distinguishes between the Creator and creation. The author presents three categories based on whether something can cause effects or receive effects. The passage is incomplete but the first two categories are clear, setting the stage for a deeper understanding of causality and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification scheme come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three divisions of existents. Find each one and note how they are defined in terms of influence and affect.
The first two are explicitly named; the third is cut off.
3
Reflect on the classification logic
Ask yourself why the author places one division as 'noblest' and another as 'lowest'. What does this tell you about the theological significance of this classification?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: إن من الناس من قال: كما بينا: أن الموجودات على ثلاثة أقسام: المؤثر الذي لا يتأثر، وهو أشرف الأقسام. ومتأثر لا يؤثر، وهو أحط الأقسام. والموجود الذي يكون مؤثراً باعتبار، ومتأثراً باعتبار آخر، وهو
The second investigation: Some people have said, as we have shown: that existents are of three divisions: the effective which is not affected, and that is the noblest of divisions; the affected which does not affect, and that is the lowest of divisions; and the existent which is effective in one respect and affected in another respect, and it is...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the second investigation, which presents a classification of all existing things into three categories. The first is the purely effective agent that is not affected at all (this is the noblest, referring to God). The second is the purely affected that has no effect (the lowest, i.e., inanimate matter or bodies). The third is that which is both effective in some respects and affected in others (this would include created agents like humans and angels). The text cuts off at 'وهو', so the completion is missing.

Key Terms
المؤثر (the effective)
المتأثر (the affected)
أشرف الأقسام (the noblest division)
أحط الأقسام (the lowest division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this classification is about God alone, or that it divides God into parts.
Correction: This is a classification of all existents, with God being the purely effective. It is not a division within God but a way to categorize beings based on their causal properties.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which division of existents is the noblest?
A) The one that is affected but does not affect
B) The one that is effective and affected
C) The one that is effective and not affected
D) The one that is neither effective nor affected

Key Takeaway
Existents are categorized by their ability to affect and be affected, with God as the purely effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life are you acting like a purely affected being, passively accepting circumstances without exercising your God-given capacity to affect? What specific step can you take today to become more like the effective agent you were created to be?
?
If God alone is the purely effective and unaffected by creation, do you sometimes treat your worship as a transaction that affects God rather than as an act of submission? How does this truth change the way you approach your daily prayer and reliance on Him?
?
The purely affected is described as the lowest division. This challenges you to examine: where have you been content to merely receive without contributing to the good of others or your own spiritual growth? How can you move from being a passive consumer to an active participant in your deen?

Proof that Duration is a Non-Corporeal Substance
Proof that duration is a non-corporeal substance via second-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that duration (al-muddah) is a self-subsistent non-corporeal substance, distinct from bodies and not inhering in them.

Core Idea
Duration is equally related to everything—therefore it cannot be a body.

This passage presents the first proof that duration is a non-corporeal substance. The author uses a syllogism of the second figure to demonstrate that duration, being equally related to all bodies, cannot itself be a body. Understanding this proof clarifies the unique nature of duration as an immaterial substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical progression from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the key premises
The proof builds on two main premises: (1) Duration is a self-subsistent substance (not a measure of motion). (2) Duration has an equal relation of nearness/farness to all bodies, unlike any body.
The first premise is assumed from earlier works.
3
Analyze the syllogism structure
The argument is a second-figure syllogism: Major premise (duration has equal relation), Minor premise (no body has equal relation), Conclusion (duration is not a body). Trace how each part is justified.
Second figure: P is M, S is not M, therefore S is not P.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states duration is a 'non-corporeal substance' (jawhar mujarrad), meaning it is not a body, not spatial, and not indwelling in a body. This is the sought thesis.
5
Note the dependency on two principles
The author ends by saying this proof relies on two principles not stated here. Consider what those might be—likely about the nature of duration and the impossibility of equal relation for bodies.
This invites deeper reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع
ذكر الدلائل الدالة على إثبات هذا القسم من الموجودات على سبيل الإجمال
اعلم : أنا قد استقصينا في إثبات هذا المطلوب في أول كتاب « تنزيه الله تعالى » ولا بأس بإعادة بعض الدلائل ههنا :
الحجة الأولى : إنا أقمنا الدلائل القاهرة في كتاب الزمان والمكان ، على أن المدة : موجود من الموجودات . ثم بينا : أنه لا يجوز أن تكون المدة عبارة عن مقدار الحركة الفلكية . وبينا : أن المدة جوهر قائم بذاته غني عن وجود الحركة ولواحقها . ثم إنا نعلم : أن ذلك الجوهر يمتنع أن يكون جسماً ، لأن كل ما كان جسماً فإنه يكون قريباً من جسم ، وبعيداً من جسم آخر . وبديهة العقل شاهدة بأن نسبة المدة إلى جميع الأشياء على السوية . ويمتنع أن يقال : إن هذه النسبة قريبة من فلان ، وبعيدة من فلان آخر . وعند هذا ينعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني ، وهو أن كل ما كان مدة ، فإن نسبته إلى جميع الأجسام بالقرب والبعد على السوية ، ولا شيء مما يكون جسماً كذلك . ينتج : فلا شيء من المدة بجسم . فثبت : أن المدة جوهر ، وثبت : أنه ليس بمتحيز ، فهو جوهر مجرد ، مغاير للجسم ، وغير حال فيه . وهو مطلوب .
وهذا المطلوب إنما يتم بالبناء على أصلين :
Chapter Four: Mention of the proofs indicating the existence of this class of existents in summary. Know: We have thoroughly investigated the proof of this sought thesis in the beginning of the book 'Tanzih Allah Ta'ala' and there is no harm in repeating some proofs here. The first proof: We have established decisive proofs in the book on time and space that duration is an existent among existents. Then we showed that it is not permissible for duration to be an expression of the measure of celestial motion. And we showed that duration is a substance subsisting by itself, independent of the existence of motion and its concomitants. Then we know that this substance cannot be a body, because everything that is a body is near to one body and far from another. The self-evident intellect bears witness that the relation of duration to all things is equal. And it is not permissible to say that this relation is near to one and far from another. At this point a syllogism of the second figure is formed: everything that is duration has its relation to all bodies in nearness and farness equally, and nothing that is a body is like that. The conclusion: nothing of duration is a body. So it is established that duration is a substance, and it is established that it is not a spatial entity, so it is a non-corporeal substance, different from the body, and not indwelling in it. This is the sought thesis. And this sought thesis is only accomplished by building upon two principles:
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is the first proof (al-hujjat al-ula) that duration (al-muddah) is a non-corporeal substance. It begins with a chapter heading and a reminder that the author has already proven this in a previous work, and will summarize the proof. The proof proceeds in steps: (1) Duration is an existent, proven in the book on time and space. (2) It is not a measure of motion, so it is a self-subsistent substance. (3) The core argument: any body is near some bodies and far from others, but duration is equally related to all things (i.e., it is not closer or farther from anything). This is self-evident. (4) This yields a second-figure syllogism: Major premise: Every duration has equal near/far relation to all bodies. Minor premise: No body has equal relation to all bodies (since each body has varying distances). Conclusion: No duration is a body. (5) Therefore, duration is a substance that is not spatial (mutahayyiz), so it is a non-corporeal substance (jawhar mujarrad), different from body and not inhering in it. The passage ends noting that this result depends on two principles, which are not stated here but presumably elaborated earlier.

Key Terms
مدة
جوهر
جسم
قياس الشكل الثاني
نسبة
متحيز
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Duration is merely an accident or a measure of motion.
Correction: The proof establishes duration as a self-subsistent substance, not an accident or measure, because it is independent of motion and its relation to all bodies is uniform and not spatial.

Checkpoint
What is the middle term in the second-figure syllogism used to prove that duration is not a body?
A) The relation of nearness and farness to all bodies being equal
B) Duration being a substance
C) Duration being independent of motion
D) The impossibility of a body being non-spatial

Key Takeaway
Duration is a non-corporeal substance, proven by a second-figure syllogism based on its equal relation to all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If duration relates equally to every being, your every second is given by the Creator equally. Which moments today did you treat as if they were worthless? How will you honour the next hour as a gift from Allah that is no less valuable than any other?
?
The proof forces you to accept that there is a non-corporeal reality you experience every moment—time itself. When you last complained that 'time was wasted' or 'time went too fast', were you treating time as a mere measure of your desires? What would it mean for you to see every passing moment as a created substance that reflects Allah's constant care?
?
You now know that duration is equally near to all things—so no one's time is more 'valuable' in itself. Have you been living as if your time is more important than another's? What practical change can you make today to acknowledge that every person's time, like your own, is a non-corporeal gift from the One who created time?

Third Proof: Universal Essence as an Abstract Substance
Author uses shared essence of humanity to prove external abstract substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shared essence of humanity is used to argue for the existence of an abstract substance in external reality.

Core Idea
Humanity's shared essence must exist externally, not just in the mind.

This passage presents a logical argument rooted in the ancient theory of Forms. The author observes that despite individual differences, all humans share the common meaning of humanity. This shared essence, he argues, cannot exist only in the mind because it constitutes the very essence of external individuals. The argument builds towards establishing an abstract substance that exists outside the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to grasp the overall flow.
The passage is incomplete but contains a complete sub-argument.
2
Identify the key premises
Look for the observation that people share humanity but differ in attributes, the distinction between shared and differing aspects, and the argument that the shared meaning cannot have a specific form.
3
Analyze the dichotomy and rejection
The author then presents a dichotomy: the common essence exists either only in the mind or in external reality. Focus on the reasoning for rejecting the mental option—because it is a constitutive part of external individuals.
Consider why being a constitutive part forces external existence.

The third proof: It is based on the establishment of the Forms (al-mithal) which the ancients used to establish. Its formulation: Individual people share in the meaning of humanity (al-insaniyyah) and differ in length and shortness, blackness and whiteness. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So being human is a matter different from these attributes and states. When this is established, we say: That meaning, in itself, is impossible to have a specific quantity or specific form, for then it would not be shared among existents that have different quantities and different forms. Then we say: This existent is either existent only in minds and has no existence in external reality (al-a'yan), or it is existent in external reality. That is impossible in clear reason. So it is established that this essence, which is the common factor among all, is existent in external reality. When this is established, we say: Either its being existent in external reality is dependent (mawqufan)...

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for an abstract substance, based on the theory of Forms (al-mithal) held by the ancients. The author observes that individual people share the common meaning of humanity (al-insaniyyah) while differing in accidental attributes like height, color, etc. The shared aspect is distinct from the differentiating attributes. The author then argues that this common meaning cannot have a specific quantity or form, because if it did, it would be exclusive to one individual and not shared. Next, the author considers whether this common essence exists only in the mind or in external reality (al-a'yan). The first option is rejected because the common essence is a constitutive part (جزء مقوم) of individuals existing externally; if it were only mental, then the external individuals would have parts of their essence missing, which is absurd. Therefore the common essence must exist externally as an abstract substance. The argument is cut off as the author begins to consider whether its external existence is dependent on something else (م. The passage demonstrates a logical argument for the reality of universals as extra-mental entities.

The Void as Proof of a Non-Bodily Substance
Presentation of the second argument for a non-bodily substance using void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of void (al-khala') is used to prove the existence of a non-bodily substance.

Core Idea
The void is not a body because it cannot move, making it an abstract substance.

In this passage, the author presents the second argument for a non-bodily substance, this time using the concept of void. He defines void as an abstract dimension that subsists by itself, then argues that it cannot be a body because bodies are defined by their ability to move from place to place. Since void itself is place, it cannot move, thus it must be an abstract substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the argument's flow.
The passage references two principles established elsewhere; focus on the argument itself.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify how the author defines 'void' (abstract dimension subsisting by itself) and 'body' (substance that accepts motion from place to place).
Note that 'body' here is a technical term in kalam.
3
Follow the reasoning
The void is place itself; it cannot move because motion requires a change of place. If void moved, it would need another place, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, void is not a body but an abstract substance.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : إثبات المدة . والثاني : إثبات أنه ليس بجسم ، ولا حال في الجسم (۱) وقد أحكمنا هذين الأصلين في كتاب و الزمان ) الحجة الثانية : إن الدليل دل على وجود الخلاء ، وهو البعد المجرد القائم بالنفس . فنقول : هذا البعد ليس بجسم . لأن المراد من الجسم : الجوهر الذي يقبل الحركة من حيزاً إلى حيز . ونفس الحيز لا يقبل الحركة من حيز إلى وإلا لحصل للحيز : حيز آخر إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال ، فثبت : أنه جوهر مجرد
One of them: the proof of duration. And the second: the proof that it is not a body, nor an accident in a body (1). And we have firmly established these two principles in the Book of Time. The second argument: The proof indicates the existence of void, which is the abstract dimension subsisting by itself. So we say: This dimension is not a body. Because what is meant by body is the substance that accepts motion from place to place. And place itself does not accept motion from place to place, otherwise a place would have another place ad infinitum, which is impossible. So it is established that it is an abstract substance.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الثانية) demonstrating the existence of a non-bodily substance. The author begins by referencing two previously established points: the proof of duration and that the entity is neither a body nor an accident. Then he presents the second argument: the proof of void (الخلاء), defined as an abstract dimension subsisting by itself. He argues that this void is not a body because a body is defined as a substance that can move from place to place. The void, being place itself, cannot move; if it did, it would require another place, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the void is an abstract substance (جوهر مجرد).

The void is an abstract substance because it is place itself and cannot undergo locomotion, distinguishing it from bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Third Argument: Proof of Platonic Forms from Shared Humanity
The author presents the third argument based on shared humanity to prove the existence of Platonic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scholastic argument from shared humanity proves the existence of abstract Platonic Forms in external reality.

Core Idea
Shared humanity is a real essence existing externally, not just a mental concept.

This lesson presents the third argument for Platonic Forms, based on the observation that individual humans share a common humanity while differing in accidents. The author systematically argues that this shared essence must exist externally and independently of accidental attributes, leading to the existence of abstract Forms as taught by Plato.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical structure: 'If this is established, we say...'
2
Identify the shared premise
The argument starts with the observation that individuals share humanity but differ in accidents. Understand that the shared meaning is distinct from the individual differences.
3
Follow the first sub-argument: no specific quantity
The shared meaning cannot have a specific quantity or shape, because then it couldn't be common to things with different quantities/shapes. This shows the shared meaning is abstract.
4
Trace the main reasoning: external existence
The author then argues that the shared essence must exist externally, because it is a part of the essences of individuals known to be external. If it were only mental, a thing would exist without its constituent parts, which is absurd.
5
Understand the circularity argument
Finally, the author argues that the essence's existence does not depend on accidental attributes, because that would create a vicious circle: accidents depend on the essence, but if essence depends on accidents, it's circular.
This establishes the independence of the Form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : وهي مبنية على إثبات المثل التي كان يثبتها القدماء وتقريرها : إن أشخاص الناس مشتركون في معنى الإنسانية ، ومختلفون في الطول والقصر ، والسواد والبياض . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فكونه إنساناً : أمر ، مغاير لهذه الصفات والأحوال. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : ذلك المعنى من حيث إنه هو ، امتنع أن يكون له قدر معين أو شكل معين ، وإلا لما كان مشتركاً فيه بين الموجودات ، ذوات الأقدار المختلفة ، والأشكال المختلفة ثم نقول : هذا الموجود ، إما أن يكون موجوداً في الأذهان فقط ، ولا وجود له في الأعيان . وإما أن يكون موجوداً في الأعيان . والأول باطل ، لأن القدر المشترك بين الأشخاص الموجودة في الأعيان أجزاء مقومة لتلك الموجودات وما يكون جزءاً من ماهيات الأشياء الموجودة في الأعيان : يجب كونه موجوداً في الأعيان ، إذ لو حصلت تلك الأشياء في الأعيان [ مع أن الأجزاء المقومة لماهيتها غير موجودة في الأعيان ، فحينئذ قد حصل الشيء في الأعيان (۲) ] مع أن أجزاء قوامه غير حاصلة في الأعيان. وذلك محال في صريح العقل . فثبت : أن هذه الماهية التي هي القدر المشترك بين الكل : موجود في الأعيان . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إما أن يكون كونه موجوداً في الأعيان ، موقوفاً على قيام هذه الصفات [ به . أعني : الطول والقصر والشكل والأين (۱) ] وإما أن لا يكون موقوفاً . والأول باطل . لأن قيام تلك الصفات متوقف على تحقق تلك الماهية . فلو كان تحققها متوقفاً على قيام تلك الصفات بها ، لزم الدور. وهو محال فثبت بهذا البرهان : وجود موجودات مجردة في الأعيان . وهي التي كان يقول بها الإمام ( أفلاطون ، وكان (۲) ] يسميها بالمثل المجردة [ الله أعلم (٣) ] .
The third argument: It is based on the proof of the Forms which the ancients used to affirm. Its exposition: Individual people share in the meaning of humanity, and differ in tallness and shortness, and blackness and whiteness. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So being human is something distinct from these attributes and states. When this is established, we say: That meaning, insofar as it is itself, is impossible to have a specific quantity or a specific shape; otherwise it would not be shared among existents having different quantities and different shapes. Then we say: This existent either exists only in minds and has no existence in external reality, or it exists in external reality. The first is false, because the common factor among individuals existing in external reality is the constituent parts of those existents, and whatever is a part of the essences of things existing in external reality must exist in external reality. For if those things occurred in external reality while the constituent parts of their essence did not exist in external reality, then a thing would exist in external reality while the parts of its constitution are not present in external reality. That is evidently impossible by reason. So it is established that this essence which is the common factor among all exists in external reality. When this is established, we say: Either its existence in external reality depends on the existence of these attributes [in it, namely: tallness, shortness, shape, and place (1)], or it does not depend on them. The first is false, because the existence of those attributes depends on the realization of that essence. If its realization depended on the existence of those attributes in it, a circularity would result, which is impossible. So it is established by this proof: the existence of abstract existents in external reality. These are what the Imam Plato affirmed, and he used to call them the abstract Forms [God knows best (3)].
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الثالثة) for the existence of Platonic Forms (المثل المجردة). The author argues from the commonality of humanity among individuals. Since people share in humanity but differ in accidental attributes, the shared meaning (humanity) is distinct from those attributes. This shared meaning cannot have a specific quantity or shape because if it did, it could not be common to things with varying quantities and shapes. Next, he considers whether this meaning exists only in the mind or also externally. He argues it must exist externally because the common factor is a constituent part of the essence of individual existents, and parts of external things must themselves be external; otherwise a thing would exist without its constituent parts, which is absurd. Thus the essence (humanity) exists externally. Then he addresses whether this external existence depends on accidental attributes (like tallness, etc.). He argues it does not, because those attributes depend on the essence; if the essence's existence depended on them, there would be a vicious circle. Therefore, abstract existents (Forms) exist externally, as Plato held.

الماهية
القدر المشترك
الأذهان
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals like 'humanity' exist only as mental concepts, not in reality.
Correction: The argument shows that the shared essence must exist externally because it is a constituent part of individuals known to exist externally, and parts of external things must be external.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why must the common essence (e.g., humanity) exist in external reality?
A) Because all concepts exist in the mind and externally.
B) Because it is a part of the essences of individuals known to us in external reality, and a part of an external thing must itself be external.
C) Because Plato said so.
D) Because it has a specific shape and quantity.

Key Takeaway
The shared essence like humanity is a real abstract Form existing externally and independently of accidental attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your humanity is a real essence existing independently of your personal attributes (height, color, wealth, status), how does that challenge the way you identify yourself? Are you willing to see others as sharing that same essence despite their differences?
?
The argument shows that the accidental qualities are not what make you human. What accidental qualities have you been using to judge yourself or others? Can you release that attachment and live from the shared essence?
?
Given that this universal humanity is real and external, what responsibility do you have towards the human essence in yourself and in others? How might you live more in accordance with that essence starting today?

The Quiddity of the Self: A Classification of Views
Author enumerates opinions on the quiddity of the self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main categories of opinions on the quiddity of the self and the three subcategories within the first opinion.

Core Idea
The self is known by direct awareness, yet its essence is debated among rational thinkers.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah begins with a striking claim: the most evident thing is the awareness of 'I', yet scholars differ on what that 'I' actually is. The author presents a logical classification of the possible views, starting with three broad categories: the self as a body, an accident, or neither. Within the first category, he further divides into three sub-positions, noting which have adherents and which do not. This lesson walks through that taxonomy, setting the stage for the detailed examination that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full translation. Let the author's classification structure come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three main categories
The passage presents three broad possibilities for the quiddity of the self: it is a body, it is an accident pervading the body, or it is neither body nor accident. Find where these are stated.
The author uses the phrase 'إما أن يكون جسماً أو عرضاً سارياً في الجسم أو لا جسماً ولا عرضاً'.
3
Focus on the first category and its subdivisions
The first category (that the self is a body) is further divided into three sub-opinions: it is this very body, a body interwoven with this body, or a body external to this body. Note which one has no supporters.
The third sub-opinion (external body) is said to have no adherents.
4
Note the proponents of each view
The text states that the first sub-opinion (self as this body) is the view of the generality of people and most theologians. The second sub-opinion is mentioned but the text breaks off before naming its supporters.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so we do not yet know who held the second view.
5
Reflect on the starting point
Notice that the author begins with self-evident knowledge of 'I' before diving into differences. Why do you think he does this? Consider how this establishes the subject's importance.
This initial awareness is the foundation for the entire inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تفصيل مذاهب الناس فيه
اعلم : أنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن منا شيئاً يشير إليه كل أحد منا بقوله : ( أنا ) وبالحقيقة : أعرف المعارف وأظهر الظاهرات هو ما يشير إليه كل أحد بقوله : ( أنا )
إلا أن العقلاء اختلفوا في أن ماهية ذلك الشيء ما هو ؟
وضبط المذاهب فيه : أن يقال : إنه إما أن يكون جسماً أو عرضاً سارياً في الجسم أو لا جسماً ولا عرضاً سارياً في الجسم
·
أما القسم الأول : وهو أنه جسم . فذلك الجسم إما أن يكون هو هذا البدن وإما أن يكون جسماً مشابكاً لهذا البدن وإما أن يكون جسماً خارجاً عنه . فأما القسم الثالث وهو أن تكون نفس الإنسان عبارة عن جسم خارج عن هذا البدن فهذا لم يقل به أحد . وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن نفس الإنسان عبارة عن هذا البدن المعين والهيكل المخصوص فهو قول جمهور الخلق وهو المختار عند أكثر المتكلمين . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن الإنسان عبارة عن جسم مخصوص موجود في داخل هذا البدن . فالقائلون
The first chapter on the detailing of the opinions of people concerning it. Know that we know by necessity that there is in us something to which each one of us points by his saying 'I'. And in reality, the most known of known things and the most apparent of apparent things is that to which everyone points by his saying 'I'. However, the rational people differed concerning what the quiddity (mahiyya) of that thing is. The control of the opinions concerning it is that it is said: it is either a body (jism), or an accident (arad) pervading the body, or neither a body nor an accident pervading the body. As for the first division: that it is a body. That body is either this body (badan), or a body interwoven with this body, or a body external to it. As for the third division—that the soul (nafs) of man is a body external to this body—no one has asserted this. As for the first division: that the soul of man is this specific body and particular structure—it is the saying of the generality of creation, and it is the chosen view of most of the theologians (mutakallimun). As for the second division: that man is a specific body existing inside this body—those who say...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the nature of the self (nafs). The author begins by stating a necessary knowledge: each person is aware of something they refer to as 'I', which is the most evident thing. Despite this evident self-awareness, rational thinkers have disputed the quiddity (mahiyya) of that thing. The author then sets forth a tripartite classification: either it is a body (jism), or an accident (arad) that pervades the body, or neither a body nor an accident. The first division (that it is a body) is further subdivided into three: (1) it is this very body (badan), (2) it is a body interwoven with this body, and (3) it is a body external to this body. The author notes that the third subdivision has no adherents. The first subdivision (the self as this body) is attributed to the majority of people and is the preferred view of most mutakallimun. The second subdivision (the self as a distinct body inside this body) is introduced but the text breaks off mid-sentence, indicating that the discussion of its proponents would follow on the subsequent pages. The passage thus lays out the logical taxonomy of positions without yet evaluating them.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
جسم (jism)
بدن (badan)
عرض (arad)
ماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the self is simply an accident or attribute, but the author shows that multiple positions exist, including the self being a body.
Correction: The classification includes the self being a body, an accident, or neither. The author does not privilege any single view at this stage.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which opinion on the nature of the self is held by 'جمهور الخلق' (the generality of people) and 'أكثر المتكلمين' (most theologians)?
A) The self is a body external to this body.
B) The self is an accident pervading the body.
C) The self is this very body (this specific body).
D) The self is a body interwoven with this body.

Key Takeaway
The self is immediately known, but its true nature is a matter of rational inquiry with multiple competing views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I', what exactly are you referring to? Is it your body, your thoughts, or something else? How does this lesson challenge your everyday understanding of yourself?
?
The author claims the self is the most known of known things, yet rational people disagree on its nature. Does this disagreement shake your confidence in your own self-awareness? Why or why not?
?
If you were forced to defend one of the views presented (the self is the body, a body interwoven, or non-physical), which would you choose? What does that choice reveal about how you live your life?

The Four Humors and Blood: Early Views of the Self
Lists the first two opinions on the human self as body, with medical background.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two opinions on the identity of the human self as a body interpenetrating the physical frame, and the medical background that led to their dispute.

Core Idea
The self was debated as the four humors or blood, rooted in medical theory.

The author begins by listing eight opinions on what constitutes the human self as a body intertwined with the physical frame. The first two opinions arise from a prior disagreement among physicians about the origin of the body. This lesson explores those views and their medical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two opinions
Read the Arabic and translation for the first and second opinions. Note the wording 'First: Some said...' and 'Second: Some said...'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'This difference is based on another principle'.
2
Understand the medical background
The author explains that the disagreement stems from physicians who differed on whether the body is generated from four humors or just blood. This is key to the two opinions.
3
Identify the core claim
Each opinion asserts that the human self is a particular substance: the four humors or blood. Ask yourself: why identify the self with a bodily component?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا القول اختلفوا في تعيين نفس ذلك الجسم على وجوه : الأول : قال بعضهم : إنه عبارة عن الأخلاط الأربعة التي يتولد منها هذا البدن . والثاني : قال بعضهم : إنه هو الدم. وهذا الإختلاف مبني على أصل آخر ، وهو أن الأطباء اختلفوا . فقال كثير منهم : إن هذا البدن متولد من مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة. وقال قوم من المحققين : إنه متولد من الدم فقط ، ثم تولد من هذا الأصل : الإختلاف في أن الإنسان . هل هو عبارة عن مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة ، أو عن الدم ؟ والقول الثالث : إنه عبارة عن الدم اللطيف، الذي يتولد من الجانب الأيسر من القلب ، وينفذ في الشريانات إلى سائر الأعضاء . والقول الرابع : إنه هو الروح الذي يصعد من القلب إلى الدماغ ، ويتكيف بالكيفية الصالحة لقبول قوة الحفظ ، والفكر ، والذكر القلب . والقول الخامس : وهو قول ابن الراوندي - إنه جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب . والقول السادس : وهو أنه جسم مخالف بالماهية للجسم ، الذي يتولد منه الأعضاء ، وذلك الجسم جسم نوراني علوي خفيف ، حي لذاته ، متحرك ، ينفذ في جواهر الأعضاء ، ويرى فيها سريان النار في الفحم و سريان ماء الورد في الورد ، وسريان الدهن في السمسم . فما دامت هذه الأعضاء تبقى صالحة لقبول الآثار الفائضة عن هذا الجسم اللطيف الشريف ، وهو قوة الحس والحركة بالإرادة بقي مع ذلك الجسم ، مشابكاً لهذه الأعضاء ، وأفادها هذه الآثار الشريفة ، والقوى النورانية . وإذا فقدت هذه الأعضاء بسبب استيلاء الأخلاط الغليظة عليها ، وخرجت عن قبول تلك الآثار فارق الروح والبدن ، وانفصل إلى عالم الأرواح . ثم قال أصحاب هذا القول : هذا القول أولى من غيره ، لأنه لما لم يكن هذا الجسم من جنس الأجسام التي تولدت الأعضاء منها ، لا جرم صح القول بأنه لا يلزم من وقوع الذوبان والتحلل في البدن ، وقوع الذوبان والتحلل في هذا الجسم الشريف ، بل يكون باقياً بحالة واحدة من أول العمر إلى آخره . وأيضاً : فلما كان جسماً ، صح عليه المجيء والذهاب والرجوع وآيات القرآن ناطقة بهذه الأحوال قال الله تعالى : ثم ردوا إلى الله مولاهم الحق وقال : يا أيتها النفس المطمئنة ، إرجعي إلى ربك . والقول السابع : إن الإنسان عبارة عن أجزاء أصلية باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره ، مصونة عن التبدل والتغير، والفرق بين هذا القول وبين ما قبله : أن في القول الذي قبله كان الجسم الذي هو عبارة عن النفس جسماً مخالفاً بالماهية للجسم الذي تولد عنه البدن ، وفي هذا القول ليس كذلك . والقول الثامن : إن الإنسان عبارة عن الأعضاء الرئيسية التي لا يمكن بقاء الحياة مع فقدانها . فهذا كله تفصيل قول من يقول : إن الإنسان عبارة عن جسم مشابك لهذا البدن .
With this statement they differed in identifying the very body on aspects: First: Some said: It is an expression of the four humors from which this body is generated. Second: Some said: It is blood. And this difference is based on another principle, which is that physicians differed. Many of them said: This body is generated from the totality of the four humors. And a group of investigators said: It is generated from blood only. Then from this principle arose the difference concerning man: is he an expression of the totality of the four humors, or of blood? Third: It is an expression of the subtle blood, which is generated from the left side of the heart and passes through the arteries to the rest of the organs. Fourth: It is the spirit that ascends from the heart to the brain and is conditioned by a disposition suitable for receiving the power of memory, thought, and recollection. Fifth: It is the statement of Ibn al-Rawandi: It is an indivisible part in the heart. Sixth: It is a body different in quiddity from the body from which the organs are generated. That body is a luminous, high, subtle body, living by itself, moving, penetrating the substances of the organs, and is seen in them like the penetration of fire in coal, the penetration of rose-water in the rose, and the penetration of oil in sesame. As long as these organs remain suitable for receiving the effects emanating from this subtle, noble body—which is the power of sensation and voluntary movement—it remains with that body, interpenetrating these organs, and bestows upon them these noble effects and luminous powers. And when these organs become corrupted due to the domination of coarse humors upon them and depart from the capacity to receive those effects, the spirit separates from the body and departs to the world of spirits. Then the proponents of this statement said: This statement is more worthy than others, because since this body is not of the same genus as the bodies from which the organs are generated, it is necessarily correct to say that the occurrence of dissolution and decomposition in the body does not necessitate the occurrence of dissolution and decomposition in this noble body; rather it remains in one state from the beginning of life to its end. Also, since it is a body, coming and going and returning are valid for it, and the verses of the Qur'an speak of these states. Allah Most High said: 'Then they are returned to Allah, their true Lord' and said: 'O tranquil soul, return to your Lord.' Seventh: Man is an expression of original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, preserved from exchange and change; and the difference between this statement and the one before it is that in the previous statement the body which is an expression of the soul is a body different in quiddity from the body from which the physical frame is generated, while in this statement it is not so. Eighth: Man is an expression of the principal organs without which life cannot persist. All this is the detailed account of the statement of those who say that man is a body interpenetrating this physical frame.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of eight opinions on the identity of the human being, specifically the self or soul that is interwoven with the physical body. The author presents these views as part of a broader discussion on the nature of man. The first opinion identifies the human with the four humors. The second opinion identifies it with blood, noting that this difference stems from a prior disagreement among physicians about whether the body is generated from the four humors or from blood alone. The third opinion says it is subtle blood from the left side of the heart. The fourth says it is the spirit that goes from heart to brain and acquires dispositions for memory, thought, and recollection. The fifth, attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, says it is an indivisible part in the heart. The sixth opinion describes it as a luminous, subtle, self-living body that interpenetrates the organs like fire in coal, and argues for its permanence despite bodily change, citing Qur'anic verses about return. The seventh opinion says it is original parts that remain unchanged, differing from the sixth in that these parts are not different in quiddity from the body. The eighth says it is the principal organs essential for life. The section concludes by summarizing that these are the detailed views of those who say man is a body interpenetrating the physical frame.

Key Terms
نفس
جسم
أخلاط أربعة
دم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, what is the human?
A) Four humors
B) Blood
C) Spirit
D) Indivisible part

Key Takeaway
The first two opinions identify the self with either the four humors or blood, reflecting medical theories of the body's origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your essential self, do you subconsciously reduce it to your biology—your genes, your body, your health? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of defining your identity solely by physical makeup, and how does that impact your relationship with your Creator?
?

?

Subtle Blood, Spirit, and Indivisible Part: Three More Views
Lists the third, fourth, and fifth opinions on the human self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third, fourth, and fifth opinions on the nature of the human self, including the role of subtle blood, the spirit's ascent to the brain, and Ibn al-Rawandi's atomic view.

Core Idea
The self was also identified as subtle blood, a spirit in the brain, or an indivisible part in the heart.

After the initial two opinions based on humors, the author presents three further views. These opinions shift focus to the heart and brain, and introduce the concept of an indivisible atom. This lesson examines these three distinct proposals.

Guided Path
1
Read the three opinions
Read the Arabic and translation for opinions three, four, and five. Notice the progression from subtle blood to spirit to indivisible part.
Opinion five is attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi.
2
Understand each opinion's specifics
Opinion three: subtle blood from left side of heart. Opinion four: spirit ascending to brain. Opinion five: indivisible part in heart.
Opinions three and four are closely related to anatomy.
3
Reflect on the diversity
These three opinions show how different thinkers located the self in different physical or quasi-physical substances.

The Luminous Body, Original Parts, and Principal Organs
Lists the sixth, seventh, and eighth opinions on the human self, with detailed reasoning for the sixth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth, seventh, and eighth opinions on the human self, including the concept of a luminous body that interpenetrates the organs, original unchanging parts, and the principal organs essential for life.

Core Idea
The self is a luminous body, original parts, or principal organs — each offering a different permanence.

The final three opinions in this enumeration present more sophisticated views. The sixth describes a luminous, self-living body that interpenetrates the organs like fire in coal, and argues for its permanence. The seventh posits original unchanging parts. The eighth identifies the self with the principal organs necessary for life. This lesson explores these views.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth opinion carefully
This is the longest opinion. Read the Arabic and translation, noting the analogy of fire in coal and the argument from permanence.
The proponents claim this view is preferable.
2
Understand the seventh and eighth opinions
The seventh opinion says the self is original parts that remain unchanged, differing from the sixth in that these parts are not different in quiddity from the body. The eighth says it is the principal organs.
3
Compare the three
Consider how each opinion addresses the issue of permanence and change. The sixth uses a different substance, the seventh uses unchanging parts, the eighth uses essential organs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا القول اختلفوا في تعيين نفس ذلك الجسم على وجوه : الأول : قال بعضهم : إنه عبارة عن الأخلاط الأربعة التي يتولد منها هذا البدن . والثاني : قال بعضهم : إنه هو الدم. وهذا الإختلاف مبني على أصل آخر ، وهو أن الأطباء اختلفوا . فقال كثير منهم : إن هذا البدن متولد من مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة. وقال قوم من المحققين : إنه متولد من الدم فقط ، ثم تولد من هذا الأصل : الإختلاف في أن الإنسان . هل هو عبارة عن مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة ، أو عن الدم ؟ والقول الثالث : إنه عبارة عن الدم اللطيف، الذي يتولد من الجانب الأيسر من القلب ، وينفذ في الشريانات إلى سائر الأعضاء . والقول الرابع : إنه هو الروح الذي يصعد من القلب إلى الدماغ ، ويتكيف بالكيفية الصالحة لقبول قوة الحفظ ، والفكر ، والذكر القلب . والقول الخامس : وهو قول ابن الراوندي - إنه جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب . والقول السادس : وهو أنه جسم مخالف بالماهية للجسم ، الذي يتولد منه الأعضاء ، وذلك الجسم جسم نوراني علوي خفيف ، حي لذاته ، متحرك ، ينفذ في جواهر الأعضاء ، ويرى فيها سريان النار في الفحم و سريان ماء الورد في الورد ، وسريان الدهن في السمسم . فما دامت هذه الأعضاء تبقى صالحة لقبول الآثار الفائضة عن هذا الجسم اللطيف الشريف ، وهو قوة الحس والحركة بالإرادة بقي مع ذلك الجسم ، مشابكاً لهذه الأعضاء ، وأفادها هذه الآثار الشريفة ، والقوى النورانية . وإذا فقدت هذه الأعضاء بسبب استيلاء الأخلاط الغليظة عليها ، وخرجت عن قبول تلك الآثار فارق الروح والبدن ، وانفصل إلى عالم الأرواح . ثم قال أصحاب هذا القول : هذا القول أولى من غيره ، لأنه لما لم يكن هذا الجسم من جنس الأجسام التي تولدت الأعضاء منها ، لا جرم صح القول بأنه لا يلزم من وقوع الذوبان والتحلل في البدن ، وقوع الذوبان والتحلل في هذا الجسم الشريف ، بل يكون باقياً بحالة واحدة من أول العمر إلى آخره . وأيضاً : فلما كان جسماً ، صح عليه المجيء والذهاب والرجوع وآيات القرآن ناطقة بهذه الأحوال قال الله تعالى : ثم ردوا إلى الله مولاهم الحق وقال : يا أيتها النفس المطمئنة ، إرجعي إلى ربك . والقول السابع : إن الإنسان عبارة عن أجزاء أصلية باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره ، مصونة عن التبدل والتغير، والفرق بين هذا القول وبين ما قبله : أن في القول الذي قبله كان الجسم الذي هو عبارة عن النفس جسماً مخالفاً بالماهية للجسم الذي تولد عنه البدن ، وفي هذا القول ليس كذلك . والقول الثامن : إن الإنسان عبارة عن الأعضاء الرئيسية التي لا يمكن بقاء الحياة مع فقدانها . فهذا كله تفصيل قول من يقول : إن الإنسان عبارة عن جسم مشابك لهذا البدن .
With this statement they differed in identifying the very body on aspects: First: Some said: It is an expression of the four humors from which this body is generated. Second: Some said: It is blood. And this difference is based on another principle, which is that physicians differed. Many of them said: This body is generated from the totality of the four humors. And a group of investigators said: It is generated from blood only. Then from this principle arose the difference concerning man: is he an expression of the totality of the four humors, or of blood? Third: It is an expression of the subtle blood, which is generated from the left side of the heart and passes through the arteries to the rest of the organs. Fourth: It is the spirit that ascends from the heart to the brain and is conditioned by a disposition suitable for receiving the power of memory, thought, and recollection. Fifth: It is the statement of Ibn al-Rawandi: It is an indivisible part in the heart. Sixth: It is a body different in quiddity from the body from which the organs are generated. That body is a luminous, high, subtle body, living by itself, moving, penetrating the substances of the organs, and is seen in them like the penetration of fire in coal, the penetration of rose-water in the rose, and the penetration of oil in sesame. As long as these organs remain suitable for receiving the effects emanating from this subtle, noble body—which is the power of sensation and voluntary movement—it remains with that body, interpenetrating these organs, and bestows upon them these noble effects and luminous powers. And when these organs become corrupted due to the domination of coarse humors upon them and depart from the capacity to receive those effects, the spirit separates from the body and departs to the world of spirits. Then the proponents of this statement said: This statement is more worthy than others, because since this body is not of the same genus as the bodies from which the organs are generated, it is necessarily correct to say that the occurrence of dissolution and decomposition in the body does not necessitate the occurrence of dissolution and decomposition in this noble body; rather it remains in one state from the beginning of life to its end. Also, since it is a body, coming and going and returning are valid for it, and the verses of the Qur'an speak of these states. Allah Most High said: 'Then they are returned to Allah, their true Lord' and said: 'O tranquil soul, return to your Lord.' Seventh: Man is an expression of original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, preserved from exchange and change; and the difference between this statement and the one before it is that in the previous statement the body which is an expression of the soul is a body different in quiddity from the body from which the physical frame is generated, while in this statement it is not so. Eighth: Man is an expression of the principal organs without which life cannot persist. All this is the detailed account of the statement of those who say that man is a body interpenetrating this physical frame.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of eight opinions on the identity of the human being, specifically the self or soul that is interwoven with the physical body. The author presents these views as part of a broader discussion on the nature of man. The first opinion identifies the human with the four humors. The second opinion identifies it with blood, noting that this difference stems from a prior disagreement among physicians about whether the body is generated from the four humors or from blood alone. The third opinion says it is subtle blood from the left side of the heart. The fourth says it is the spirit that goes from heart to brain and acquires dispositions for memory, thought, and recollection. The fifth, attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, says it is an indivisible part in the heart. The sixth opinion describes it as a luminous, subtle, self-living body that interpenetrates the organs like fire in coal, and argues for its permanence despite bodily change, citing Qur'anic verses about return. The seventh opinion says it is original parts that remain unchanged, differing from the sixth in that these parts are not different in quiddity from the body. The eighth says it is the principal organs essential for life. The section concludes by summarizing that these are the detailed views of those who say man is a body interpenetrating the physical frame.

جسم نوراني
أجزاء أصلية
أعضاء رئيسية
نار
فحم

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which of your daily habits or attachments reveals that you treat your soul as something that dissolves with the body, rather than as a permanent luminous reality? Can you name one attachment today that you need to loosen?
?
The passage says the soul separates when the organs become corrupted by coarse humors. What 'coarse humors' in your spiritual life—like pride, envy, or heedlessness—are making your heart unable to receive the effects of the soul's light? What is the first thing you will do this week to clear them?
?
The seventh opinion says the self consists of original parts that remain unchanged. When you look at your own character, which part of you do you think is that unchanging essence? Are you living in a way that honors that permanent self, or are you distracted by what passes away?

Why the Human Cannot Be an Accident
Refutes the idea that man is an accident using necessary self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians reject the view that a human being is merely an accident inhering in the body, relying on immediate self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Every person knows directly that he is a self-subsistent substance, not a mere accident.

Some philosophers have argued that the human being is nothing more than an accident that happens to reside in the body. But is this plausible? The author dismisses this view outright, grounding his argument in an appeal to direct, undeniable human experience. As you read the passage, notice how he uses the concept of 'necessary knowledge' to refute the accident theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
The Arabic is one concise sentence—notice its logical structure.
2
Identify the claim and the reason
Find the view that the author rejects and the single reason he gives for its absurdity. What does 'necessary knowledge' mean here?
3
Reflect on self-knowledge as proof
Ask yourself: why does the author think self-awareness is enough to disprove the accident theory? How does this argument rely on introspection?
This connects to the learning objective about immediate knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من الأقسام المذكورة في أول هذا التقسيم . وهو أن يقال : إن الإنسان عبارة عن عرض حال في الجسم ، فهذا لا يقول به عاقل ، لأن كل إنسان يجد من نفسه علما ضروريا ، بأنه جوهر قائم بنفسه .
As for the second division of the divisions mentioned at the beginning of this classification—namely, that it is said that man is an accident subsisting in the body—no intelligent person would say this, because every human finds in himself necessary knowledge that he is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qā'im bi-nafsihi).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opinion. After detailing views that consider man a body interpenetrating the physical frame, the author turns to the second main division: the claim that man is an accident inhering in the body. He dismisses this view as absurd, stating that no intelligent person would hold it. The reason given is that every human being possesses direct, necessary self-knowledge that he is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qā'im bi-nafsihi) — i.e., an entity that exists independently, not requiring a subject in which to inhere. This argument appeals to immediate introspection: one's own awareness of being a substantial entity, not a mere quality or accident of something else.

Key Terms

جوهر
قائم بنفسه
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the human being is an accident inhering in the body, like a quality or property of a physical object.
Correction: Every human has direct, necessary knowledge that he is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qā'im bi-nafsihi), not a mere accident requiring a subject to exist.

Checkpoint

C) Because the human body is not a substance.
D) Because accidents are only in the body.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the human as an accident rests on the immediate, undeniable self-awareness every person has of being an independent substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If someone tried to convince you that you are just a temporary quality of your body, would you reject it immediately? That immediate rejection is a sign of necessary knowledge—but do you live as if you are merely a body, or as a responsible, independent being? Where have you been hiding from that responsibility today?
?
The author says 'no intelligent person' would deny that he is a self-subsistent substance. But do your daily actions reflect that you are a substance accountable before Allah? Or do you sometimes act as if you are just a victim of circumstances? What one change can you make right now to live in the light of this necessary knowledge?
