First Premise: Intuitive Knowledge of the Soul's Attributes
The author introduces the first proof and details the first premise with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the proof that the soul is a body: that our knowledge of our own soul's attributes is intuitive, and why this is undeniable.

Core Idea
We know our own mental states directly – that is the starting point of the proof.

The author begins the first proof by establishing a foundation: we have immediate, intuitive knowledge of our own attributes like seeing, hearing, and thinking. Without this premise, the rest of the argument would collapse. In this lesson, we examine this first premise and the evidence supporting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and translation carefully. Notice that the author states the proof is built on three premises and then gives the first premise with its supporting examples.
Pay attention to the examples of intuitive knowledge: seeing, hearing, speaking, thinking, desiring, anger, etc.
2
Identify the first premise
Find where the author explicitly states the first premise: 'Our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive knowledge.' Underline it.
3
Understand the evidence
The author argues that we know these attributes necessarily and immediately, and anyone who disputes this is disputing the clearest knowledge. Reflect on why this is considered undeniable.
This evidence is meant to be self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فنقول : الذي يدل على أن النفس جسم مخصوص وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن العلم بصفات النفس : بديهي ، ومتى كان كذلك (١)
كان العلم بالذات بديهياً، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون علمنا بذاتنا المخصوصة علماً بديهياً ، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن تكون ذاتنا عبارة عن هذا البدن . فهذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمات [ ثلاث (۱) ] :
المقدمة الأولى : إن علمنا بصفات نفوسنا : علم بديهي . والذي يدل عليه : أني أعلم بالضرورة : أني (۲) أبصرت وسمعت وقلت وعرفت ، وتفكرت ، واشتهيت ، وغضبت [ ودخلت الدار وخرجت وسافرت إلى البلدة الفلانية ورجعت منها (۳) ] ومن نازع في كون هذه العلوم ، علوماً بديهية ، فقد نازع في أجلى العلوم البديهية وأقواها . فثبت : أن علمنا بصفات أنفسنا : علم بديهي
والمقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : إن العلم بصفة النفس إذا كان بديهياً ، كان العلم بذات النفس ، يجب أن يكون بديهيا . فالدليل عليه (٤) : أن علمي بأني أبصرت وسمعت وعقلت ونظرت : حكم على نفسي بثبوت هذه الصفات لها . والحاكم بشيء على شيء ، لا بد وأن يكون قد سبق له تصور الطرفين أولا ، فلو كان علمي بوجود نفسي علماً كسبياً ، فقبل ذلك الكسب : أكون شاكاً في وجود نفسي . ومن كان شاكاً في وجود النفس يمتنع أن يكون عالماً بحصول الصفات المخصوصة بها. ولما دللنا على أن هذا العلم علم بديهي وجب أن يكون العلم بوجود النفس بديهياً . وهذا هو المسألة المشهورة عند العقلاء بأنه متى كان العلم بصفة الشيء بديهياً ، وجب أن يكون العلم بأصل الذات بديهياً .
والمقدمة الثالثة : إنه لما ثبت : أن علم كل أحد بنفسه المخصوصة ، علم بديهي . وجب أن تكون النفس هو هذا البدن ، أو شيء داخل فيه . والدليل
When you have understood this preliminary, we say: What indicates that the soul is a particular body are [several] aspects:
The first proof: Knowledge of the attributes of the soul is intuitive, and whenever it is so (1), knowledge of the essence is intuitive, and this necessitates that our knowledge of our particular essence be intuitive knowledge. And when this is established, it is necessary that our essence be this body. So this proof is built upon three premises (1):
The first premise: Our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive knowledge. What indicates it is that I know necessarily that I (2) saw, heard, spoke, knew, thought, desired, became angry, [entered the house, left, traveled to such-and-such town, and returned from it (3)]. And whoever disputes that these knowledges are intuitive has disputed the clearest and strongest of intuitive knowledges. So it is established that our knowledge of the attributes of our selves is intuitive.
The second premise: Our statement: If knowledge of an attribute of the soul is intuitive, then knowledge of the soul's essence must be intuitive. The proof for it (4) is that my knowledge that I saw, heard, understood, and reflected is a judgment upon my self of the establishment of these attributes for it. The one who judges something about something must have previously conceived both parties first. So if my knowledge of the existence of my self was acquired, then before that acquisition I would be doubtful about the existence of my self. And one who is doubtful about the existence of the soul cannot be knowing of the establishment of its particular attributes. Since we have proven that this knowledge is intuitive, it is necessary that knowledge of the existence of the soul be intuitive. This is the well-known issue among rational people that whenever knowledge of an attribute of a thing is intuitive, knowledge of the essence itself must be intuitive.
The third premise: Since it is established that every person's knowledge of their particular self is intuitive, it is necessary that the soul be this body, or something inside it. And the proof [continues].
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (ḥujjah) for the claim that the soul (nafs) is a physical body. The proof is built on the idea that we have intuitive knowledge of our own attributes (like seeing, hearing, thinking), and from that we necessarily have intuitive knowledge of our own essence. Since the essence is known intuitively, it must be identical to the body we perceive. The author breaks down the argument into three premises. The first premise establishes that our knowledge of our soul's attributes is intuitive, using undeniable examples from everyday experience—anyone who denies this is disputing the clearest of intuitive knowledge. The second premise argues that intuitive knowledge of an attribute implies intuitive knowledge of the essence, because making a judgment about a thing requires prior conception of both the subject and the predicate; if knowledge of the self were acquired, one would be doubtful about the self before acquisition, making it impossible to know the attributes. The third premise is stated but its proof is cut off at the end of the page; it concludes that since everyone's knowledge of their own self is intuitive, the soul must be this body or something within it.

Key Terms
نفس
بديهي
صفات
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that knowledge of our own mental states is acquired through inference or reflection.
Correction: The author argues that we know our mental states directly and necessarily; they are the clearest examples of intuitive knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the proof that the soul is a body?
A) Knowledge of the soul's essence is intuitive.
B) Knowledge of the soul's attributes is intuitive.
C) The soul is this body.
D) All knowledge is acquired.

Key Takeaway
The first premise is that we have immediate, intuitive knowledge of our soul's attributes, such as seeing and thinking, and this is undeniable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author calls knowledge of your soul's attributes the clearest intuitive knowledge. Is your soul's state clear to you right now? If not, what are you avoiding seeing?
?
If you truly and immediately know your own desires and anger, then why do you often act against what you know? What would change if you took that knowledge seriously in your next decision?

Second and Third Premises: From Attributes to Essence
The author presents the second premise with its proof and introduces the third premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise — that intuitive knowledge of attributes implies intuitive knowledge of the essence — and the third premise that identifies the soul with the body.

Core Idea
If you know the attributes directly, you must know the essence directly — and that essence is the body.

Having established that we have intuitive knowledge of our soul's attributes, the author now argues that this necessarily implies intuitive knowledge of the soul itself. This leads to the conclusion that the soul is the body. In this lesson, we examine the second premise and its proof, and the statement of the third premise, though its full proof is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
Read the Arabic and translation for the second premise. The author states that if knowledge of an attribute is intuitive, then knowledge of the essence must be intuitive.
Notice the logical structure: attribute → essence.
2
Analyze the proof of the second premise
The proof is based on the judgment relation: to judge that something has a property, you must have already conceived the subject. If knowledge of the self were acquired, you'd be doubtful before acquisition, contradicting your direct knowledge of attributes.
This argument shows the necessary connection between attribute-knowledge and essence-knowledge.
3
Examine the third premise
The third premise concludes that since everyone's knowledge of their self is intuitive, the soul must be this body or something inside it. Notice that the proof is cut off here.
The continuation is in the next lesson.

والمقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : إن العلم بصفة النفس إذا كان بديهياً ، كان العلم بذات النفس ، يجب أن يكون بديهيا . فالدليل عليه (٤) : أن علمي بأني أبصرت وسمعت وعقلت ونظرت : حكم على نفسي بثبوت هذه الصفات لها . والحاكم بشيء على شيء ، لا بد وأن يكون قد سبق له تصور الطرفين أولا ، فلو كان علمي بوجود نفسي علماً كسبياً ، فقبل ذلك الكسب : أكون شاكاً في وجود نفسي . ومن كان شاكاً في وجود النفس يمتنع أن يكون عالماً بحصول الصفات المخصوصة بها. ولما دللنا على أن هذا العلم علم بديهي وجب أن يكون العلم بوجود النفس بديهياً . وهذا هو المسألة المشهورة عند العقلاء بأنه متى كان العلم بصفة الشيء بديهياً ، وجب أن يكون العلم بأصل الذات بديهياً .
والمقدمة الثالثة : إنه لما ثبت : أن علم كل أحد بنفسه المخصوصة ، علم بديهي . وجب أن تكون النفس هو هذا البدن ، أو شيء داخل فيه . والدليل
When you have understood this preliminary, we say: What indicates that the soul is a particular body are [several] aspects:
The first proof: Knowledge of the attributes of the soul is intuitive, and whenever it is so (1), knowledge of the essence is intuitive, and this necessitates that our knowledge of our particular essence be intuitive knowledge. And when this is established, it is necessary that our essence be this body. So this proof is built upon three premises (1):
The first premise: Our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive knowledge. What indicates it is that I know necessarily that I (2) saw, heard, spoke, knew, thought, desired, became angry, [entered the house, left, traveled to such-and-such town, and returned from it (3)]. And whoever disputes that these knowledges are intuitive has disputed the clearest and strongest of intuitive knowledges. So it is established that our knowledge of the attributes of our selves is intuitive.
The second premise: Our statement: If knowledge of an attribute of the soul is intuitive, then knowledge of the soul's essence must be intuitive. The proof for it (4) is that my knowledge that I saw, heard, understood, and reflected is a judgment upon my self of the establishment of these attributes for it. The one who judges something about something must have previously conceived both parties first. So if my knowledge of the existence of my self was acquired, then before that acquisition I would be doubtful about the existence of my self. And one who is doubtful about the existence of the soul cannot be knowing of the establishment of its particular attributes. Since we have proven that this knowledge is intuitive, it is necessary that knowledge of the existence of the soul be intuitive. This is the well-known issue among rational people that whenever knowledge of an attribute of a thing is intuitive, knowledge of the essence itself must be intuitive.
The third premise: Since it is established that every person's knowledge of their particular self is intuitive, it is necessary that the soul be this body, or something inside it. And the proof [continues].
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (ḥujjah) for the claim that the soul (nafs) is a physical body. The proof is built on the idea that we have intuitive knowledge of our own attributes (like seeing, hearing, thinking), and from that we necessarily have intuitive knowledge of our own essence. Since the essence is known intuitively, it must be identical to the body we perceive. The author breaks down the argument into three premises. The first premise establishes that our knowledge of our soul's attributes is intuitive, using undeniable examples from everyday experience—anyone who denies this is disputing the clearest of intuitive knowledge. The second premise argues that intuitive knowledge of an attribute implies intuitive knowledge of the essence, because making a judgment about a thing requires prior conception of both the subject and the predicate; if knowledge of the self were acquired, one would be doubtful about the self before acquisition, making it impossible to know the attributes. The third premise is stated but its proof is cut off at the end of the page; it concludes that since everyone's knowledge of their own self is intuitive, the soul must be this body or something within it.

Key Terms
ذات
بديهي
جسم مخصوص
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intuitive knowledge of attributes does not necessarily imply intuitive knowledge of the essence.
Correction: The author provides a specific argument: to judge attributes, you must have a prior conception of the subject; hence, if attribute knowledge is intuitive, essence knowledge must also be intuitive.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, why must knowledge of the soul's essence be intuitive?
A) Because the attributes are intuitive.
B) Because the soul is a body.
C) Because all knowledge is intuitive.
D) Because the author says so without proof.

Key Takeaway
The second premise shows that intuitive knowledge of attributes forces intuitive knowledge of the essence; the third premise identifies that essence with the body, though its proof is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know your own actions and thoughts directly, yet do you live as if your very self is an open book to Allah? If your knowledge of your self is intuitive, why do you still hide from the truth about yourself?
?
The proof says that to know your attributes, you must already have a conception of your self. When you sin, you know you sinned. What does that immediate recognition say about the reality of your soul's accountability? Are you truly aware that you cannot plead ignorance of your own existence before Allah?
?
The author concludes that the soul is the body or inside it. Does this challenge your own assumptions about what you truly are? If your identity is tied to this physical form, how does that change your responsibility for every limb and organ you possess?

The Intuitive Distinction Against the Soul as a Separate Substance
A definitive proof using the intuitive distinction between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered' to refute the soul as a separate substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitive proof that refutes the idea of the soul as a separate substance by appealing to an intuitive distinction between the self and its body.

Core Idea
The self is not a separate substance because 'I entered' differs from 'my horse entered.'

This lesson presents a concise but powerful proof from the author against the view that the human soul is a separate, independent substance. The argument relies on a simple intuition that every rational person possesses: the difference between saying 'I entered the house' and 'my horse entered the house.' By analyzing this distinction, we can see that identifying the self with a separate soul-substance would collapse a fundamental intuitive truth.

Guided Path
1
Encounter the definitive proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it begins by stating what would follow if the soul were a separate substance, then presents an intuitive distinction, and concludes that the soul-as-substance view is false.
The passage uses a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Analyze the rider-horse analogy
The passage says: if the human being is the substance of the soul, then the body is like a mount (horse) for the soul. Understand that 'I' would then refer to the soul-substance, and 'my body' would be an instrument.
This analogy is crucial for the argument.
3
Identify the intuitive distinction
The core of the proof is that every rational person perceives a clear difference between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered.' This distinction is known by intuition (badihah).
We intuitively know that 'I' is not the same as 'my horse'.
4
Recognize the contradiction
If the soul were a separate substance, then 'I entered' would actually mean 'my soul-substance entered,' which is analogous to 'my horse entered.' But that would eliminate the intuitive distinction we just identified. Therefore, the premise leads to an absurdity.
The argument shows that the soul-as-separate-substance thesis contradicts a basic intuition.
5
Accept the conclusion
Since the statement that the soul is a separate substance would destroy a known intuitive distinction, that statement must be false. The proof is definitive (qati').
The author leaves no room for doubt: it is a demonstrative proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل القاطع عليه : أنه إذا كان الإنسان عبارة عن جوهر النفس ، كان البدن مركباً له ، ومحلاً لتصرفه . فكان دخول بدنه في الدار وخروجه منها ، جارياً مجرى دخول فرسه في الدار ، وخروجه منها . لكن كل عاقل يدرك تفرقة بديهية بين قوله : دخلت في الدار وخرجت منها ، وبين قوله : دخل فرسي الدار ، وخرج منها. ولما كان القول بأن النفس جوهر مجرد ، يوجب زوال هذه التفرقة المعلومة بالبديهة ، وجب أن يكون القول بإثبات النفس : باطلاً
The definitive proof for it: that if the human being is the substance of the soul, then the body is a mount for it and a locus for its actions. So the entry of his body into the house and its exit from it would be like the entry of his horse into the house and its exit from it. But every rational person perceives an intuitive distinction between saying 'I entered the house and exited from it' and saying 'my horse entered the house and exited from it.' And since the statement that the soul is a separate substance would cause the elimination of this distinction known by intuition, it must be that the statement affirming the soul is false.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitive proof (دليل قاطع) that the human self is not a separate substance (جوهر مجرد). The proof proceeds by analogy: if the human being were essentially the soul-substance, the body would be merely a vehicle or instrument for it, like a horse for its rider. Then the expression 'I entered the house' would be equivalent to 'my horse entered the house'. But every rational person intuitively distinguishes these two statements: when I say 'I entered', I refer to myself, not my horse. The statement that the soul is a separate substance would eliminate this intuitive distinction. Therefore, that statement is false.

Key Terms
نفس
جوهر مجرد
بديهة
تفرقة بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies the existence of the soul altogether.
Correction: The proof only denies that the soul is a separate substance independent of the body, not the existence of the soul itself.

Checkpoint
What is the key intuitive distinction used in the definitive proof against the soul as a separate substance?
A) The distinction between 'I entered' and 'the house entered.'
B) The distinction between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered.'
C) The distinction between entering and exiting.
D) The distinction between body and soul.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive distinction between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered' provides a decisive refutation of the claim that the soul is a separate substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I entered the room' or 'I left the house,' do you ever pause to wonder what that 'I' refers to? How does this lesson challenge you to think about your own sense of self beyond habit?
?
Consider a moment when you felt your body and your self were in conflict—like when you were sick or exhausted. Does the proof's analogy make you rethink whether that feeling is an illusion? What would it mean to live as if your body is not just a vehicle?
?
If the soul is not a separate substance in the way you might have assumed, how does that change your awareness of where your true identity lies? Are you ready to accept that your 'I' is more integrated with your body than you previously believed?

Third Argument: Personal Identity Refutes a Separate Soul
The third argument: if the soul can transfer bodies, personal identity becomes uncertain; but we are certain of identity, so the soul is not separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument against the view that the human soul is a separate substance, and how it defends against an objection about identical bodies.

Core Idea
If the soul were separate, we could never be certain of personal identity—but we are.

The third argument tackles the problem of personal identity over time. If the soul could hop between bodies, then even when we see the same body, we wouldn't know if it's the same person. But we do know, so the soul cannot be a separate substance. The author then addresses a clever objection about identical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument, objection, and response.
Pay attention to the house ownership analogy.
2
Analyze the core analogy
Identify how the analogy of a house with changing owners parallels the soul-body relationship. Understand why this leads to doubt about identity.
3
Note the conclusion
The arguer concludes that because we have no doubt about personal identity when seeing the same body, the premise of a separate soul must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the objector's claim that identical bodies could arise naturally or by divine action, and understand the author's counter: our certainty about the body's identity precludes that doubt.
The response focuses on cases where we are certain the body is the same.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether the argument successfully refutes the separate soul. Does the response fully address the objection?
Think about cases where the body is not clearly identical, such as growth or decay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت النفس عبارة عن جوهر مجرد ، له تعلق بهذا البدن بالتصرف والتدبير ، لم يبعد في أول العقل أن ينقطع تعلق هذه النفس بهذا البدن ، وتصير متعلقة بالبدن الثاني ، وبالعكس. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإذا رأينا بدن زيد ، ثم رأيناه بعد ذلك وجب أن نبقى شاكين في أن هذا الإنسان هل هو الإنسان الأول ؟ لأن بتقدير أن يتبدل المدبر الأول لهذا البدن مدبر آخر ، فقد تبدل ذلك الإنسان بغيره . كما أنا لما رأينا بيتاً وجوزنا أن ملك ذلك البيت قد انتقل إلى مالك آخر ، فحينئذ لا نقطع عند رؤية ذلك البيت في المرة الثانية ، بأن مالكه هو المالك الأول . بل نبقى شاكين فيه . فكذا ههنا وجب أن يبقى الشك في أن هذا الإنسان الذي نراه الآن ، هل هو الإنسان الذي رأيناه أولاً ؟ ولما لم يحصل هذا الشك لأحد من العقلاء ، وثبت : أن على تقدير أن يكون الإنسان شيئاً آخر غير هذا البدن، وجب حصول هذا الشك ، علمنا : أن القول بأن الإنسان شيء غير هذا البدن ، قول باطل. فإن قالوا : التجويز الذي ذكرتم قائم على كل التقديرات ، لاحتمال أن الشخص الأول فني ، وحدث شخص آخر يساوي الأول في الصورة والحلقة . إما بإيجاد الفاعل المختار ، وإما لأجل حدوث شكل غريب في الفلك ، اقتضى حدوث هذا المعنى. فنقول : موضع الإلزام : إنا مع القطع بأن هذا الجسد هو الجسد الذي رأيناه أولاً لم يبق الشك في أن هذا الإنسان هو الإنسان الأول. و معلوم : أن السؤال المذكور ، لا يتوجه على هذا الكلام
The third proof: if the soul were a separate substance having a connection to this body by way of direction and management, it would not be far from the intellect for this soul's connection to this body to be severed and for it to become connected to a second body, and vice versa. On this assumption, if we see Zayd's body then see it afterwards, we would necessarily remain in doubt as to whether this human is the first human? Because if the first manager of this body were replaced by another manager, then that human would have been replaced by another. Just as when we see a house and we allow that the ownership of that house may have transferred to another owner, then at the second sight we are not certain that its owner is the first owner, but rather we remain in doubt. So here similarly, doubt would necessarily remain as to whether this human we see now is the human we saw first. And since this doubt does not occur to any rational person, and it is established that on the assumption that the human is something other than this body, this doubt would necessarily occur, we know that the statement that the human is something other than this body is false. Then if they say: the possibility you mentioned applies on all assumptions, because it is possible that the first person perished and another person similar in form and shape occurred, either by the action of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) or due to a strange form in the celestial sphere necessitating this occurrence. We say: the point of the objection is that when we are certain that this body is the body we saw first, no doubt remains that this human is the first human. And it is known that the aforementioned objection does not apply to this speech.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the soul as a separate substance and then addresses an objection. The argument: if the soul were a separate substance that could transfer from one body to another, then when we see a body we could never be certain it is the same person as before. Just as if the owner of a house changes, we are uncertain about ownership, similarly we would be uncertain about personal identity. But we are not uncertain: we know for certain that the person we see now is the same as before. Thus the premise that the soul is a separate substance is false. The objection: the possibility of the person dying and a new person appearing identical in form would create uncertainty anyway, regardless of the soul theory. The answer: the point of the argument is that we are certain that this body is the same body we saw before, and that certainty cannot be explained by the possibility of substitution. The objection does not apply because we are speaking of the case where we are certain of the body's identity.

Key Terms

جوهر مجرد
بدن
مدبر
شك
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that the body never changes.
Correction: The argument only deals with cases where we are certain the body is the same; it does not deny physical change over time.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence of the soul being a separate substance that can transfer bodies, according to the third argument?
A) The soul would become immortal.
B) We would remain uncertain about personal identity even when seeing the same body.
C) The body would be immaterial.
D) The soul would have no connection to the body.

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that our certainty about personal identity when seeing the same body is incompatible with the soul being a separate substance capable of transferring.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The argument says no rational person doubts identity when seeing the same body. But do you live as though your identity is anchored in your body, or in something else? Where do you place your trust for who you truly are?

The Fourth Proof: The Soul is the Body – Two Premises and Conclusion
Al-Razi presents a two-premise proof for the identity of soul and body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's fourth proof that the soul is the body, which uses empirical localization of mental faculties to argue for a material soul.

Core Idea
Mental faculties are localized in organs, so the soul must be the body.

Al-Razi's fourth proof is a direct argument from experience. He argues that since all mental activities — sensation, thought, desire — are experienced as occurring in specific body parts, the subject of these activities must be the body itself. This lesson walks through the two premises and the conclusion that follows from them.

Guided Path
1
Read the full proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note that it has two clear premises (amrain).
Pay attention to the linguistic argument (the 'ta' pronoun) in the first premise.
2
Examine the first premise
The first premise establishes that the soul is the substance described by attributes like seeing, hearing, etc. Understand the evidence from language and intuition.
3
Examine the second premise
The second premise shows that the one described by these attributes is the bodily structure. Evidence comes from necessary knowledge about sensory localization.
Note the examples: taste in tongue, hearing in ear, thought in brain, perception in heart.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion combines the two premises: the substance of the soul is either the whole body or subtle bodies flowing in it. Reflect on the materialistic implication.
The proof does not fully commit to gross body; it allows for 'bodies flowing in it' (ajsam sariya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن نفسي جوهر ، موصوف بأنه علم وتفكر وتذكر وأبصر وسمع وذاق وشم واشتهى وغضب وتحرك وسكن . ثم إن الموصوف بهذه الصفات ليس إلا هذه البنية أو ما يكون داخلا فيها فوجب أن تكون نفسي عبارة عن هذا البدن أو ما يكون داخلا فيه . ولا بد في تقرير هذه الحجة من تقرير أمرين : الأول : إن نفسي عبارة عن الجوهر الموصوف بهذه الصفات . والدليل عليه : أني لا أشك أني إذا رأيت المبصرات، فأنا أبصرتها . وإذا سمعت الحروف والأصوات ، فأنا سمعتها . لأن التاء في قولي أبصرت وسمعت وفهمت وعرفت : ضمير النفس . ولا فرق بين قولك : أبصرت وسمعت ، وبين قولك : أنا موصوف بإبصار كذا ، وسماع كذا . ولما كانت بديهة العقل حاكمة بأني أبصرت وسمعت ، كانت تلك البديهة حاكمة بأني أنا الموصوف بهذه الصفات . وأما الثاني : فهو في بيان أن الموصوف بهذه الصفات هو هذه البنية . فالدليل عليه : أني كما أعلم بالضرورة أن ذوق الطعام غير حاصل في أخص الرجل ، وفي الساق اعلم بالضرورة : أن ذوق الطعام حاصل في اللسان وأن سماع الصوت حاصل بالأذن ، وكذا القول في سائر الإحساسات . وأيضا : الإنسان يعلم بالبديهة أنه يجد الفكر والتخيل من جانب الدماغ ، ويجد العلم والإدراك من ناحية القلب . وجميع هذه الشواهد دالة على أن هذه الإدراكات مختصة بهذه الأعضاء . فثبت : أن جوهر النفس جوهر مخصوص موصوف بهذه الصفات ، وثبت : أن الموصوف بهذه الصفات : هو هذه الأعضاء ، فوجب القطع بأن جوهر النفس إما هذه البنية ، وإما أجسام سارية في هذه البنية مشابكة لها .
The fourth proof: that my soul is a substance, described as having knowledge, thinking, recollection, sight, hearing, taste, smell, desire, anger, movement, and rest. Then that which is described by these attributes is nothing but this bodily structure or what is inside it, so it follows that my soul is this body or what is inside it. And in establishing this proof, it is necessary to establish two matters. The first: that my soul is the substance described by these attributes. The evidence for it is that I do not doubt that when I see visible things, I see them. And when I hear letters and sounds, I hear them. Because the 'ta' in my saying 'I saw' and 'I heard' and 'I understood' and 'I knew' is the pronoun of the soul. And there is no difference between your saying 'I saw' and 'I heard' and your saying 'I am described as having such-and-such sight and such-and-such hearing.' And since the intuition of the intellect rules that I saw and heard, that intuition rules that I am the one described by these attributes. As for the second, it is in explaining that the one described by these attributes is this bodily structure. The evidence for it is that just as I know necessarily that the taste of food is not located in the lower leg and the shin, I know necessarily that the taste of food is located in the tongue, and that hearing of sound is located in the ear, and likewise for the rest of the sensations. Also, man knows intuitively that he finds thought and imagination from the side of the brain, and finds knowledge and perception from the direction of the heart. And all these evidences indicate that these perceptions are particular to these organs. So it is established that the substance of the soul is a particular substance described by these attributes, and it is established that the one described by these attributes is these organs, so it is necessarily concluded that the substance of the soul is either this bodily structure or bodies flowing in this bodily structure interwoven with it.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the soul is identical to the body. It is a definitional and empirical argument structured in two premises (amrain). The first premise establishes that the soul is the substance that possesses attributes like knowledge, thought, sensation, desire, etc. The evidence is linguistic and intuitive: when one says 'I saw,' the pronoun 'I' refers to the soul, and intuition confirms that the 'I' is the subject of these experiences. The second premise shows that the subject of these attributes is the bodily structure itself. Evidence comes from necessary knowledge that taste is in the tongue, hearing in the ear, thought in the brain, perception in the heart. Thus, all perceptions are localized in specific organs. The conclusion follows: the substance of the soul must be either the entire bodily structure or subtle bodies flowing within it. The proof thus argues for a material conception of the soul, rejecting a separate immaterial substance.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
جوهر (jawhar)
بدن (badan)
صفات (sifat)
بديهة العقل (badihah al-aql)
إدراكات (idrakat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims the soul is only the physical body with no subtle aspect.
Correction: The conclusion actually allows that the soul could be 'bodies flowing in this bodily structure interwoven with it,' leaving room for a subtle material substance.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what is the evidence that the one described by mental attributes is the bodily structure?
A) The intuition that the self is a separate substance.
B) The necessary knowledge that perceptions like taste and hearing are located in specific organs.
C) The claim that the soul is a captain of the body.
D) The impossibility of the soul transferring between bodies.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fourth proof uses observable localization of mental faculties to argue that the soul must be the body or a subtle material substance within it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience anger or desire, do you automatically attribute it to your body or to something immaterial? How does Al-Razi's argument challenge your immediate intuition, and what does that mean for how you understand your own self? Will you now examine your emotions as bodily events, or resist that reduction?
?
Consider a time when you felt a sudden burst of thought or emotion. Could you pinpoint it to a specific bodily location? If not, does that weaken or strengthen Al-Razi's proof? What reality does your answer point to about the nature of your soul, and are you willing to accept that conclusion?
?
The proof relies on the intuition that when you see, you are the one seeing. But have you ever felt detached from your senses, for example when tired or drugged? How does that experience complicate the simple identity of 'I' and the body, and what does that say about the limitations of this proof? In light of your own experiences, where do you stand on the soul-body question, and what will you do to seek certainty?

The Fifth Proof: Soul as Body via Transfer Impossibility
Al-Razi's proof by transfer impossibility and refutation of natural love objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's fifth proof that the soul is identical to the body because it cannot transfer to another body, and how he refutes the objection of natural love by analyzing the concept of union.

Core Idea
If the soul were independent, it could manage any body – its inability proves it is the body.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a concise argument for the identity of soul and body. Using an analogy with a sailor and a ship, he shows that a separate soul would be able to transfer between bodies. Since that is impossible, the soul must be the body itself. He then anticipates an objection based on natural love and refutes it by demonstrating that union of separate substances is logically incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof carefully
Read the passage where al-Razi presents the analogy of the sailor and the ship. Note the logical structure: if the soul is separate, it could manage any body; since it cannot transfer, it must be the body.
The analogy is key to understanding the argument.
2
Understand the objection about natural love
Identify the objection that the soul has a natural love (ishq tabi'i) for its particular body, which prevents it from transferring. Consider why this might be a plausible counterargument.
3
Follow the refutation through the analysis of union
Al-Razi refutes the objection by analyzing the concept of union (ittihad). He lists three logical possibilities for what union could mean and shows each is impossible. Conclude that the soul's attachment cannot be a union of separate substances.
The three possibilities are: both remain, both cease and a third arises, or one remains and the other ceases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن النفس لو كانت جوهرا مفارقا للبدن ، ولا تعلق لها بالبدن ، إلا تعلق التدبير والتصرف، كتعلق الملاح بالسفينة ، والجمال بالمحمول، فكما أن الملاح يمكنه أن يترك تدبير هذه السفينة ، ويشتغل بتدبير سفينة أخرى ، وجب أن تتمكن النفس من أن تترك تدبير هذا البدن ، وتشتغل بتدبير بدن آخر . يعني أن مدير هذا البدن ، ينتقل إلى تدبير ذلك البدن ، ومدير ذلك البدن ينتقل إلى تدبير هذا البدن . ولما كان ذلك ممتنعا ، علمنا : أن النفس ليست إلا هذا البدن . فإن قالوا : تم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه النفس لها انتقالها عشق طبيعي ، وشوق ذاتي ، إلى تدبير هذا البدن ، فلهذا السبب يمتنع إلى تدبير بدن آخر . والجواب عن الأول : إن القول بالاتحاد باطل ، مشهور البطلان . لأنهما بعد الاتحاد إن بقيا معا . فهما اثنان لا واحد ، وإن عدما معا ، وحدث ثالث فهذا ليس من الاتحاد في شيء وإن بقي أحدهما وعدم الآخر . فهذا أيضا ليس
The fifth proof: if the soul were a substance separate from the body, and it had no attachment to the body except the attachment of management and disposition, like the attachment of the sailor to the ship and the porter to the carried load, then just as the sailor can leave managing this ship and occupy himself with managing another ship, it would be necessary that the soul be able to leave managing this body and occupy itself with managing another body. That is, the manager of this body would transfer to managing that body, and the manager of that body would transfer to managing this body. And since that is impossible, we know that the soul is nothing but this body. Then if they say: it is not permissible to say that this soul has a natural love and an essential longing for managing this body, and for this reason it is prevented from [transferring to] managing another body. And the answer to the first [objection]: the claim of union is false, famously false. Because after union, if they both remain, then they are two, not one; and if they both cease and a third occurs, then this is not union at all; and if one remains and the other ceases, then this also is not.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof and an objection with its answer. The proof argues by analogy: if the soul were separate like a captain, it could manage any body; since it cannot transfer, it is not separate but identical to the body. The objection claims the soul has a natural love (ishq tabi'i) for its particular body, preventing transfer. The answer refutes this by analyzing the concept of union (ittihad). Union of two separate entities is impossible: if both remain, they are two; if both vanish and a third arises, it is not union; if one remains and the other vanishes, it is not union either. Thus, the soul's attachment to the body cannot be a union of separate substances, supporting the conclusion that the soul is the body itself.

Key Terms
نفس
جوهر مفارق
تدبير
عشق طبيعي
اتحاد
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural love (ishq tabi'i) could explain why the soul is attached to one particular body even if it is separate.
Correction: Al-Razi's refutation shows that any real union of separate substances is logically impossible, so natural love cannot salvage the idea of a separate soul.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fifth proof?
A) The soul is like a captain that can manage many bodies.
B) Because the soul cannot transfer to another body, it must be the body itself.
C) The soul has a natural love for the body.
D) The soul is a separate substance that manages the body.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of soul transfer between bodies proves the soul is not separate but identical to the body, and the notion of natural love cannot salvage a separate soul because union of distinct substances is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt as though your 'self' is trapped in your body, wishing you could escape its limitations? What does al-Razi's proof of immanent transfer tell you about the reality of that feeling? Will you continue to imagine yourself as a pilot in a vessel, or will you accept that you are the vessel itself?
?
If your soul were truly capable of managing another body, what would that mean for your relationships and responsibilities? The fact that you cannot abandon your body—even in fantasy—reveals something undeniable. Are you ready to stop treating your body as a mere tool and start seeing it as your very self?
?
Al-Razi demolishes the idea of 'natural love' as a refuge for dualism, showing that even if love existed, it could never produce a true union. What attachments in your life have you justified by calling them 'natural' or 'essential'? Will you let this logical standard expose the emptiness of those excuses?

Razi refutes the soul's natural love for a specific body
Razi refutes the claim of the soul's natural love for a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument against the claim that the soul has a natural love for a specific body.

Core Idea
The soul loves no body in particular because all bodies equally serve its goals.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles the objection that each soul has a natural longing for a specific body. He argues that such a claim is weak because the soul's aim is to obtain pleasures, and all bodies are equally capable of providing them. Razi then presents a decisive proof to show that preference among equals is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of Razi's argument.
2
Identify the objection and Razi's response
Notice that Razi first states the objection (the soul has natural love for a body) and then declares it weak. Then he gives two levels of reasoning: a general statement and a decisive proof.
The proof uses an analogy of two equal paths.
3
Reflect on the argument's structure
Trace the logic: soul seeks pleasures → all bodies provide equally → no preference → no natural love. Consider how this refutes the objection.
This shows Razi's method of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : وهو قولكم : النفس المعينة لها عشق طبيعي ، وشوق ذاتي ، إلى تدبير هذا البدن. فنقول : هذا أيضاً ضعيف . لان عشق البدن لا يصح إلا [ ممن يقدر ] على الإلتذاذ بالمحسوسات ، وإلا على الإنتفاع بما في عالم الجسمانيات من المنافع والراحات . فأما أن تكون النفس عاشقة لهذه الآلهة بعينها ، مع أنه يمكنها تحصيل المقصود بسائر الآلات . فهذا محال . والدليل القاطع عليه : أن النفس متى جزمت وقطعت بأن تحصيل هذه اللذات والطيبات بهذا الطريق : ممكن . وبالطريق الثاني أيضاً : ممكن . وأنه لا رجحان لأحد الطريقين على الثاني في كثرة الفوائد والمنافع . فههنا يمتنع أن يكون للنفس ميل إلى أحد الطريقين بعينه دون الثاني . فكذا ههنا ، مطلوب النفس هو الإنتفاع باللذات الجسمانية والراحات الشهوانية. ولما كانت الأبدان بأسرها متساوية في الإفضاء إلى هذا المطلوب ، وجب أن تكون نسبة النفس إليها بأسرها على السوية. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان قولهم : إن النفس المعينة عاشقة لهذا البدن المعين عشقاً طبيعياً : قول باطل
And the answer to the second [objection]: which is your saying: the specific soul has a natural love and an essential longing to manage this body. We say: this too is weak. Because love of the body is not valid except from one who is able to take pleasure in sensory things, and to benefit from the benefits and comforts in the world of corporeal things. But that the soul should be in love with these specific instruments, when it can obtain the goal by other instruments, this is impossible. And the decisive proof for it is: when the soul firmly decides and determines that obtaining these pleasures and delights by this way is possible, and by the second way also possible, and that there is no preference of one way over the other in abundance of benefits and advantages, then it is impossible for the soul to have an inclination to one specific way to the exclusion of the other. Similarly here: the soul's goal is to benefit from corporeal pleasures and sensual comforts. And since all bodies are equal in leading to this goal, the soul's relation to all of them must be equal. And when this is the case, their statement that the specific soul loves this specific body with a natural love is a false statement.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection: the claim that each soul has a natural love and essential longing for a particular body. Razi first calls this claim weak. He argues that love for a body is only meaningful for someone who seeks sensory pleasures and benefits from the physical world. But the soul can achieve its goals through various bodies, not just one. He then gives a decisive proof: if a person knows that two different paths equally lead to the same goal with no advantage to either, then the soul cannot have an inclination to one path specifically. Similarly, the soul's goal is to obtain physical pleasures and sensual comforts, and all bodies serve this purpose equally. Therefore, the soul's relation to all bodies is equal, and the claim that it loves one particular body naturally is false.

Key Terms
نفس
عشق طبيعي
بدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul has a necessary natural love for its body.
Correction: Razi argues that the soul's attachment is purely instrumental; it loves the body only as a means to attain pleasures, and since all bodies are equally effective, there is no specific love.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the soul cannot have a natural love for a specific body?
A) Because the soul is immaterial and cannot love anything material.
B) Because all bodies equally serve the soul's goal of attaining pleasures, so there is no reason for preference.
C) Because the soul was created before the body.
D) Because the soul's love is only for God.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the idea of a natural soul-body love by showing that all bodies are equally suited to the soul's quest for pleasure, so preferential love is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a person, place, or thing you feel strongly attached to. Is that attachment truly about that specific entity, or is it about the pleasure and benefit you receive through it? Would you still cling to it if you could get the same benefit from something else? Be honest — what does your answer reveal about the nature of your love?
?
Razi claims the soul's relation to all bodies is equal because all equally provide pleasure. In your own life, have you ever elevated a particular 'instrument' as indispensable, even though the real goal could be achieved elsewhere? What would it look like to treat all worldly means as equal in value, focusing only on the ultimate goal?

Razi's Sixth Proof: The Soul is Not Transcendent
Razi argues that if the soul were transcendent like God, its nature would be self-evident, which it is not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof that the human soul is not a transcendent abstract entity like Allah, based on the self-evident nature of self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge would reveal transcendence if it existed, but it does not.

Razi presents another argument about the soul's nature, this time from the evident character of self-knowledge. He argues that if the soul were an abstract entity beyond space and time, like God, then everyone would know this by necessity. Since that is not the case, the soul cannot be such an entity. The proof is a straightforward conditional argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about self-knowledge
Razi begins by claiming that self-knowledge is the most evident and strongest of all knowledges. Notice his evidence: knowledge of other things depends on self-knowledge, so the dependent cannot be stronger.
This premise is crucial for the argument's logic.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi then says: if the soul were a transcendent abstract entity (like God, neither inside nor outside the world), then I would know by necessity that I am such an entity. This is the 'if' part.
3
Examine the rejection of the consequent
Razi shows that the consequent is false: many rationalists deny that such a being can exist even for God, and those who affirm it for God never think it applies to themselves. So the consequent 'I would know by necessity' is not true.
The falsity of the consequent is based on empirical observation of disagreement.
4
Conclude the argument
Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (that the soul is a transcendent entity) must also be false. Thus the soul is not such an entity.
This is a modus tollens argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن علمي بنفسي أظهر العلوم وأقواها والدليل عليه : أن علمي بما يغاير نفسي، تابع لعلمي بنفسي ، والتابع يمتنع أن يكون أقوى من المتبوع . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كانت نفسي موجوداً مجرداً ، لا داخل العالم ، ولا خارج العالم ، ولا متصلاً بالعالم ولا مفصلا عنه كما أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى - موجود لا داخل العالم ، ولا خارجاً عنه ، ولا متصلاً بالعالم ، ولا منفصلا عنه، لكنت أعلم بالضرورة : أني موجود وموصوف بهذه الصفة . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . لأن كثيراً من العقلاء زعموا : أن إثبات هذا الموجود بهذه الصفة : محال في العقول ، حتى قالوا : إن إله العالم يجب أن يكون متحيزاً ، ومختصاً بالحيز والجهة . وأما العقلاء الذين سلموا ثبوت هذا المعنى في حق الله تعالى ، فلم يخطر ببال أحد منهم : أنه في نفسه موصوف بهذه الصفة . فثبت : أن نفس الإنسان ، لو كان موصوفاً بهذه الصفة ، لكان العلم بكونه كذلك ، علماً ضرورياً ، وثبت : أن التالي باطل ، فكان المقدم باطلاً
The sixth proof: My knowledge of myself is the most evident and strongest of knowledges. The evidence for it is that my knowledge of what is other than myself is dependent on my knowledge of myself, and the dependent cannot be stronger than the independent. And when this is established, we say: If my soul were an existent abstract entity, neither inside the world nor outside the world, neither connected to the world nor separate from it, just as the Creator, exalted and high, is an existent neither inside the world nor outside it, neither connected to the world nor separate from it, then I would know by necessity that I exist and am described with this description. And it is known that this is false. Because many of the rationalists claimed that affirming this existent with this description is impossible in intellects, such that they said: The God of the world must be corporeal and specific to location and direction. And as for the rationalists who affirmed the existence of this meaning with respect to God, it did not occur to any of them that he himself is described with this description. So it is established: If the human soul were described with this description, then knowledge of its being thus would be necessary knowledge, and it is established that the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the sixth) regarding the nature of the soul. Razi begins by asserting that self-knowledge is the most evident and powerful knowledge. The evidence: knowledge of other things depends on self-knowledge, and a dependent cannot be stronger than that on which it depends. Then he argues hypothetically: if the soul were an abstract existent that is neither inside nor outside the world, neither connected nor disconnected (like God), then everyone would know by necessity that they exist with that description. But this is false, because many rationalists deny that such an entity can exist even for God, claiming God must be corporeal and located. Even those who affirm such an attribute for God never think that they themselves have that attribute. So if the soul were like that, knowledge of it would be necessary, but it is not, so the assumption is false.

Key Terms
علم النفس
وجود مجرد
باري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul's transcendence is obvious or self-evident.
Correction: Razi argues that if it were transcendent, everyone would know it necessarily, but since they do not (as evidenced by disagreement), it cannot be transcendent.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the sixth proof?
A) The soul is a transcendent entity like God.
B) The soul cannot be a transcendent, abstract existent.
C) Self-knowledge is weaker than knowledge of others.
D) God is inside the world.

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof demonstrates that the soul cannot be a transcendent entity like Allah because if it were, its transcendence would be necessarily known, yet it is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your self-knowledge is truly the strongest, why are you so often uncertain about who you really are? What does that uncertainty reveal about your soul?
?
Razi says that if your soul were transcendent like God, you would know it necessarily—without doubt. Do you have any doubt about your soul's nature? If yes, what does that imply about the kind of being you are?
?
Even those who affirm God's transcendence never claim that they themselves are transcendent. Have you ever, even subtly, assumed that you share some attribute of God? What would it mean to truly accept that your soul is not like Him?

Negative Restrictions vs. Knowledge of Essence
Objection that negative restrictions do not entail knowledge of essence, with reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between knowledge of an essence and knowledge of its negative restrictions, and how this distinction functions as an objection in theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Negative restrictions do not give us knowledge of the essence itself.

This short passage presents a subtle objection to a proof about the soul. The objector argues that knowing what something is not does not mean you know what it is. Before we evaluate the strength of the proof, we need to grasp this logical distinction clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The passage presents a possible objection (that the restrictions are negative) and a reply. Find both parts.
The reply seems to accept the point but still maintains the proof?
3
Understand the key distinction
The key is: knowledge of what something is not (negative restrictions) is different from knowledge of what it is (essence). Ask yourself if this distinction weakens the proof.
This is a fundamental point in logic and theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this objection challenges the sixth proof. If the proof relied on negative attributes alone, does knowing those attributes give us knowledge of the soul's essence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمجيب أن يجيب عنه ، فيقول : هذه القيود سلبية ، والعلم بالماهية ، مغاير للعلم بقيودها السلبية ، وغير موجب له
And a responder may answer concerning it, saying: These restrictions are negative, and knowledge of the essence is different from knowledge of its negative restrictions, and does not necessitate it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a possible objection and answer to the sixth proof. The objector argues that the attributes mentioned (not inside, not outside, etc.) are negative restrictions. Knowledge of the essence (al-mahiyya) is distinct from knowledge of its negative restrictions, and knowledge of the latter does not imply knowledge of the former. In the context, this challenges Razi's claim that if the soul had those negative attributes, knowledge of its essence would be necessary. The reply (likely Razi's own) acknowledges the distinction but seems to accept the point, leaving the force of the proof intact.

Key Terms

ماهية
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing the negative attributes of something (what it is not) is the same as knowing its essence.
Correction: Knowledge of negative restrictions is distinct from knowledge of the essence; the former does not necessitate the latter.

Checkpoint

A) They are essential to the soul.
B) Knowledge of them is identical to knowledge of the essence.
C) They are negative and distinct from the essence.
D) They prove the soul's transcendence.

Key Takeaway
Negative restrictions are not equivalent to the essence, and knowing them does not automatically give knowledge of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Body as Locus of Perceptions
Razi introduces the seventh proof and its first premise with partial evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the body is the locus of perceptions and voluntary powers, and how this supports the view that the human being is the body and its contents.

Core Idea
The body is not a shell; it is the very seat of perception and will.

Razi presents the seventh proof for identifying the human self with the body. He argues that the body is the locus of both particular and universal perceptions, and also the locus of power over perceptions and voluntary movements. If that is the case, then the body and what is in it must be the human being. This lesson examines the first premise of this proof and the initial evidence Razi provides.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's opening claim
Read the first two sentences of the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Razi states his conclusion before giving the premises.
This is a common structure in kalam: state the thesis, then provide the proof.
2
Identify the first premise
Find where Razi says 'The first premise' and what it is. Understand that he is breaking the proof into parts.
3
Examine the evidence for the first premise
Razi gives a brief argument for the first premise using a dichotomy. Read the evidence and see why he rejects the second option.
The passage ends before the argument is complete, but you can grasp the direction.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Razi thinks the body must be the locus of perceptions. How does this connect to the overall proof that the body is the human self?
This connects to debates about whether the self is the body, the soul, or a composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : هذا الجسد محل للإدراكات الجزئية والكلية ، ومحل للقدرة على الإدراكات والتحريكات الإرادية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون هذا الجسد وما فيه هو الإنسان . فهذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمات المقدمة الأولى : وهي إن هذا الجسد محل للإدراكات . فالدليل عليه : أن هذا الجسد . إما أن يقال : حصل فيه قوة شعور وإدراك ، أو لم يحصل فيه البتة قوة الشعور والإدراك والقسم الثاني : باطل . لأن كل جسم يكون خالياً عن قوة الشعور والإدراك ، يكون جماداً محضاً ، فإنا لا نعقل بين الجماد وبين الحيوان : فرقاً
The seventh proof: This body is a locus for particular and universal perceptions, and a locus for power over perceptions and voluntary movements. And when this is the case, it follows that this body and what is in it is the human being. This proof is based on premises. The first premise: That this body is a locus for perceptions. The evidence for it: This body—either it is said that a power of sensation and perception occurs in it, or that no power of sensation and perception occurs in it at all. The second division is false, because every body devoid of the power of sensation and perception is pure inanimate matter, for we do not conceive a difference between inanimate matter and the animal.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh proof, which argues that the body and its contents constitute the human being. Razi states that the body is the locus (maḥall) for both particular and universal perceptions, and for the power over perceptions and voluntary movements. If so, the body and what is in it must be the human. He then begins to break down the proof into premises. The first premise is that the body is a locus for perceptions. The evidence: either the body has a power of sensation and perception, or it does not. The second option is false, because a body without such power is mere inanimate matter, and we cannot distinguish inanimate matter from an animal. The argument is incomplete here, but it establishes the first premise by elimination.

Key Terms
جسد
محل للإدراكات
قوة الشعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body is merely a passive vehicle that the soul uses to interact with the world.
Correction: Razi argues that the body is itself the locus of perception and voluntary power, meaning it is not a passive shell but the very site of the self's activities.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the seventh proof?
A) The soul is separate from the body.
B) The body is a locus for perceptions.
C) The body is composed of humors.
D) The soul is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
Razi's seventh proof begins with the claim that the body is the locus of perceptions and powers, which leads to the conclusion that the body and its contents constitute the human self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is the very locus of all your perceptions and voluntary actions, not merely a vehicle, what changes would you make today in how you care for your eyes, ears, and limbs?
?
Razi argues that a body without perception is pure inanimate matter. When you are asleep or unconscious, your body still has the potential for perception. Do you live as if your body is merely a tool, or as the very seat of your self? Name one habit that treats your body as a vehicle and one step you will take this week to honor it as your self.
?
If your body is the locus of universal perceptions (concepts), then your ability to think and reason is tied to your physical brain. How does that challenge or affirm your understanding of the relationship between mind and body? Commit to one action this week that dignifies the body as the foundation of your intellect.

The Body Perceives Universals: Al-Razi's Argument
Al-Razi proves that the body perceives universals using logical analysis of compound perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's proof that the body must perceive universals, based on the logical analysis of compound perceptions.

Core Idea
Perceiving a particular means perceiving a universal in disguise.

Al-Razi presents the second premise of his seventh argument: that the body perceives universals. He builds on the already-established fact that the body perceives particulars. Using a logical principle about compound concepts, he shows that universal perception is unavoidable. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical connectives like 'لما ثبت في المنطق'.
The argument hinges on a logical principle from the chapter on 'al-dhati al-muqawwim' (constitutive essential).
2
Identify the key logical principle
Al-Razi states that 'the conception of the compound is preceded by the conception of its simple parts.' Understand why this principle is crucial for the argument.
This principle is from logic: you cannot conceive a whole without conceiving its parts.
3
Analyze the first example: pain
Follow the example of pain: 'this pain' is a compound of the universal 'pain' plus the particularizing 'this'. The perceiver of 'this pain' must therefore perceive 'pain' itself, which is a universal essence.
4
Analyze the second example: vision of a human
Similarly, seeing 'this human' involves perceiving the universal 'human' and the individuating 'this'. Thus the visual power perceives the universal essence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion: since the body perceives particulars (established earlier), and perceiving any particular requires perceiving universals, the body must perceive universals. This supports the overall argument for the bodily self.
This premise is a building block in al-Razi's larger argument for the nature of the self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدمة الثانية : في بيان أن هذا البدن مدرك للكليات . فنقول : الدليل على صحة هذه المقدمة : إنا بينا : أن البدن مدرك للجزئيات ، وكل ما كان مدركاً للجزئيات ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات . والدليل عليه : إن هذا الألم عبارة عن الألم مع قيد كونه هذا ، ومدرك المركب ، لا بد وأن يكون مدركاً لمفرداته ، لما ثبت في المنطق في باب الذاتي المقوم : إن تصور المركب ، مسبوق بتصور مفرداته . فثبت : أن مدرك هذا الألم مدرك للألم ، والألم ماهية كلية ، فثبت : أن مدرك الجزئيات ، يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات. وأيضاً : قد بينا أن البدن يبصر هذا الإنسان ، وهذا الإنسان عبارة عن الإنسان مع قيد كونه هذا . فمبصر هذا الإنسان يجب أن يكون قد أبصر الإنسان . وأبصر كونه هذا . لكن الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان ماهية كلية . فالقوة الباصرة قد أدركت الماهية الكلية
And the second premise: In explanation that this body is a perceiver of universals. So we say: The proof of the soundness of this premise: We have shown that the body is a perceiver of particulars, and everything that is a perceiver of particulars must be a perceiver of universals. And the proof of this: This pain is expression of pain with the qualification of being this particular; and the perceiver of the compound must be a perceiver of its simple parts, as established in logic in the chapter on the constitutive essential: that the conception of the compound is preceded by the conception of its simple parts. So it is established that the perceiver of this pain is a perceiver of pain, and pain is a universal essence. So it is established that the perceiver of particulars must be a perceiver of universals. Also: We have shown that the body sees this human, and this human is expression of the human with the qualification of being this particular. So the seer of this human must have seen the human and seen its being this particular. But the human insofar as it is a human is a universal essence. So the visual power has perceived the universal essence.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise (al-muqaddima al-thāniya) of the seventh argument: that the body perceives universals (kulliyāt). The proof is that since the body perceives particulars (juzʾiyāt), and anything that perceives particulars must perceive universals. The reasoning is based on the nature of compound perception: a particular pain is a compound of the universal pain and the particularizing qualifier (this). Following logical principles, the perceiver of the compound must perceive its components; thus, the perceiver of the particular pain perceives the universal pain, which is a universal essence. Similarly, vision of a particular human involves perception of the universal human essence plus the individuating qualifier. Therefore, the visual power perceives the universal essence. This supports the overall argument that the body (with its faculties) is the human subject of both particular and universal perception.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body only perceives particulars; universals are perceived by an immaterial intellect.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even bodily perception of a particular requires perceiving the universal essence, so the body does perceive universals.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that the body perceives universals?
A) By arguing that perception of a particular necessarily involves perception of the universal essence.
B) By appealing to divine revelation about the soul.
C) By arguing that the body is not material and thus can perceive abstract ideas.
D) By citing Aristotelian authority on the active intellect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the body perceives universals by showing that every particular perception contains a universal essence.

What would change if you meditated on the fact that your pain is not merely yours but shared in a universal reality?

Al-Razi argues that seeing a particular human requires seeing the universal 'human'. The next time you look at someone, pause and ask yourself: Am I only seeing their outward particularity, or do I also perceive the universal human essence? How might this shift your interactions with them, especially when they are difficult or different from you?
?
This argument shows that your body—through its senses—perceives universals. Does your daily experience reflect this truth? Or do you live as if your bodily perceptions are only particular and isolated? What practical step can you take today to become more aware of the universal dimension in your everyday sensations?

Three Arguments Against Perceiving Only Particularity
Author gives three arguments proving visual perception includes quiddity, not just particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three logical arguments proving that visual perception includes the quiddity (essence) of objects, not just their particularity.

Core Idea
Visual perception sees both the 'what' and the 'this'.

The author anticipates an objection that sight only apprehends the bare 'this-ness' of an object. To refute this, he presents three powerful arguments grounded in the nature of determination (ta'ayyun) and universality. This passage is a masterclass in applying logical reasoning to defend the scope of sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the objection being refuted: that visual perception only perceives particularity (ta'ayyun) and not the quiddity (e.g., blackness or whiteness).
The objection is mentioned at the beginning of the passage.
2
Follow Argument One: Privative condition
The first argument states that determination (ta'ayyun) is a privative condition (qayd 'adami), meaning it has no positive existence, so it cannot be an object of sight.
This argument relies on the metaphysical nature of ta'ayyun.
3
Follow Argument Two: Indistinguishability
The second argument: if only particularity were perceived, two individuals (e.g., this black and this white) would be indistinguishable since they share the same nature of determination; but we do distinguish them, so quiddity must be perceived.
This argument uses the absurd consequence of the opposing view.
4
Follow Argument Three: Universality and regress
The third argument: even if determination were positive and sensed, it would itself be a universal quiddity, so the sensed would be universal. If one objects that the sensed is the determination of determination, an infinite regress follows.
This argument attacks the coherence of the opposing position.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that visual perception includes both particularity and quiddity. Reflect on how the three arguments together refute the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنجيب عن الأول : بأنا إذا أدركنا هذا السواد وهذا البياض . فإما أن يكون المرئي ، مجرد كون هذا : هذا . وذاك : ذاك . من غير أن تتعلق القوة الباصرة بماهية السواد والبياض ، وإما أن يكون المرئي مجموع كونه هذا . كونه سواداً ، أو مجموع كونه ذاك بياضاً . أما الأول فباطل لوجوه : أحدها : إنا قد دللنا على أن التعين قيد عدمي ، فيمتنع كونه مرئياً . وثانيها : إن بتقدير أن يكون [ التعين أمراً ثابتاً ، فإن تعين هذا وتعين ذاك . كل واحد منهما يتشاركان في كونه تعينا ] فهذا التعين وذلك التعين شخصان داخلان تحت نوع واحد ، ولا مخالفة بينهما في الماهية أصلا . فإذا أحسسنا بهذا السواد وهذا البياض ، وفرضنا أن المحسوس ليس إلا مجرد كونه هذا وذاك ، وثبت أن مجرد كونه هذا وذاك شخصان داخلان تحت نوع واحد وأنه لا مخالفة بينهما في الماهية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يحس من طريق الإبصار بكون السواد والبياض متخالفين. ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بالبديهة ، علمنا : أنه ليس المحسوس بحس البصر، هو مجرد كونه هذا وذاك، بل كونه سواداً أو بياضاً أيضاً محسوس بحس البصر . وثالثها : إن بتقدير أن يكون التعين أمراً ثابتاً ، وأن يكون المحسوس هو التعين. إلا أن التعين أيضاً ماهية كلية فيكون المحسوس بالبصر أيضاً أمراً كلياً . إلا أن يقال : المحسوس ليس هو التعين بل تعين التعين . ثم يعود الكلام الأول فيه ، ويلزم التسلسل
We answer the first [objection]: when we perceive this blackness and this whiteness, then either the visible is merely the fact of being this and that, without the visual power attaching to the quiddity of blackness and whiteness, or the visible is the sum of its being this and its being black, or the sum of its being that and its being white. The first is false for several reasons: One: We have shown that determination (al-ta'ayyun) is a privative condition (qayd 'adami), so it cannot be visible. Two: Even if determination is something fixed, this determination and that determination each share in being a determination; these two determinations are two individuals under one species, with no essential difference at all. So if we sense this black and this white, and suppose that the sensed is only the mere being-this and being-that, and it is established that mere being-this and being-that are two individuals under one species with no essential difference, then it would follow that we do not sense through vision that black and white are different. Since that is obviously false, we know that what is sensed by sight is not merely being-this and being-that, but its being black or white is also sensed by sight. Three: Even if determination is fixed and the sensed is determination, yet determination itself is a universal quiddity, so what is sensed by sight is also universal. Unless it is said that the sensed is not determination but the determination of determination. Then the same argument recurs, leading to an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that visual perception only apprehends particularity (the 'this-ness') and not the quiddity. The author presents three arguments. First, determination (ta'ayyun) is a privative condition, meaning it is not a positive reality, so it cannot be an object of sight. Second, if only determination were perceived, then two different determinations (e.g., this black and this white) would be indistinguishable as they share the same nature of determination; but we do distinguish them, so the quiddity must be perceived. Third, even if determination were fixed and sensed, determination itself is a universal quiddity, so the sensed would be universal; if one objects that the sensed is the determination of determination, that leads to an infinite regress. Thus, the author concludes that visual perception includes both particularity and the quidditive essence.

قيد عدمي
ماهية
سواد
بياض
إبصار
مرئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sight only perceives the particularity (the 'this-ness') of an object, not its essential nature.
Correction: The author shows that quiddities like blackness and whiteness are also perceived, as demonstrated by three arguments: (1) particularity is a privative condition and cannot be seen; (2) if only particularity were seen, different objects would be indistinguishable; (3) even if particularity were seen, it would be universal, leading to regress.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a universal
B) Because it is a privative condition
C) Because it leads to infinite regress
D) Because it is not perceived by the senses

Key Takeaway
Visual perception reveals both the individual and its essential nature; the quiddity is as much perceived as the particularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If determination is a privative condition, then what you consider 'unique' about yourself might be an absence, not a positive trait. How does that change your self-perception and your gratitude to Allah?
?
The author argues that quiddity is perceived. When you see a sunset, do you pause to recognize the quiddity of beauty itself? What would change if you consciously perceived the essential qualities behind every sight?
