Distinguishing Logical, Mental, and Natural Universals
Author answers second objection by distinguishing three types of universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between logical, mental, and natural universals and why only the natural universal is relevant to the argument about perception.

Core Idea
The natural universal is perceived through particulars — the soul grasps the quiddity of what it senses.

After establishing that sensing individual black and white gives sensation of blackness and whiteness, the author turns to a second objection: why claim the universal is perceived by sight? The answer lies in distinguishing three types of universal. This lesson clarifies that distinction and explains the key principle that perceiving particulars entails perceiving natural universals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conclusion from the first part
Read the opening sentence that sums up the previous arguments: sensing individual black and white yields sensation of those colors themselves.
This is the conclusion of the first refutation.
2
Identify the second objection
Find the objection: even if we perceive blackness, why say we perceive it as a universal? The author now answers this.
The objection is introduced by 'هب أنا أدركنا السواد بحس البصر...'
3
Learn the three types of universal
The author defines logical universal (universality itself), mental universal (quiddity with condition of universality), and natural universal (quiddity that can be judged universal).
Only the natural universal is relevant here.
4
Grasp the principle about the soul and particulars
The passage ends with a key principle: the soul perceives particulars, and whatever perceives particulars must perceive natural universals.
This principle links perception of particulars to perception of their quiddities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذه الوجوه : إنا إذا أحسنا بهذا السواد وبهذا البياض ، فقد حصل الإحساس بالسواد والبياض وأما السؤال الثاني ، وهو قوله : ( هب أنا أدركنا السواد بحس البصر ، لكن لم قلتم : إنا أدركنا بحس البصر السواد من حيث إنه كلي ) ؟ فجوابه : إنا قد بينا في المنطق : أن الكلي قد يراد به الكلي المنطقي ، وهو نفس الكلية ، وقد يراد به [ الكلي ] العقلي وهو مجموع الماهية [ مع قيد كونها كلية . وقد يراد به الكلي الطبيعي ، وهي تلك الماهية ] التي يمكن أن يحكم العقل عليها ، بكونها كلية. وكلامنا في هذا المقام في بيان أن الكلي الطبيعي مدرك بالقوة الحساسة . أما الكلي المنطقي والعقلي فلا حاجة إلى ذكرهما في هذا الباب . ثبت بما ذكرنا : إن النفس مدركة للجزئيات ، وكل ما كان مدركاً للجزيئات ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات الطبيعية
Thus it is established by these arguments: when we sense this black and this white, the sensation of blackness and whiteness occurs. As for the second question, namely his statement: 'Suppose we perceive blackness by sight, but why do you say we perceive blackness by sight insofar as it is universal?' The answer is: We have explained in logic that the universal may refer to the logical universal, which is universality itself; it may refer to the mental universal, which is the quiddity together with the condition of its being universal; and it may refer to the natural universal, which is that quiddity that the mind can judge as universal. Our discussion here is to show that the natural universal is perceived by the sensitive power. As for the logical and mental universal, there is no need to mention them in this chapter. It is established by what we have said: the soul perceives particulars, and whatever perceives particulars must necessarily perceive natural universals.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage first concludes the previous three arguments: they prove that when we sense individual black and white, we also sense the quiddities of blackness and whiteness. Then the author addresses a second objection: why claim that the universal is perceived by sight? The answer distinguishes three types of universal: the logical universal (universality itself), the mental universal (the quiddity plus the condition of universality), and the natural universal (the quiddity that the mind can consider as universal). The discussion is specifically about the natural universal, which is perceived by the sensitive power. The author then asserts a key principle: the soul perceives particulars, and any entity that perceives particulars must also perceive natural universals. This is because natural universals are the very quiddities of particulars, so perceiving the particular entails perceiving its quiddity.

Misconception: One might think that the universal perceived by sight is the logical or mental universal.
Correction: The author clarifies that only the natural universal is perceived by the sensitive power; the logical and mental universals are not relevant in this context.

The Third Premise: Proving Voluntary Movement in the Body
Author proves the body has voluntary movement via a syllogism on perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that the body has voluntary movement through the necessity of perception for intention and choice.

Core Idea
Voluntary movement requires perception; the body perceives, so it moves voluntarily.

This lesson unpacks the third premise of the author's argument about the soul-body relationship. You will see a logical chain connecting voluntary movement, intention, choice, and perception. The goal is to show that the body itself—or something within it—must be the source of voluntary actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Note the logical connectors: 'الدليل عليه', 'فإن', 'فثبت', 'فلما', 'وجب'.
The author uses a deductive style.
2
Identify the key components of the proof
The proof states: voluntary mover acts by intention and choice; choice requires perception of the thing's quiddity; therefore the voluntary agent must be percipient.
3
Connect to the body as percipient
Earlier (not in this snippet) it was established that the body perceives particulars and universals. Here the author uses that conclusion to argue that the voluntary agent is the body or something in it.
This step assumes prior knowledge; the passage refers to it.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: If the body is percipient and voluntary movement requires perception, does it follow that the body is the voluntary mover? What alternative does the author leave open?
The author allows that the agent could be 'something existing in the body'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي في بيان أن البدن موصوف بالقدرة على التحريكات الإرادية . والدليل عليه : أن المحرك بالإرادة هو الذي يحرك بواسطة القصد والاختيار . واختيار الشيء مشروط بإدراك ماهيته ، فإن ما لا يكون متصوراً ولا مشعوراً به ، فإنه يمتنع القصد إلى تكوينه . فثبت : أن الفاعل المختار يجب أن يكون مدركاً. فلما كان البدن هو المدرك ، وجب أن يكون الفاعل المختار : هو البدن ، أو شيء موجود في البدن وأما المقدمة الرابعة : وهي أنه لما ثبت أن البدن موصوف بالإدراكات الجزئية ، والإدراكات الكلية ، وبالحركات الإرادية، كان جوهر البدن موصوفاً بمجموع هذه الصفات. ولا معنى للإنسان إلا الجوهر الموصوف بهذه الصفات وذلك يوجب القطع بأن الإنسان هو البدن أو جسم موجود فيه وإذا ثبت هذا
As for the third premise: It is to show that the body is described with the power over voluntary movements. The proof for it is: The voluntary mover is the one that moves by means of intention and choice. And choice of something is conditional upon perception of its quiddity; for that which is not conceived nor perceived, intending its coming into being is impossible. Thus it is established that the free agent must be percipient. Since the body is the percipient, it is necessary that the free agent is the body, or something existing in the body. As for the fourth premise: It is that since it has been established that the body is described with partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements, the substance of the body is described with the sum of these attributes. And the meaning of 'man' is nothing but the substance described with these attributes; and that necessitates the certainty that man is the body or a body existing in it. And when this is established...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth premises in a chain of reasoning about the nature of the human soul or self. The third premise aims to prove that the body possesses the power of voluntary movement. The proof: voluntary motion requires intention and choice, which require perception of the thing's quiddity. Since it was earlier established that the body is percipient (it perceives particulars and universals), the voluntary agent must be the body itself or something within it. The fourth premise builds on this and on earlier conclusions that the body has both particular and universal perceptions and voluntary movement. These three attributes characterize the substance of the body. The definition of 'man' (insan) is the substance possessing these attributes. Therefore, man must be the body or a body existing within it (perhaps a part of the body like the heart or brain). The argument is presented as a step toward a larger conclusion about the soul-body relationship.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Voluntary movement can occur without perception, e.g., reflex actions.
Correction: The author defines voluntary movement as that which proceeds from intention and choice, both of which require perception of the thing's quiddity. Reflex actions are not voluntary in this sense.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Voluntary movement implies perception; since the body perceives, it must be the source of voluntary acts—either directly or through something within it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You often claim your actions are 'involuntary' or 'just habit'. But according to this proof, every voluntary movement requires intention and perception. Which of your daily habits are you actually choosing unthinkingly? Will you bring conscious intention to one habitual action today as an exercise?

The Fourth Premise: Defining Man as the Body or Something in It
Author defines man based on perception and voluntary movement, concluding man is the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines 'man' (insan) as the substance with perception and voluntary movement, concluding that man is the body or a body existing in it.

Core Idea
Man is defined by perception and voluntary movement; the body has both, so man is the body.

This lesson covers the fourth premise, which builds on earlier conclusions. The author has shown that the body possesses partial and universal perceptions as well as voluntary movement. Now he argues that the meaning of 'man' is precisely the substance with these attributes, leading to the conclusion that man is the body or something within it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the cumulative reasoning
Notice the phrase 'لما ثبت أن' (since it has been established) referring back to the body's perceptions and movements. The author is building on previous premises.
This premise is a synthesis: perception + movement = man.
2
Understand the definition of man
The author defines man as 'the substance described with these attributes' (الجوهر الموصوف بهذه الصفات). Identify the three attributes: partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements.
3
Consider the conclusion
Since the body's substance has these attributes, man must be either the body or a body existing in it. Ask yourself why the author leaves the alternative open.
This leaves room for a part of the body (e.g., brain) as the seat of self.
4
Reflect on the significance for soul-body debate
This conclusion narrows down the location of the human self. It is materialistic but not necessarily identical to the whole body.
The argument is part of a larger discussion on the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي في بيان أن البدن موصوف بالقدرة على التحريكات الإرادية . والدليل عليه : أن المحرك بالإرادة هو الذي يحرك بواسطة القصد والاختيار . واختيار الشيء مشروط بإدراك ماهيته ، فإن ما لا يكون متصوراً ولا مشعوراً به ، فإنه يمتنع القصد إلى تكوينه . فثبت : أن الفاعل المختار يجب أن يكون مدركاً. فلما كان البدن هو المدرك ، وجب أن يكون الفاعل المختار : هو البدن ، أو شيء موجود في البدن وأما المقدمة الرابعة : وهي أنه لما ثبت أن البدن موصوف بالإدراكات الجزئية ، والإدراكات الكلية ، وبالحركات الإرادية، كان جوهر البدن موصوفاً بمجموع هذه الصفات. ولا معنى للإنسان إلا الجوهر الموصوف بهذه الصفات وذلك يوجب القطع بأن الإنسان هو البدن أو جسم موجود فيه وإذا ثبت هذا
As for the third premise: It is to show that the body is described with the power over voluntary movements. The proof for it is: The voluntary mover is the one that moves by means of intention and choice. And choice of something is conditional upon perception of its quiddity; for that which is not conceived nor perceived, intending its coming into being is impossible. Thus it is established that the free agent must be percipient. Since the body is the percipient, it is necessary that the free agent is the body, or something existing in the body. As for the fourth premise: It is that since it has been established that the body is described with partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements, the substance of the body is described with the sum of these attributes. And the meaning of 'man' is nothing but the substance described with these attributes; and that necessitates the certainty that man is the body or a body existing in it. And when this is established...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth premises in a chain of reasoning about the nature of the human soul or self. The third premise aims to prove that the body possesses the power of voluntary movement. The proof: voluntary motion requires intention and choice, which require perception of the thing's quiddity. Since it was earlier established that the body is percipient (it perceives particulars and universals), the voluntary agent must be the body itself or something within it. The fourth premise builds on this and on earlier conclusions that the body has both particular and universal perceptions and voluntary movement. These three attributes characterize the substance of the body. The definition of 'man' (insan) is the substance possessing these attributes. Therefore, man must be the body or a body existing within it (perhaps a part of the body like the heart or brain). The argument is presented as a step toward a larger conclusion about the soul-body relationship.

Misconception: Man is purely a non-physical soul separate from the body.
Correction: The author argues that man is the substance with perception and movement, which the body possesses, so man must be physical—either the body or a part of it.

Checkpoint

B) The substance with partial and universal perceptions and voluntary movements

Key Takeaway
Man is defined by perception and voluntary action; since the body's substance has these, man is identical to the body or a physical component within it.

?
If the body is the locus of perception and will, then how you treat your body (through food, rest, worship, sin) is not just a physical matter but a definitional one. What one change will you make today in how you treat your body because of this truth?

The Tenth Proof: Human Being is the Observable Structure
Author presents the tenth proof that the human being is the observable bodily structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the human being is the observable body and why it is considered self-evident (badīhī) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The human being is nothing other than the visible body—this is self-evident to all rational people.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from the Mutakallimun that identifies the human being with the observable body. The argument appeals to universal common sense: when asked 'what is man?' everyone points to the body. The author uses this consensus to establish the knowledge as primary (badīhī) and to refute those who posit a separate rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the strong claim in the opening: 'Indeed, any rational person... points to this particular structure.'
2
Identify the core claim and evidence
Pinpoint the central thesis: the human being is the observable body. Look for the two main pieces of evidence: (1) universal pointing when asked 'what is man?' and (2) the direction of commands, rewards, and punishments to the body.
Also note the appeal to 'first principles of the intellects' (badāʾiʿ al-ʿuqūl).
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that knowledge of man being this body is self-evident (ʿilm badīhī) and that objecting to it is false. Recognize the rhetorical force: it's not just an opinion but a primary truth.
The final sentence mentions that these are the proofs of those who deny the rational soul as a separate substance.
4
Reflect on the context
Consider that this argument is part of a larger debate about the nature of the human being. The author is opposing the view that the soul is an immaterial substance distinct from the body.
The passage does not deny the soul itself but argues that in terms of practical religious address, the human is the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة: وهي الحجة التي عليها تعويل المتكلمين. إن كل عاقل إذا قلت له: ما الإنسان؟ وما حقيقته؟ فإنه يشير إلى هذا الهيكل المخصوص، وهذه البنية المخصوصة. ولو قلت له: الإنسان شيء آخر سوى هذه البنية. فإن جمهور العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم يكذبونه. وأيضاً: أوائل العقول قاضية بأن الخطاب متوجه إلى هذه البنية والثواب والعقاب متوجه إليها والذم والترهيب متوجه إلى هذه البنية. ولو أن رجلاً قال: المأمور والمنهي: شيء آخر، مغاير لهذه البنية. لرأيت العقلاء بأسرهم مطبقين على تكذيبه. وكل ما شهدت بدائه العقول، وصرائح الأذهان ببطلانه، كان بطلانه بديهياً أولياً. فثبت: أن العلم بأن الإنسان هو هذه البنية المشاهدة، وهذا الهيكل المخصوص: علم بديهي. فكان القدح فيه باطلا. فهذه جملة دلائل نفاة النفس الناطقة والله أعلم.
The tenth proof: it is the proof upon which the mutakallimūn (speculative theologians) rely. Indeed, any rational person, if you say to him: 'What is man? And what is his reality?' he points to this particular structure and this particular constitution. And if you said to him: 'Man is something else besides this constitution,' then the majority of rational people, by the first principles of their intellects, would deny him. And also: the first principles of the intellects judge that discourse is directed to this constitution, and reward and punishment are directed to it, and blame and intimidation are directed to this constitution. And if a man said: 'The commander and the prohibited are something else, different from this constitution,' you would see all rational people unanimously denying him. And everything whose falsity is witnessed by the first principles of intellects and the clear intuitions of minds, its falsity is primary (badīhī) and obvious. So it is established that the knowledge that man is this observed constitution and this particular structure is primary knowledge (ʿilm badīhī). So to object to it is false. These are the proofs of the deniers of the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof that the human being is identical to the observable body. It is an argument from common sense and universal consensus. The author states that any rational person, when asked 'What is man?' points to the physical body. If someone claims man is something else, rational people deny him by their innate first principles. Furthermore, commands, rewards, punishments, blame, and intimidation are all directed to this body. If someone says the subject of commands and prohibitions is something other than the body, all rational people would reject that. Therefore, the knowledge that man is this visible body is primary (badīhī) and self-evident, and to deny it is false. The author concludes that these are the proofs of those who deny the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) as a separate substance. Note: The context is a refutation of those who claim the soul is an immaterial substance; the argument here is that the body is the locus of discourse and accountability, so the human being is this body in terms of practical religious address.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa)
بديهي (badīhī)
الهيكل المخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies the existence of the soul altogether.
Correction: The passage does not deny the soul; it argues against the view that the soul is a separate immaterial substance distinct from the body. It affirms that in terms of practical religious address (commands, rewards, punishments), the human being is the body.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim is the self-evident truth about the human being?
A) The human is a rational soul separate from the body.
B) The human is the observable bodily structure.
C) The human is both body and soul together.
D) The human is a composite of mind and matter.

Key Takeaway
The knowledge that the human being is the observable body is self-evident (badīhī) and universally accepted by rational people, making it a foundational proof in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Proof of the Soul's Immateriality from Indivisible Existents
Author narrates a proof for soul's immateriality and begins first premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial structure of a proof for the soul's immateriality (tajarrud al-nafs) based on the existence of indivisible entities, beginning with the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
The soul is immaterial because it knows indivisible realities, which cannot be known by a divisible subject.

In this passage, the author begins laying out a proof that the soul is immaterial (not a body nor residing in a body). The argument relies on the existence of indivisible existents—things that cannot be divided into parts. If such existents are known, the knower must also be indivisible. The first and most fundamental indivisible existent is the Necessary Existent (God), whose indivisibility is argued from His necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal English translation to grasp the author's phrasing and flow. Notice the logical connectors like 'فوجب' (so it is necessary).
The passage is cut off at the end; we only have the beginning of the first evidence.
2
Identify the main claim of the proof
The author states: if there are indivisible existents, then knowledge of them is indivisible, so the knower (the soul) is also indivisible. This leads to the conclusion that the soul is not material.
3
Examine the first premise and its first evidence
The first premise is that there are indivisible existents. The first evidence is the Necessary Existent (God), who is indivisible in every way. Understand why: if divisible, He would be dependent on His parts, thus contingent, contradicting His necessity.
This is a key theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في حكاية الحجة التي هي أنيس المجوء في اثبات تجرد النفس. نقول: إنه حصل في الوجود موجودات غير قابلة للقسمة، فوجب أن يكون العلم بها غير قابل للقسمة، فوجب أن يكون الموصوف بذلك العلم غير قابل للقسمة. وكل متحيز وكل حال في المتحيز منقسم. فوجب أن يكون الموصوف بتلك العلوم غير متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على أربع مقدمات: المقدمة الأولى: في بيان أنه حصل في الوجود موجودات لا تقبل القسمة. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إن واجب الوجود لذاته غير قابل للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه، لا القسمة المقدارية ولا القسمة العقلية الحاصلة من الجنس والفصل ولا القسمة العقلية الحاصلة من المادة والصورة، لأن كل منقسم فهو يحتاج إلى جزئه وجزؤه غيره فكل منقسم فهو محتاج إلى غيره وكل محتاج إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن كل ما يكون
The third chapter: on relating the proof that is Anīs al-Majūʾ? in proving the immateriality (tajarrud) of the soul. We say: There have come to be in existence existents that are not divisible (ghayr qābila lil-qisma). So it is necessary that knowledge of them is not divisible. So it is necessary that the one described with that knowledge is not divisible. And every occupant of place (mutaḥayyiz) and every inherence in an occupant of place is divisible. So it is necessary that the one described with those knowledges is neither an occupant of place nor inhering in an occupant of place. And know that this proof is built upon four premises: The first premise: on showing that there have come to be in existence existents that do not admit division. And it is indicated by aspects: The first: that the Necessary Existent by virtue of Itself (Wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihi) is not divisible in any respect, neither quantitative division, nor intellectual division arising from genus and differentia, nor intellectual division arising from matter and form, because everything divisible needs its part, and its part is other than it, so everything divisible is in need of something other than it, and everything in need of something other than it is possible by virtue of itself, so it follows that everything that is
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Three, which narrates a proof for the immateriality (tajarrud) of the soul. The argument begins by asserting that there exist indivisible existents in reality. If so, then the knowledge of such existents must also be indivisible. Consequently, the subject that possesses that knowledge (the soul) must be indivisible. Since everything that occupies space (mutaḥayyiz) or inheres in space is divisible, the possessor of indivisible knowledge can neither occupy space nor inhere in it—thus it is immaterial. The author then notes that this proof rests on four premises. He begins the first premise, which aims to demonstrate that there indeed are indivisible existents. The first evidence for this is the Necessary Existent (Wājib al-wujūd), i.e., God, who is indivisible in every way: not quantitatively, not conceptually via genus and differentia, and not in terms of matter and form. The reasoning is that anything divisible is dependent on its parts, which are other than itself, and thus it is contingent (mumkin). Since the Necessary Existent cannot be contingent, It cannot be divisible. The passage is cut off here, but the argument is that the existence of the Necessary Existent (agreed upon by the opponent) proves the existence of an indivisible existent, which then supports the overall proof for the soul's immateriality.

واجب الوجود (Wājib al-wujūd)
متحيز (mutaḥayyiz)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes without evidence that indivisible existents exist.
Correction: The author immediately provides evidence: the Necessary Existent (God) is indivisible, as argued from His necessity.

Checkpoint

A) The existence of atoms.
B) The existence of the Necessary Existent (Wājib al-wujūd).
C) The existence of the soul itself.
D) The existence of abstract concepts.

Key Takeaway
The first step in the proof for the soul's immateriality is establishing the existence of indivisible entities, beginning with the Necessary Existent's indivisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The proof shows that the Necessary Existent is completely independent, while your soul is contingent. When was the last time you genuinely felt your utter need for God in your heart? Set a specific time today to consciously acknowledge your contingency before Him.
?
You claim to know God, the ultimate Indivisible. But do you really know Him if you compartmentalize your faith, treating it as one part of your life? Commit to breaking down one such compartment this week.

Four Proofs for Indivisible Entities
Razi enumerates four proofs for the existence of indivisible existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four rational arguments that demonstrate the existence of indivisible entities such as atoms and points.

Core Idea
In a world of compounds, indivisible simples must exist.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, a master of kalam, presents four proofs that indivisible entities—such as the atom or the point—are not just conceptual but real. These arguments use different starting points: possibility, geometry, unity, and essence. They together build a formidable case for the existence of simples in reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first proof: the necessary simple
Read the first proof which argues that anything possible in itself must be free from composition, hence a pure individual.
This proof relies on the concept of possibility and composition.
2
Analyze the second proof: the indivisible point
Examine the geometric argument that a point, as the end of a line, cannot be divided without ceasing to be an end.
Consider why a divided point would not be an end.
3
Examine the third proof: unity vs multiplicity
Follow the logical argument that multiplicity presupposes unity, so unity must exist in any case.
This is a transcendental argument from the existence of multiplicity.
4
Consider the fourth proof: from composite to simple
Trace the reasoning that essences are either simple or composite; if composite, they require simples, so simples must exist.
This covers all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممكناً لذاته ، فإنه يكون منزهاً عن التأليف والتركيب ، فيكون فرداً محضاً الثاني : إن النقطة يمتنع كونها منقسمة . وقد ذكرنا في المسألة المشتملة على إثبات الجوهر الفرد : وجوهاً كثيرة في تقرير هذا المطلوب . ومن جملتها : أن النقطة نهاية للخط ، فلو كانت النقطة منقسمة ، لم يكن كل واحد من نصفها نهاية للخط . وهذا يلزمه أن كل ما كان نهاية للخط ، يمتنع أن يكون منقسماً . الثالث : إن الوحدة أبعد الأشياء عن طبائع الكثرة . فالوحدة إن كانت موجودة فهو المطلوب ، وإن لم تكن موجودة كانت الكثرة موجودة لكن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات فمتى كانت الكثرة موجودة كانت الوحدة موجودة فثبت : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود الوحدة على كل حال . الرابع : لا شك أن في الوجود ماهيات موجودة ، فهذه الماهيات إما أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة . فإن كانت بسيطة مفردة فهو المطلوب وإن كانت مركبة وكل مركب فإنما يتركب عن البسائط . فعلى جميع التقديرات لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود ماهية بسيطة مفردة عن جميع جهات التركيب . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة : أنه قد حصل في الوجود موجودات لا تقبل القسمة .
The first: something possible in itself, then it is free from composition and synthesis, so it is a pure individual. The second: the point cannot be divided. We have mentioned in the question that includes the proof of the indivisible atom many ways in establishing this required point. Among them: the point is the end of a line; if the point were divided, then not each one of its halves would be the end of the line. This entails that everything that is an end of a line cannot be divided. The third: unity is the furthest thing from the natures of multiplicity. If unity exists, then that is the required thing; and if it does not exist, then multiplicity exists, but multiplicity is composed of units; so when multiplicity exists, unity exists. Thus it is established that one must acknowledge the existence of unity in any case. The fourth: there is no doubt that in existence there are existing essences; these essences are either simple or composite. If they are simple and individual, then that is the required thing; and if they are composite, and every composite is composed of simples, then in all scenarios one must acknowledge the existence of a simple essence free from all aspects of composition. Thus it is established by these four proofs that there exist in existence existents that do not admit division.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of four proofs for the existence of indivisible entities (al-mawjūdāt allatī lā taqbal al-qisma, i.e., atoms or simples). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, is demonstrating that such entities must exist in reality. 

The first proof begins by stating that something possible in itself (mumkinan li-dhātihi) must be free from composition (al-ta’līf wa al-tarkīb) and thus a pure individual (fardan maḥḍan). This argument implies that any composite requires parts that are prior, and the regress stops at a simple. 

The second proof concerns the point (al-nuqṭa). The point is defined as the end of a line; if it were divided, each half would not be the end, contradicting its nature. Therefore, the point is indivisible. 

The third proof deals with unity (al-waḥda). Unity is the opposite of multiplicity. If unity exists, the goal is achieved; if not, multiplicity exists, but multiplicity is composed of units, so unity must exist. This is a transcendental argument: the existence of multiplicity implies the existence of unity. 

The fourth proof examines essences (māhiyyāt) in existence. They are either simple (basīṭa) or composite (murakkaba). If simple, the required is present; if composite, they are composed of simples, so simples must exist. Thus, in all cases, a simple essence exists. 

The conclusion of these four proofs is that there are existents that do not admit division, which are the indivisible atoms or simple substances.

Key Terms
موجود لا يقبل القسمة (indivisible existent)
نقطة (point)
وحدة (unity)
ماهية بسيطة (simple essence)
فرد محض (pure individual)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indivisible entities are merely conceptual abstractions.
Correction: Razi argues that indivisibility is a real property of actual existents, as shown by the four proofs.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the geometric concept of a point as the end of a line?
A) The first proof
B) The second proof
C) The third proof
D) The fourth proof

Key Takeaway
The existence of indivisible entities is proven by arguments from possibility, geometry, unity, and essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is a composite of body, mind, and spirit. If every composite must be built from simples, what is the simple core within you that remains indivisible? How does acknowledging that core change the way you approach your daily distractions?
?
The third proof shows that unity must exist because multiplicity cannot stand alone. In your life, you pursue many goals and desires. What is the one ultimate purpose that unifies all your pursuits? Are you living as if that purpose is truly indivisible and constant?
?
Razi argues that simple essences are necessary. Do you treat your own essence—your soul—as something simple and valuable, or do you let external circumstances divide your attention and dilute your identity? What concrete step can you take this week to protect the unity of your inner self?

The Indivisible Knowledge Requires an Indivisible Subject
Proof by trilemma that an indivisible knowledge implies an indivisible subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that an indivisible attribute (knowledge) cannot inhere in a divisible subject, and why all three possibilities in the argument lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Indivisible knowledge cannot be split across parts; its subject must be indivisible too.

In this passage, the author establishes a crucial premise for arguments about the attributes of God: if a knowledge is indivisible (like God's knowledge), then the thing described by that knowledge must also be indivisible. This may seem abstract, but it has direct implications for how we understand God's essence and attributes. The proof proceeds by assuming the opposite and showing it leads to impossible consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Notice the clear structure: statement of premise, then 'فنقول' introducing the proof.
The proof uses a trilemma (three options) to show that division leads to contradiction.
2
Identify the trilemma
The author presents three possibilities for each part of a divisible subject: (1) it has all the knowledge, (2) part of it, or (3) none. Examine each one.
These are exhaustive and mutually exclusive – they cover every possibility.
3
Understand why each option is impossible
Option 1: impossible because a single accident cannot be in multiple loci. Option 2: impossible because it would make the knowledge divisible. Option 3: impossible because the whole subject would then lack the knowledge.
The contradiction in each case shows the original assumption (divisible subject) is false.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all three lead to contradiction, the subject must be indivisible. Grasp how this proof protects the unity of God's knowledge and essence.
This premise is foundational for later arguments about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في بيان أنه متى كان العلم غير منقسم كان الموصوف بذلك العلم غير منقسم : فنقول : الذي يدل عليه : أن ذلك الموصوف ، إذا كان منقسما ، افترض فيه أجزاء وأقسام . وكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ، إما أن يكون موصوفا بكل ذلك العلم ، أو بجزء من أجزائه ، أو لا بكله ولا ببعضه . والأول : محال ، وإلا لزم حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة . والثاني : محال ، وإلا لزم أن يكون ذلك العلم منقسماً . وقد فرضناه غير منقسم ، خلف . والثالث : محال ، لأنه إذا كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل : خالياً عن ذلك العلم وعن جميع أجزائه يلزم كون ذلك المحل خالياً عن كل ذلك العلم مع أنا فرضناه موصوفاً به . هذا خلف.
The third premise concerning the explanation that whenever knowledge is indivisible, the subject described by that knowledge is indivisible. So we say: what indicates it is that that subject, if it is divisible, parts and divisions are assumed in it. And each one of those parts is either described by all of that knowledge, or by a part of its parts, or neither by all nor by some. The first is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate the inherence of a single accident in multiple loci. The second is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate that that knowledge is divisible, while we have assumed it indivisible, a contradiction. And the third is impossible, because if each one of the parts of that locus is devoid of that knowledge and of all its parts, it would necessitate that that locus is devoid of all of that knowledge, while we have assumed it described by it. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is the statement and proof of the third premise (al-muqaddima al-thalitha) in a larger argument. The premise is: if knowledge (al-ilm) is indivisible (ghayr munqasim), then the subject (al-mawsuf) that possesses that knowledge must also be indivisible. The proof proceeds by assuming the opposite: let the subject be divisible. Then it has parts. For each part, there are three possibilities: (1) it is described by the entire knowledge; (2) it is described by a part of the knowledge; (3) it is described by neither the whole nor a part. The first is impossible because it would require one accident to inhere in multiple loci, which is impossible. The second is impossible because it would imply that the knowledge is divisible, contradicting the assumption. The third is impossible because if all parts are devoid of the knowledge, the whole subject would be devoid of it, contradicting the assumption that it is described by the knowledge. Since all three possibilities lead to contradiction, the original assumption that the subject is divisible is false. Hence the subject must be indivisible.

Key Terms
مقدمة ثالثة
علم
موصوف
محل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an indivisible quality could be distributed over divisible parts (e.g., the same knowledge existing in each part).
Correction: The proof shows that distributing an indivisible knowledge over parts leads to three contradictions: it either implies one accident in multiple loci, divides the knowledge, or leaves the subject without knowledge. Hence it is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what is the second possibility examined for each part of a divisible subject?
A) The part is described by all of the knowledge.
B) The part is described by a part of the knowledge.
C) The part is described by neither all nor some of the knowledge.
D) The part is described by the opposite of the knowledge.

Key Takeaway
For an indivisible attribute like knowledge to inhere in a subject, that subject must itself be indivisible; otherwise, logical contradictions arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have knowledge of Allah (tawḥīd). But does that knowledge truly unify your heart and actions, or do you compartmentalize it—acting as if some areas of your life are outside His command? What part of your life have you left devoid of that knowledge, and will you repent tonight?
?
The proof shows that an indivisible attribute cannot be split across parts. Your faith (īmān) is a single, indivisible reality. Have you allowed doubt to break it into pieces, believing in some aspects while ignoring others? Which 'part' of your faith have you rejected, and when will you restore the whole?

The Locus of Sciences: A Non-Corporeal Substance
The author completes the proof by answering the second question, stating the fourth premise, and drawing the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth premise and conclusion of the proof that the locus of sciences is a non-corporeal substance.

Core Idea
The soul's knowledge cannot reside in a divisible body; it requires a non-corporeal substance.

The author has been building a proof that the locus of human sciences (the soul) is not a body or anything corporeal. In this passage, he completes the argument by stating the critical fourth premise: every extended thing is divisible. This leads to the conclusion that the locus must be a non-material substance. The argument hinges on negating the existence of indivisible atoms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage concludes a proof. The author has already established earlier premises. Now he addresses a second objection and states the fourth premise.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage twice: once for sound, once for meaning. Then read the English translation to ensure full comprehension.
Pay attention to the phrase 'العلم البديهي' (immediate knowledge).
3
Identify the fourth premise
Find the sentence that starts with 'وأما المقدمة الرابعة'. This is the fourth premise: every extended thing is divisible.
The author notes this premise depends on negating the indivisible atom.
4
Trace the conclusion
The conclusion is that the locus of sciences is a substance neither a body nor corporeal. See how it follows from the premises.
This is the 'matalib' (desired conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محال . ولما لزم المحال ، علمنا : أنه إنما لزم لا من هذا الغرض ، وإنما لزم من قيام الصفة التي لا تقبل القسمة بالمحل، الذي يقبل القسمة ، فوجب أن يكون هذا الغرض محالاً وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إنا ندعي العلم البديهي بأنه متى كان جميع أجزاء الذات خالياً عن كل الصفة، وعن كل واحد من أجزاء تلك الصفة ، امتنع بعد كون تلك الذات موصوفة بتلك الصفة . وأما المقدمة الرابعة : فهق في بيان أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم . وهذا بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد . والكلام فيه معلوم . وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمات الأربعة، ظهر أن محل هذه العلوم جوهر ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني . وهو المطلوب
Impossible. And when the impossible occurred, we knew that it occurred not from this purpose, but rather from the subsistence of the attribute that does not accept division in the locus that accepts division. So it is necessary that this purpose be impossible. As for the second question, its answer is: We claim immediate knowledge that when all parts of the essence are devoid of the entire attribute and of every single part of that attribute, it is impossible for that essence to be described by that attribute. As for the fourth premise, it is a statement that every extended thing is divisible. This is based on negating the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). And the discussion about it is known. And when these four premises become apparent, it becomes apparent that the locus of these sciences is a substance that is neither a body nor a corporeal. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the completion of a proof. The author first addresses the impossibility that would arise from the attribute inhering in a divisible locus, showing that the purpose (al-gharad) is impossible. Then he answers a second question by claiming immediate knowledge (al-ilm al-badihi) that if the essence is completely devoid of an attribute and its parts, it cannot become described by it afterwards. Next, he states the fourth premise: every extended thing (mutahayyiz) is divisible, which relies on negating the indivisible atom (nafy al-jawhar al-fard). Finally, he concludes that from these four premises, the locus of sciences (mahall hadhihi al-ulum) is a substance (jawhar) that is neither a body nor a corporeal being (la bi-jism wa la bi-jismani).

كل متحيز فهو منقسم
نفي الجوهر الفرد
جوهر ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof does not depend on the atom dispute.
Correction: The fourth premise explicitly relies on negating the indivisible atom, so the proof does depend on rejecting atomism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fourth premise of the proof?
A) The locus of sciences is a substance.
B) Every extended thing is divisible.
C) The indivisible atom exists.
D) The accident cannot inhere in a divisible.

Key Takeaway
The soul's knowledge cannot reside in a divisible body; it must be a non-corporeal substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge and consciousness cannot be located in any physical part of your body, what does that imply about the true nature of your 'self'? Do you live as if you are merely a body, or do you recognize your soul as a non-corporeal substance?
?
The proof asserts that an indivisible attribute cannot inhere in a divisible locus. Your acts of knowledge and awareness are indivisible. How does this challenge you to reconsider the materialist assumptions you might unconsciously hold?
?
The author relies on immediate knowledge (al-ilm al-badihi) that if a thing is completely devoid of an attribute, it cannot later acquire it. Apply this to your own spiritual state: are you relying on an external change to bring you closer to God, or do you recognize that the locus of your soul already has the potential for knowledge and faith?

First Objection: Proof Depends on Negating the Indivisible Atom
First objection: proof relies on denying indivisible atom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection challenges the proof's foundation by pointing to its reliance on a disputed premise, the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
A proof built on a controversial premise cannot claim universal acceptance.

This passage presents the first of several objections to the proof. The objection attacks the very foundation by asserting that the proof depends on rejecting the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), a doctrine that is itself disputed among scholars. Understanding this objection is crucial for evaluating the strength of the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the concise style: the objection is stated briefly but carries a heavy implication.
The phrase 'والكلام فيه معلوم' indicates the controversy is well-known.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection claims the entire proof is built upon negating the indivisible atom. Underline what 'نفي الجوهر الفرد' means.
3
Understand the implication
If the proof relies on a premise that is not universally accepted, then the proof itself is not decisive. Ask yourself: Why would building on a disputed premise weaken the argument?
This is a standard technique in polemics: show that the argument depends on something debatable.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might dismiss this objection as a minor technicality, but it actually strikes at the proof's foundation. Consider how a proof can be valid only if its premises are accepted by all parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : الإعتراض على هذا الدليل من وجوه : الأول : إن هذا الدليل بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد والكلام فيه معلوم
Then we say: The objections to this proof are in several aspects: The first: This proof is based on negating the indivisible atom, and the discussion about it is known.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage introduces the objections to the proof. The first objection is that the entire proof rests on negating the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is a controversial topic. The phrase 'والكلام فيه معلوم' indicates that the debate over the atom is well-known among scholars, implying that building a proof on such a disputed premise weakens the argument.

Key Terms
الإعتراض
نفي الجوهر الفرد
الكلام فيه معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is just a minor technicality and does not affect the proof's strength.
Correction: The objection attacks the foundation: if the proof depends on a disputed premise (negation of the indivisible atom), then the proof is only valid for those who accept that premise. This is a serious challenge, not a trivial point.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection to the proof?
A) The proof assumes the existence of the indivisible atom.
B) The proof denies the indivisible atom.
C) The proof is invalid because the fourth premise is impossible.
D) The proof uses an unsound logical form.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that the proof's reliance on denying the indivisible atom – a controversial doctrine – weakens its claim to universal validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself building your faith on arguments that rely on assumptions you haven't fully examined? How can you ensure your beliefs are grounded in what is certain, not just in disputed premises?
?
When you encounter an objection to a belief you hold, do you dismiss it as minor, or do you honestly consider whether its premise is weak? What assumption about your own faith might be the 'indivisible atom' that you never question?
?
The phrase 'والكلام فيه معلوم' reminds us that some debates are well-known. Are there places where you accept a conclusion without being aware of the controversy surrounding its foundation? What would it take for you to honestly revisit those foundations?

The Point Argument for Divisibility of States
Objector's first proof using the point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses the concept of a point to argue that a state in a divisible must be divisible.

Core Idea
The point is indivisible, leading to a dilemma that refutes the opponent's proof.

The objector begins by challenging the claim that a state in a divisible is itself divisible. He presents a proof based on the nature of a point—an indivisible referent. By analyzing whether the point is a substance or an accident, he shows that both options lead to contradictions of the original proof, thus invalidating it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and proof
Read the passage from 'والثاني : لم قلتم' to 'يبطل دليلكم بالكلية'. Notice how the objector sets up the dilemma.
2
Identify the two options
The point is either a substance or an accident. Trace the consequences of each option.
Both lead to the indivisible atom or an indivisible in a divisible locus.
3
Understand the conclusion
The objector concludes that the original proof is entirely invalid on both suppositions.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : لم قلتم : إن الحال في المنقسم : منقسم . والذي يدل على صحته وجوه (۲) : الحجة الأولى (۳) : إن النقطة شيء مشار إليه ، لا يقبل القسمة . فهو إما ان يكون جوهراً ، وإما أن يكون عرضاً . فإن كان جوهراً فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد ، وبطل هذا الدليل . وإن كان عرضاً ، عاد الكلام فيه ، ويلزم إما إثبات الجوهر الفرد ، وإما حلول ما لا ينقسم في محل منقسم . وعلى كلا التقديرين ، يبطل دليلكم بالكلية فإن قالوا : الكلام فيه من وجوه : الأول : إن النقطة وجودها في الوهم ، لا في الأعيان . الثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الخط عبارة عن عدمه وفنائه ، وذلك عدم محض . الثالث : إن النقطة طرف ، والطرف لا يكون سارياً في ذي الطرف ، ولا شائعاً فيه . وأما العلم فبتقدير أن يكون صفة للجسم ، فإنه يجب أن يكون سارياً فيه . لأنه [ يلزم عليه (۲) ] إن لم يكن سارياً فيه ، كان إما طرفاً ، وإما حالاً في الطرف. لا جائز أن يكون طرفاً . لأن الطرف هو النقطة . والعلم ليس بنقطة . ولا جائز أن يكون حالاً في الطرف ، الذي هو النقطة . لوجهين : الأول: إن النقطة موجود لا يقبل القسمة. فيمتنع أن تختلف أحواله بحسب اختلاف أحوال المزاج. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : طبيعة النقطة [ إن كانت (۳) ] مستعدة لقبول العلم ، فوجب أن تكون كل نقطة موصوفة بالعلم ، لأن الفيض عام ، فإن كان القابل مستعداً ، وجب عموم الفيض. الثاني : إن النقطة طرف الخط ، فلا يحل فيه طرف ما كان حاصلا في الطرف (1) حتى يكون الحال في مقابلة المحل، ويكون طرف الحال في مقابلة طرف المحل . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : ( النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم ،
And the second: Why do you say that the state (al-hal) in the divisible is divisible? And what indicates its correctness are aspects (2): The first proof (3): The point (al-nokta) is something referable, which does not accept division. So it is either a substance or an accident. For if it is a substance, then the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is established, and this proof is invalid. And if it is an accident, then the discussion returns to it, and either the establishment of the indivisible atom is necessary, or the indwelling of what does not divide in a divisible locus. And on both suppositions, your proof is invalid entirely. Then they said: The discussion about it is in aspects: The first: The point has existence only in the imagination, not in external realities. The second: The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of the line is an expression for its non-existence and annihilation, and that is pure non-existence. The third: The point is a limit (taraf), and the limit does not pervade in that which has the limit, nor does it spread in it. And as for knowledge (al-ilm), on the assumption that it is an attribute of the body, it must be pervading in it. Because [it would follow] if it were not pervading in it, it would be either a limit or a state in the limit. It is not possible to be a limit, because the limit is the point, and knowledge is not a point. And it is not possible to be a state in the limit, which is the point, for two reasons: The first: The point is an existent that does not accept division. So it is impossible for its states to differ according to the differences of the temperament. And when this is established, we say: The nature of the point [if it were] disposed to accept knowledge, then every point must be described by knowledge, because the effusion (al-fayd) is general. For if the recipient is disposed, then the generality of effusion is necessary. The second: The point is the limit of the line, so a limit of what is in the limit does not indwell in it, such that the state is in corresponding to the locus, and the limit of the state is in corresponding to the limit of the locus. And the answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: "The point has no existence except in the imagination,"
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection in detail. The objector questions the claim that 'the state in a divisible is itself divisible'. To support this, he gives the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) using the concept of a point (al-nokta). The point is an indivisible referent. It must be either a substance or an accident. If a substance, then the indivisible atom exists, which the original proof denies, so the proof fails. If an accident, then either the atom is established or an indivisible accident inheres in a divisible locus, both contradicting the proof. Next, the objector entertains counterarguments from the opponent (فإن قالوا) who raise three objections: 1) The point exists only in the mind, not externally. 2) The point is merely the end of a line, which is non-existence. 3) The point is a limit and does not pervade. The objector then applies this to knowledge (al-ilm) as an attribute of the body: knowledge must pervade; if not, it would be a point or in a point. Being a point is impossible because knowledge is not a point. Being in a point is impossible because points are uniform and cannot vary, so all points would have knowledge, which is false. Also, a point as a limit cannot contain another limit. The answer begins: 'As for the first question, which is his saying: The point has no existence except in the imagination...' but is cut off.

Key Terms
الحال في المنقسم
النقطة
الجوهر الفرد
حلول ما لا ينقسم في محل منقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point argument proves the existence of the indivisible atom.
Correction: The point argument is used as a reductio: showing that assuming the opponent's premise leads to the atom, which the opponent rejects, thus refuting the premise.

Checkpoint
In the objector's first proof, what are the two possibilities for the point?
A) It is a substance or an accident.
B) It is a body or a spirit.
C) It is divisible or indivisible.
D) It is mental or external.

Key Takeaway
The objector uses the point as an indivisible referent to create a dilemma that refutes the opponent's claim that a state in a divisible is divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that seems logically sound, are you open to the possibility that it could be refuted by a clever dilemma like this one? What would it take for you to abandon a cherished argument?
?
The objector refutes not by rejecting the opponent's premise but by accepting it and exposing its consequences. In your next disagreement, will you try to first fully accept the other's premise and see where it leads, rather than immediately attacking it?
?
This passage shows that even a seemingly simple concept like a point can unravel an entire proof. Do you have 'points' of certainty in your life that you have never questioned? What would happen if you submitted them to rigorous scrutiny?

Three Counterarguments: The Nature of the Point
Opponent's three counterarguments about the point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three responses the opponent gives to the point argument, questioning its reality and applicability.

Core Idea
The point is imaginary, nonexistent, or a non-pervading limit, undermining its use in the argument.

After the objector presents his proof using the point, the opponent offers three counterarguments. These challenge the point's existence in external reality, its ontological status as the end of a line (non-existence), and its nature as a limit that does not pervade. These responses aim to neutralize the point argument by questioning its foundational assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the three counterarguments
Read from 'فإن قالوا' to 'ولا شائعاً فيه'. Identify each of the three points.
2
Understand each objection
First: point exists only in imagination. Second: point is end of line, which is non-existence. Third: point is a limit, not pervading.
Each undermines a different aspect of the point's reality.
3
Connect to the overall argument
Realize that these counterarguments are meant to show that the point cannot serve as a valid counterexample to the original claim.
They target the point's existence and pervasiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : لم قلتم : إن الحال في المنقسم : منقسم . والذي يدل على صحته وجوه (۲) : الحجة الأولى (۳) : إن النقطة شيء مشار إليه ، لا يقبل القسمة . فهو إما ان يكون جوهراً ، وإما أن يكون عرضاً . فإن كان جوهراً فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد ، وبطل هذا الدليل . وإن كان عرضاً ، عاد الكلام فيه ، ويلزم إما إثبات الجوهر الفرد ، وإما حلول ما لا ينقسم في محل منقسم . وعلى كلا التقديرين ، يبطل دليلكم بالكلية فإن قالوا : الكلام فيه من وجوه : الأول : إن النقطة وجودها في الوهم ، لا في الأعيان . الثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الخط عبارة عن عدمه وفنائه ، وذلك عدم محض . الثالث : إن النقطة طرف ، والطرف لا يكون سارياً في ذي الطرف ، ولا شائعاً فيه . وأما العلم فبتقدير أن يكون صفة للجسم ، فإنه يجب أن يكون سارياً فيه . لأنه [ يلزم عليه (۲) ] إن لم يكن سارياً فيه ، كان إما طرفاً ، وإما حالاً في الطرف. لا جائز أن يكون طرفاً . لأن الطرف هو النقطة . والعلم ليس بنقطة . ولا جائز أن يكون حالاً في الطرف ، الذي هو النقطة . لوجهين : الأول: إن النقطة موجود لا يقبل القسمة. فيمتنع أن تختلف أحواله بحسب اختلاف أحوال المزاج. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : طبيعة النقطة [ إن كانت (۳) ] مستعدة لقبول العلم ، فوجب أن تكون كل نقطة موصوفة بالعلم ، لأن الفيض عام ، فإن كان القابل مستعداً ، وجب عموم الفيض. الثاني : إن النقطة طرف الخط ، فلا يحل فيه طرف ما كان حاصلا في الطرف (1) حتى يكون الحال في مقابلة المحل، ويكون طرف الحال في مقابلة طرف المحل . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : ( النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم ،
And the second: Why do you say that the state (al-hal) in the divisible is divisible? And what indicates its correctness are aspects (2): The first proof (3): The point (al-nokta) is something referable, which does not accept division. So it is either a substance or an accident. For if it is a substance, then the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is established, and this proof is invalid. And if it is an accident, then the discussion returns to it, and either the establishment of the indivisible atom is necessary, or the indwelling of what does not divide in a divisible locus. And on both suppositions, your proof is invalid entirely. Then they said: The discussion about it is in aspects: The first: The point has existence only in the imagination, not in external realities. The second: The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of the line is an expression for its non-existence and annihilation, and that is pure non-existence. The third: The point is a limit (taraf), and the limit does not pervade in that which has the limit, nor does it spread in it. And as for knowledge (al-ilm), on the assumption that it is an attribute of the body, it must be pervading in it. Because [it would follow] if it were not pervading in it, it would be either a limit or a state in the limit. It is not possible to be a limit, because the limit is the point, and knowledge is not a point. And it is not possible to be a state in the limit, which is the point, for two reasons: The first: The point is an existent that does not accept division. So it is impossible for its states to differ according to the differences of the temperament. And when this is established, we say: The nature of the point [if it were] disposed to accept knowledge, then every point must be described by knowledge, because the effusion (al-fayd) is general. For if the recipient is disposed, then the generality of effusion is necessary. The second: The point is the limit of the line, so a limit of what is in the limit does not indwell in it, such that the state is in corresponding to the locus, and the limit of the state is in corresponding to the limit of the locus. And the answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: "The point has no existence except in the imagination,"
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection in detail. The objector questions the claim that 'the state in a divisible is itself divisible'. To support this, he gives the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) using the concept of a point (al-nokta). The point is an indivisible referent. It must be either a substance or an accident. If a substance, then the indivisible atom exists, which the original proof denies, so the proof fails. If an accident, then either the atom is established or an indivisible accident inheres in a divisible locus, both contradicting the proof. Next, the objector entertains counterarguments from the opponent (فإن قالوا) who raise three objections: 1) The point exists only in the mind, not externally. 2) The point is merely the end of a line, which is non-existence. 3) The point is a limit and does not pervade. The objector then applies this to knowledge (al-ilm) as an attribute of the body: knowledge must pervade; if not, it would be a point or in a point. Being a point is impossible because knowledge is not a point. Being in a point is impossible because points are uniform and cannot vary, so all points would have knowledge, which is false. Also, a point as a limit cannot contain another limit. The answer begins: 'As for the first question, which is his saying: The point has no existence except in the imagination...' but is cut off.

Key Terms
النقطة
الوهم
الأعيان
العدم
الطرف
الساري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, what is the point's existence?
A) It exists in the imagination only.
B) It exists as a substance.
C) It exists as an accident.
D) It exists as both substance and accident.

Key Takeaway
The opponent offers three objections to the point argument: it is imaginary, non-existent, or a non-pervading limit, thus defusing its force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent says the point exists only in imagination. What in your own life do you treat as absolutely real, though it may exist only in your imagination or assumptions? How might that belief affect your relationship with Allah?
?
The third argument says a limit does not pervade its object. Are there ways you have limited Allah's attributes by human concepts, treating them like points that do not truly penetrate His essence? How can you repent from such anthropomorphism?
?
If the point is pure non-existence, consider a belief or action you hold that is based on 'nothing'—perhaps a habit or doubt. What is one concrete step you will take today to align your conviction with reality as taught by revelation?

Applying the Point Argument to Knowledge
Application of point argument to knowledge, with two reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector applies the point argument to knowledge (al-ilm) as an attribute of the body, and why knowledge cannot be a limit or in a limit.

Core Idea
Knowledge must pervade the body; it cannot be a point or reside in a point, due to uniformity and containment issues.

The objector now applies the point argument to the specific case of knowledge (al-ilm) as an attribute of the body. He argues that knowledge, being an attribute, must pervade the body. If it does not, it would have to be either a limit (like a point) or reside in a limit. Both options are impossible: knowledge cannot be a limit because it is not a point, and it cannot reside in a point because points are uniform and cannot vary, and a limit cannot contain another limit.

Guided Path
1
Read the application to knowledge
Read from 'وأما العلم' to 'طرف المحل'. Notice how the objector builds on the previous point discussion.
2
Understand the necessity of pervasion
Knowledge must be pervading (sariy) in the body. If not, it would be a limit or in a limit.
3
Examine the two reasons
First reason: points are uniform, so if one point could accept knowledge, all would, which is false. Second reason: a limit (point) cannot contain another limit.
These reasons show knowledge cannot be in a point.
4
See the beginning of the answer
The objector then starts to answer the opponent's first question, but the passage cuts off.
The answer is incomplete in this source.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can be localized in a point of the body.

?

?

?
The point, as a mere mental construct, cannot hold real knowledge. Have you ever reduced your deen to abstract concepts without letting them permeate your daily choices? What one step will you take to make your knowledge a living reality?

The First Objection: The Point Exists Only in Imagination
The first objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the point must exist in external reality despite objections that it is merely imaginary.

Core Idea
Points are real endpoints of real surfaces and lines.

Opponents of atomism often argue that a point is just a mental construct with no external reality. In this passage, the author refutes that claim by showing that physical bodies have actual boundaries—surfaces and lines—whose endpoints must themselves exist. Let’s see his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first objection: that the point exists only in the imagination, not in external reality.
This is a direct challenge to the existence of indivisible atoms.
2
Read the author's response
Now read how the author refutes it by tracing the chain from body to surface to line to point.
Notice the logical necessity: if the body is finite, its surface exists; if the surface is finite, its line exists; if the line is finite, its endpoint exists.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for kalam: if points exist, then indivisible atoms (جوهر فرد) are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الكلام فيه من وجوه : الأول : إن النقطة وجودها في الوهم ، لا في الأعيان الثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الخط عبارة عن عدمه وفنائه ، وذلك عدم محض . الثالث : إن النقطة طرف ، والطرف لا يكون سارياً في ذي الطرف ، ولا شائعاً فيه . وأما العلم فبتقدير أن يكون صفة للجسم ، فإنه يجب أن يكون سارياً فيه . لأنه [ يلزم عليه ] إن لم يكن سارياً فيه ، كان إما طرفاً ، وإما حالاً في الطرف. لا جائز أن يكون طرفاً . لأن الطرف هو النقطة . والعلم ليس بنقطة . ولا جائز أن يكون حالاً في الطرف ، الذي هو النقطة . لوجهين : الأول: إن النقطة موجود لا يقبل القسمة. فيمتنع أن تختلف أحواله بحسب اختلاف أحوال المزاج. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : طبيعة النقطة [ إن كانت ] مستعدة لقبول العلم ، فوجب أن تكون كل نقطة موصوفة بالعلم ، لأن الفيض عام ، فإن كان القابل مستعداً ، وجب عموم الفيض. الثاني : إن النقطة طرف الخط ، فلا يحل فيه طرف ما كان حاصلا في الطرف حتى يكون الحال في مقابلة المحل، ويكون طرف الحال في مقابلة طرف المحل . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : ( النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم ، فكلام باطل . لأن الجسم متناهي في الخارج، ونهايته هو السطح ، فوجب أن يكون السطح موجوداً في الخارج. والسطح متناه الخط ، ونهايته الخط ، والخط موجود في الخارج فنهايته موجودة في الخارج [ فالنقطة موجودة في الخارج ] وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الشيء عبارة عن عدمه » فنقول : إن الخط يلاقي بطرفه خطاً آخر ، فلو كانت النقطة عدماً ، لكان محل الملاقاة عدماً محضاً . وإنه باطل . وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : ( النقطة غير سارية في الجسم ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يكون العلم حالاً في المحل ، لا على وجه الشيوع. والسريان ؟ قوله : والعرض الذي لا يكون سارياً في الجسم : إما النقطة ، وإما الحال في النقطة ، قلنا : هذا الحصر ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز إثبات أعراض غير سارية في الجسم مع أنها لا تكون نقطة ولا حالة في النقطة ؟ وهذا الحصر ليس لهم في إثباته شبهة ، فضلاً عن حجة . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون محل هذه العلوم هو النقطة ؟ أما قوله أولاً : « إن النقطة يمتنع أن يختلف حالها في القبول وعدم القبول ، بسبب اختلاف الإستعدادات ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون شرط كون تلك التقطة قابلة للعلم المخصوص : حصول استعداد مخصوص في الجسم الذي تكون تلك النقطة حالة فيه ؟ وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائما ، سقط كلامكم وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إنه لا يحل في طرف المحل إلا طرف الحال قلنا : هذا تحكم ولا بد فيه من الدليل . فهذا تمام الكلام في سؤال النقطة
Then they said: The discussion about it has several aspects: First: The point has existence only in imagination, not in external reality. Second: The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of the line is an expression for its non-existence and extinction, and that is pure non-existence. Third: The point is an extremity, and the extremity cannot be pervading in that which has the extremity, nor diffused in it. As for knowledge, if it is supposed to be an attribute of the body, then it must be pervading in it, because it follows that if it is not pervading in it, it would be either an extremity or a state in the extremity. It cannot be an extremity, because the extremity is the point, and knowledge is not a point. And it cannot be a state in the extremity, which is the point, for two reasons: First: The point is an existent that does not accept division. So it is impossible for its states to differ according to differences in temperament. And if this is established, we say: If the nature of the point were disposed to accept knowledge, then every point would necessarily be described with knowledge, because the emanation is universal, and if the receiver is prepared, the universality of emanation is necessary. Second: The point is the extremity of the line, so the extremity of whatever is located in the extremity cannot reside in it such that the state is opposite the locus, and the extremity of the state is opposite the extremity of the locus. The answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: 'The point has no existence except in imagination' – this is false speech. Because the body is finite in external reality, and its end is the surface, so the surface must exist in external reality. And the surface is finite by the line, and its end is the line, and the line exists in external reality, so its end exists in external reality; thus the point exists in external reality. As for the second question, which is his saying: 'The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of a thing is an expression for its non-existence' – we say: The line meets another line at its extremity. So if the point were non-existence, the place of meeting would be pure non-existence, and that is false. As for the third question, which is his saying: 'The point is not pervading in the body' – we say: Why is it not also permissible that knowledge resides in the locus not in a manner of diffusion and pervasion? His saying: 'And the accident that is not pervading in the body is either the point or a state in the point' – we say: This enumeration is denied. So why is it not permissible to affirm accidents that are not pervading in the body while they are neither a point nor a state in the point? There is not for them, in affirming this enumeration, any doubt, let alone a proof. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the locus of these sciences is the point? As for his first saying: 'It is impossible for the state of the point to differ in acceptance and non-acceptance due to differences in dispositions' – we say: Why is it not permissible that the condition for that point to be receptive to specific knowledge is the occurrence of a specific disposition in the body in which that point resides? And if this possibility stands, your speech collapses. As for his second saying: 'Only the extremity of the state can reside in the extremity of the locus' – we say: This is arbitrary and requires proof. This completes the discussion on the question of the point.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised by opponents regarding the point (al-nuqta) and their refutation. The objections are part of a larger debate about whether an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) exists and whether accidents can inhere in it. The author first lists the three objections: (1) The point exists only in the imagination, not in external reality. (2) The point is merely the end of a line, which is non-existence. (3) The point is an extremity and cannot be pervading in the body; hence if knowledge were an attribute of the body, it would have to be pervading, but if it is not, it must be either a point or a state in a point, both impossible. The author answers each objection in turn. For the first, he argues that bodies are finite externally, so their surfaces must exist, and similarly lines and their endpoints—hence the point exists. For the second, he points out that lines meet at their extremities; if the point were non-existence, the meeting place would be nothing, which is absurd. For the third, he denies the opponent's strict disjunction: there can be accidents that are neither points nor states in points, and it is possible for knowledge to reside in a point if there is a specific disposition in the body. Thus the objections are invalid.

Key Terms
نقطة (point)
وهم (imagination)
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
سطح (surface)
خط (line)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the point has no external reality, existing only as a mental abstraction.
Correction: The author proves that points are real because they are the endpoints of physically existing surfaces and lines, which themselves arise from finite bodies.

Checkpoint
How does the author prove that the point exists in external reality?
A) By citing scripture.
B) By arguing that finite bodies have real boundaries, and the point is the endpoint of a line.
C) By saying it is obvious.
D) By appealing to consensus.

Key Takeaway
The point is not merely imaginary; it exists as the real endpoint of lines and surfaces in the physical world.
