The Second Objection: The Point Is the End of a Line, Hence Non-Existent
The second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the point cannot be pure non-existence, as shown by the meeting of lines.

Core Idea
Lines meet at real points; if points were nothing, meetings would be impossible.

The second objection claims that the point is merely the end of a line, which is like an extinction—pure non-being. The author rebuts this by appealing to the observable fact that lines meet at points. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the second objection: the point is only the end of a line, which is non-existence.
This seems plausible if we see endings as nothingness.
2
Read the author's response
Now read how the author argues: if two lines meet, their meeting point cannot be nothing, because then they would meet nowhere.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the implication
If points were non-existent, then all contact between physical things would be impossible, which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الكلام فيه من وجوه : الأول : إن النقطة وجودها في الوهم ، لا في الأعيان الثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الخط عبارة عن عدمه وفنائه ، وذلك عدم محض . الثالث : إن النقطة طرف ، والطرف لا يكون سارياً في ذي الطرف ، ولا شائعاً فيه . وأما العلم فبتقدير أن يكون صفة للجسم ، فإنه يجب أن يكون سارياً فيه . لأنه [ يلزم عليه ] إن لم يكن سارياً فيه ، كان إما طرفاً ، وإما حالاً في الطرف. لا جائز أن يكون طرفاً . لأن الطرف هو النقطة . والعلم ليس بنقطة . ولا جائز أن يكون حالاً في الطرف ، الذي هو النقطة . لوجهين : الأول: إن النقطة موجود لا يقبل القسمة. فيمتنع أن تختلف أحواله بحسب اختلاف أحوال المزاج. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : طبيعة النقطة [ إن كانت ] مستعدة لقبول العلم ، فوجب أن تكون كل نقطة موصوفة بالعلم ، لأن الفيض عام ، فإن كان القابل مستعداً ، وجب عموم الفيض. الثاني : إن النقطة طرف الخط ، فلا يحل فيه طرف ما كان حاصلا في الطرف حتى يكون الحال في مقابلة المحل، ويكون طرف الحال في مقابلة طرف المحل . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : ( النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم ، فكلام باطل . لأن الجسم متناهي في الخارج، ونهايته هو السطح ، فوجب أن يكون السطح موجوداً في الخارج. والسطح متناه الخط ، ونهايته الخط ، والخط موجود في الخارج فنهايته موجودة في الخارج [ فالنقطة موجودة في الخارج ] وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الشيء عبارة عن عدمه » فنقول : إن الخط يلاقي بطرفه خطاً آخر ، فلو كانت النقطة عدماً ، لكان محل الملاقاة عدماً محضاً . وإنه باطل . وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : ( النقطة غير سارية في الجسم ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يكون العلم حالاً في المحل ، لا على وجه الشيوع. والسريان ؟ قوله : والعرض الذي لا يكون سارياً في الجسم : إما النقطة ، وإما الحال في النقطة ، قلنا : هذا الحصر ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز إثبات أعراض غير سارية في الجسم مع أنها لا تكون نقطة ولا حالة في النقطة ؟ وهذا الحصر ليس لهم في إثباته شبهة ، فضلاً عن حجة . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون محل هذه العلوم هو النقطة ؟ أما قوله أولاً : « إن النقطة يمتنع أن يختلف حالها في القبول وعدم القبول ، بسبب اختلاف الإستعدادات ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون شرط كون تلك التقطة قابلة للعلم المخصوص : حصول استعداد مخصوص في الجسم الذي تكون تلك النقطة حالة فيه ؟ وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائما ، سقط كلامكم وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إنه لا يحل في طرف المحل إلا طرف الحال قلنا : هذا تحكم ولا بد فيه من الدليل . فهذا تمام الكلام في سؤال النقطة
Then they said: The discussion about it has several aspects: First: The point has existence only in imagination, not in external reality. Second: The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of the line is an expression for its non-existence and extinction, and that is pure non-existence. Third: The point is an extremity, and the extremity cannot be pervading in that which has the extremity, nor diffused in it. As for knowledge, if it is supposed to be an attribute of the body, then it must be pervading in it, because it follows that if it is not pervading in it, it would be either an extremity or a state in the extremity. It cannot be an extremity, because the extremity is the point, and knowledge is not a point. And it cannot be a state in the extremity, which is the point, for two reasons: First: The point is an existent that does not accept division. So it is impossible for its states to differ according to differences in temperament. And if this is established, we say: If the nature of the point were disposed to accept knowledge, then every point would necessarily be described with knowledge, because the emanation is universal, and if the receiver is prepared, the universality of emanation is necessary. Second: The point is the extremity of the line, so the extremity of whatever is located in the extremity cannot reside in it such that the state is opposite the locus, and the extremity of the state is opposite the extremity of the locus. The answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: 'The point has no existence except in imagination' – this is false speech. Because the body is finite in external reality, and its end is the surface, so the surface must exist in external reality. And the surface is finite by the line, and its end is the line, and the line exists in external reality, so its end exists in external reality; thus the point exists in external reality. As for the second question, which is his saying: 'The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of a thing is an expression for its non-existence' – we say: The line meets another line at its extremity. So if the point were non-existence, the place of meeting would be pure non-existence, and that is false. As for the third question, which is his saying: 'The point is not pervading in the body' – we say: Why is it not also permissible that knowledge resides in the locus not in a manner of diffusion and pervasion? His saying: 'And the accident that is not pervading in the body is either the point or a state in the point' – we say: This enumeration is denied. So why is it not permissible to affirm accidents that are not pervading in the body while they are neither a point nor a state in the point? There is not for them, in affirming this enumeration, any doubt, let alone a proof. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the locus of these sciences is the point? As for his first saying: 'It is impossible for the state of the point to differ in acceptance and non-acceptance due to differences in dispositions' – we say: Why is it not permissible that the condition for that point to be receptive to specific knowledge is the occurrence of a specific disposition in the body in which that point resides? And if this possibility stands, your speech collapses. As for his second saying: 'Only the extremity of the state can reside in the extremity of the locus' – we say: This is arbitrary and requires proof. This completes the discussion on the question of the point.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised by opponents regarding the point (al-nuqta) and their refutation. The objections are part of a larger debate about whether an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) exists and whether accidents can inhere in it. The author first lists the three objections: (1) The point exists only in the imagination, not in external reality. (2) The point is merely the end of a line, which is non-existence. (3) The point is an extremity and cannot be pervading in the body; hence if knowledge were an attribute of the body, it would have to be pervading, but if it is not, it must be either a point or a state in a point, both impossible. The author answers each objection in turn. For the first, he argues that bodies are finite externally, so their surfaces must exist, and similarly lines and their endpoints—hence the point exists. For the second, he points out that lines meet at their extremities; if the point were non-existence, the meeting place would be nothing, which is absurd. For the third, he denies the opponent's strict disjunction: there can be accidents that are neither points nor states in points, and it is possible for knowledge to reside in a point if there is a specific disposition in the body. Thus the objections are invalid.

Key Terms
نقطة (point)
خط (line)
عدم (non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because a point has zero dimension, it must be nothing at all.
Correction: The author shows that even dimensionless entities can be real (as endpoints) and that their reality is necessary for contact and meeting.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the objection that the point is non-existence?
A) Points are necessary for division.
B) Lines meet at points, so points must exist for meeting to occur.
C) The Quran says points exist.
D) Points are made of substance.

Key Takeaway
Lines meet at points, proving that points have real existence despite lacking dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own beliefs about what is 'real,' do you require that things be physically measurable or extended? This lesson shows that a point is real yet has no dimension. What non-material realities have you been dismissing because they lack physical extension? How might that change your willingness to accept the unseen?
?
The objection in this lesson assumed that if something has no dimension, it does not exist. Have you ever assumed that something is unreal simply because it is not tangible? What in your life—like your soul, your thoughts, or your relationship with Allah—have you treated as less real because it cannot be quantified? How does this argument call you to re-evaluate that?
?
In your own life, do you act as if only the material world is truly real? How would your priorities shift if you genuinely believed that non-extended realities (like the point in this lesson) are real and have effects? What one change can you make today to take the reality of the unseen more seriously?

The Third Objection: Why Knowledge Cannot Reside in a Point (Part 1)
The third objection's main argument and first reason with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that knowledge cannot be an accident in a point due to the point's indivisibility, and the author's response regarding dispositions.

Core Idea
A point's indivisibility does not prevent it from receiving knowledge if conditions vary.

The third objection is more complex: since a point cannot be pervaded by accident, knowledge (as an accident) must either be a point (impossible) or reside in a point (also problematic). The first reason given is that the point is indivisible, so its states cannot differ. The author counters by invoking the concept of dispositions (isti'dad) in the body. Let's follow this intricate debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's opening
Read the beginning of the third objection: the point is an extremity, not pervading, so knowledge must be either a point or in a point.
2
Read the first reason
The first reason why knowledge cannot be in a point: points are indivisible, so they cannot have varying states.
This is a problem for knowledge, which varies with temperament.
3
Study the author's reply
The author argues that a point can receive specific knowledge if the body it resides in has a specific disposition.
This preserves the possibility of variation.

The Third Objection: Why Knowledge Cannot Reside in a Point (Part 2)
The second reason of the third objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second reason given against knowledge residing in a point (only extremity of state fits extremity of locus) and how the author dismisses it as arbitrary.

Core Idea
The claim that only the extremity of an accident can reside in an extremity is an arbitrary assertion without proof.

The opponent's second reason is that a point, being the extremity of a line, can only contain the extremity of an accident, not the accident itself. The author rejects this as mere stipulation. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason
Read the opponent's second reason: only the extremity of a state can reside in the extremity of a locus.
This is a geometric analogy.
2
Read the author's response
The author says this is arbitrary and requires proof.
He demands a demonstration, not just an assertion.
3
Reflect on the method
Notice how the author consistently demands evidence for claims, a hallmark of kalam reasoning.

لا جائز أن يكون طرفاً . لأن الطرف هو النقطة . والعلم ليس بنقطة . ولا جائز أن يكون حالاً في الطرف ، الذي هو النقطة . لوجهين : الأول: إن النقطة موجود لا يقبل القسمة. فيمتنع أن تختلف أحواله بحسب اختلاف أحوال المزاج. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : طبيعة النقطة [ إن كانت ] مستعدة لقبول العلم ، فوجب أن تكون كل نقطة موصوفة بالعلم ، لأن الفيض عام ، فإن كان القابل مستعداً ، وجب عموم الفيض. الثاني : إن النقطة طرف الخط ، فلا يحل فيه طرف ما كان حاصلا في الطرف حتى يكون الحال في مقابلة المحل، ويكون طرف الحال في مقابلة طرف المحل . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : ( النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم ، فكلام باطل . لأن الجسم متناهي في الخارج، ونهايته هو السطح ، فوجب أن يكون السطح موجوداً في الخارج. والسطح متناه الخط ، ونهايته الخط ، والخط موجود في الخارج فنهايته موجودة في الخارج [ فالنقطة موجودة في الخارج ] وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( النقطة عبارة عن نهاية الخط ، ونهاية الشيء عبارة عن عدمه » فنقول : إن الخط يلاقي بطرفه خطاً آخر ، فلو كانت النقطة عدماً ، لكان محل الملاقاة عدماً محضاً . وإنه باطل . وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : ( النقطة غير سارية في الجسم ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يكون العلم حالاً في المحل ، لا على وجه الشيوع. والسريان ؟ قوله : والعرض الذي لا يكون سارياً في الجسم : إما النقطة ، وإما الحال في النقطة ، قلنا : هذا الحصر ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز إثبات أعراض غير سارية في الجسم مع أنها لا تكون نقطة ولا حالة في النقطة ؟ وهذا الحصر ليس لهم في إثباته شبهة ، فضلاً عن حجة . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون محل هذه العلوم هو النقطة ؟ أما قوله أولاً : « إن النقطة يمتنع أن يختلف حالها في القبول وعدم القبول ، بسبب اختلاف الإستعدادات ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون شرط كون تلك التقطة قابلة للعلم المخصوص : حصول استعداد مخصوص في الجسم الذي تكون تلك النقطة حالة فيه ؟ وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائما ، سقط كلامكم وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إنه لا يحل في طرف المحل إلا طرف الحال قلنا : هذا تحكم ولا بد فيه من الدليل . فهذا تمام الكلام في سؤال النقطة
Then they said: The discussion about it has several aspects: First: The point has existence only in imagination, not in external reality. Second: The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of the line is an expression for its non-existence and extinction, and that is pure non-existence. Third: The point is an extremity, and the extremity cannot be pervading in that which has the extremity, nor diffused in it. As for knowledge, if it is supposed to be an attribute of the body, then it must be pervading in it, because it follows that if it is not pervading in it, it would be either an extremity or a state in the extremity. It cannot be an extremity, because the extremity is the point, and knowledge is not a point. And it cannot be a state in the extremity, which is the point, for two reasons: First: The point is an existent that does not accept division. So it is impossible for its states to differ according to differences in temperament. And if this is established, we say: If the nature of the point were disposed to accept knowledge, then every point would necessarily be described with knowledge, because the emanation is universal, and if the receiver is prepared, the universality of emanation is necessary. Second: The point is the extremity of the line, so the extremity of whatever is located in the extremity cannot reside in it such that the state is opposite the locus, and the extremity of the state is opposite the extremity of the locus. The answer: As for the first question, which is his saying: 'The point has no existence except in imagination' – this is false speech. Because the body is finite in external reality, and its end is the surface, so the surface must exist in external reality. And the surface is finite by the line, and its end is the line, and the line exists in external reality, so its end exists in external reality; thus the point exists in external reality. As for the second question, which is his saying: 'The point is an expression for the end of the line, and the end of a thing is an expression for its non-existence' – we say: The line meets another line at its extremity. So if the point were non-existence, the place of meeting would be pure non-existence, and that is false. As for the third question, which is his saying: 'The point is not pervading in the body' – we say: Why is it not also permissible that knowledge resides in the locus not in a manner of diffusion and pervasion? His saying: 'And the accident that is not pervading in the body is either the point or a state in the point' – we say: This enumeration is denied. So why is it not permissible to affirm accidents that are not pervading in the body while they are neither a point nor a state in the point? There is not for them, in affirming this enumeration, any doubt, let alone a proof. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the locus of these sciences is the point? As for his first saying: 'It is impossible for the state of the point to differ in acceptance and non-acceptance due to differences in dispositions' – we say: Why is it not permissible that the condition for that point to be receptive to specific knowledge is the occurrence of a specific disposition in the body in which that point resides? And if this possibility stands, your speech collapses. As for his second saying: 'Only the extremity of the state can reside in the extremity of the locus' – we say: This is arbitrary and requires proof. This completes the discussion on the question of the point.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised by opponents regarding the point (al-nuqta) and their refutation. The objections are part of a larger debate about whether an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) exists and whether accidents can inhere in it. The author first lists the three objections: (1) The point exists only in the imagination, not in external reality. (2) The point is merely the end of a line, which is non-existence. (3) The point is an extremity and cannot be pervading in the body; hence if knowledge were an attribute of the body, it would have to be pervading, but if it is not, it must be either a point or a state in a point, both impossible. The author answers each objection in turn. For the first, he argues that bodies are finite externally, so their surfaces must exist, and similarly lines and their endpoints—hence the point exists. For the second, he points out that lines meet at their extremities; if the point were non-existence, the meeting place would be nothing, which is absurd. For the third, he denies the opponent's strict disjunction: there can be accidents that are neither points nor states in points, and it is possible for knowledge to reside in a point if there is a specific disposition in the body. Thus the objections are invalid.

Key Terms
نقطة (point)
طرف (extremity)
حال (state)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the opponent's second reason that only the extremity of an accident can reside in a point?
A) He agrees with it.
B) He says it is arbitrary and lacks proof.
C) He provides a counterexample.
D) He changes the subject.

Key Takeaway
Unproven claims, even if plausible, are not accepted in kalam; every assertion requires evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent made a claim without proof. In your own life, what assumption about Allah or faith have you held without verifying it from revelation? Will you seek evidence today?
?
The author says 'this is arbitrary' – when have you accepted a theological claim simply because it sounded logical? Commit to replacing speculation with knowledge from the Quran and Sunnah this week.
?
This passage shows that even plausible claims need proof. What area of your aqeedah do you rely on 'it just makes sense' rather than actual evidence? What step will you take to learn the proofs?

Refuting the Opponent: The Substance-Accident Dichotomy for Atomic Substance
The author refutes an opponent using the substance-accident dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the dichotomy of substance and accident to refute the opponent and establish the existence of atomic substance as a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
An entity is either a substance or an accident; both paths lead to the atomic substance.

In this passage, the author employs a powerful logical dichotomy to dismantle the opponent's argument. By considering only two possibilities—substance or accident—the author shows that in every case, the opponent's own reasoning leads to the conclusion of atomic substance, thus invalidating their objection. Let's trace the steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
2
Identify the two main branches
The author splits the possibilities into substance and accident. Note what follows from each branch.
Substance directly yields atomic substance; accident requires a locus.
3
Follow the accident branch
Within the accident branch, the author examines the locus. Trace the sub-cases: divisible or indivisible.
Divisible locus leads to a contradiction; indivisible locus is itself the atomic substance.
4
Notice the universal conclusion
The author concludes that on all suppositions, the opponent's proof is invalidated. Recognize the rhetorical force of this refutation.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this dichotomy is effective: it covers all possibilities and shows that the opponent's position is self-defeating.
This is a classic kalam refutation technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوهراً فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد وإن كانت عرضاً وجب أن يكون له محل. فمحلها إن كان منقسماً فقد جوزتم قيام غير المنقسم بالمنقسم، وإن كان غير منقسم فهو الجوهر الفرد، وعلى جميع التقديرات، يبطل دليلكم.
...as a substance, then the atomic substance is established; and if it is an accident, then it must have a locus. If that locus is divisible, then you have allowed the non-divisible to subsist in the divisible; and if it is indivisible, then it is the atomic substance. And on all suppositions, your proof is invalidated.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation argument employing a dichotomy. The author considers two possibilities for the entity in question: it is either a substance or an accident. If it is a substance, this directly establishes the existence of the atomic substance (al-jawhar al-fard). If it is an accident, it must inhere in a locus. The author then examines the locus: if the locus is divisible, then it would mean an indivisible accident (the one being argued) subsists in a divisible subject, which the opponent presumably rejects. If the locus is indivisible, then it is itself the atomic substance. In either case, the opponent's argument is refuted.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
عرض
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the existence of atomic substance cannot be proven logically; it is merely a speculative doctrine.
Correction: The author shows that from the dichotomy of substance and accident, the atomic substance necessarily follows: either directly if the entity is a substance, or indirectly as the indivisible locus of an accident.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities the author considers for the entity in question?
A) substance or accident
B) existent or non-existent
C) indivisible or divisible
D) one or many

Key Takeaway
By using the dichotomy of substance and accident, the author demonstrates that atomic substance is unavoidable, effectively turning the opponent's own argument against them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage forces you to admit that atomic substance is unavoidable—everything rests on an indivisible foundation. What is the one indivisible foundation that your own life is built upon? Is it solid enough to withstand every challenge, or have you been building on compromises?
?
The opponent’s argument collapses because it leads to contradictions. Are there beliefs or habits in your own life that, if examined honestly, would also collapse? What would it cost you to let go of a cherished argument that does not match reality?
?
The dichotomy forces a choice: either substance or accident, either divisible or indivisible. In your relationship with truth, is there a clear choice you have been avoiding—a line you must cross today to stand on solid ground rather than on shifting sand?

The Sixth Argument: Indivisibility of the Accident of Number
Presents the sixth argument against accident divisibility using number ten

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the accident of tenness cannot be divided even though the number ten can be split into units.

Core Idea
A single concept cannot be divided, even if its physical substrate can.

This lesson presents the sixth argument from the author's series against the divisibility of accidents when their substrates divide. Using the example of number ten, the argument demonstrates that the accident of tenness is a single, indivisible concept. The passage establishes that numbers have distinct essences and that being ten is an accident, then refutes two possible ways it could be divided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the concise style of kalam reasoning.
The passage uses technical terms like 'طباع الأعداد' (natures of numbers) and 'عرض' (accident).
2
Understand the premise about number natures
Notice that the author begins by stating that numbers have different essences, and that being ten is an accident distinct from being nine. This establishes the basis for the argument.
The accidents fall under the genus of discrete quantity (الكم المنفصل).
3
Examine the two options for tenness
The argument presents two possibilities: either the single essence of tenness attaches to each unit, or it divides along with the units. Identify both options.
4
Grasp why both options fail
The first option is impossible because it would make each unit ten and one accident inhere in many substrates. The second is impossible because the concept of tenness does not accept division. Understand how these refutations prove indivisibility.
The argument also addresses a potential objection from an opponent about division of tenness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن طباع الأعداد ماهيات مختلفة . فكون الشيء : عشرة : عرض ، وكونه تسعة : عرض آخر . وهذان العرضان : [ نوعان ] مختلفان بالماهية ، داخلان تحت جنس الكم المنفصل . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المفهوم من كون العشرة : عشرة : مفهوم واحد ، وماهية واحدة . فتلك الماهية الواحدة ، إما أن تكون عارضة لكل واحد من تلك الأحاد ، وإما أن تنقسم بحسب انقسام تلك الآحاد . والأول محال . وإلا لزم أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأحاد ، موصوفاً بأنه عشرة . وذلك محال ، وأنه يلزم قيام العرض الواحد بالمحال الكثيرة . وهو محال والثاني أيضاً : محال لأن المفهوم من كون العشرة عشرة ، لا يقبل القسمة [ نعم العشرة تقبل القسمة ] أما مجرد العشرية فإنها لا تقبل القسمة البتة . فإن نازع الخصم فيه فنقول : لو انقسمت العشرية لكان كل واحد من أجزائها ، إما أن يكون عشرية ، وإما أن لا يكون [ عشرية ] ويذكر التقسيم الذي ذكروه في العلم إلى آخره
The sixth argument: Indeed, the natures of numbers are different essences. So the thing being ten is an accident, and its being nine is another accident. These two accidents are [two types] differing in essence, falling under the genus of discrete quantity. When you understand this, we say: The concept of ten being ten is one concept and one essence. That one essence either occurs to each one of those units, or divides according to the division of those units. The first is impossible; otherwise it would necessitate that each one of those units be described as 'ten', and that is impossible, and it would necessitate that one accident inhere in many substrates, which is impossible. The second also is impossible, because the concept of ten being ten does not accept division. [Yes, ten accepts division] but mere tenness does not accept division at all. If the opponent disputes this, we say: If tenness were divided, each one of its parts would either be tenness or not [tenness]. And mention the division they mentioned in [the science?] to the end.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument against the divisibility of accidents when their substrate divides. The argument uses the example of numbers, specifically the accident of 'ten-ness'. First, the author establishes that numbers have distinct essences (ماهيات) and that being ten is an accident, as is being nine. These accidents differ essentially and fall under the category of discrete quantity (الكم المنفصل). Then the argument proceeds: the concept of 'ten' is a single, unified concept. This single essence (الماهية الواحدة) can either (1) attach to each individual unit of the number ten, or (2) divide along with the division of the units. The first option is impossible because it would require each unit to be described as 'ten', which is false, and it would require one accident to inhere in multiple substrates, which is also impossible. The second option is also impossible because the concept of 'ten' as such does not accept division—though the number ten itself can be divided, mere 'tenness' (العشرية) is indivisible. If the opponent insists it can be divided, the author notes a standard division argument: the parts would either be tennness or not, leading to contradictions. The argument concludes that the accident of number is indivisible, supporting the broader thesis that attributes do not divide with their substrates.

Key Terms
طباع الأعداد
ماهيات
عرض
كم منفصل
مفهوم
ماهية
العشرية
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the number ten can be divided into units, the accident of tenness is also divisible.
Correction: The accident of tenness is a single concept that does not accept division; only the physical number (the units) can be divided.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, why is it impossible for the accident of tenness to be divided among the units of ten?
A) Because the units are not actually parts of the accident.
B) Because the concept of tenness is one and does not accept division.
C) Because tenness is a substance, not an accident.
D) Because division would require tenness to be a universal.

Key Takeaway
The accident of number is indivisible because its conceptual essence remains single and undivided even if the physical number can be split into parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that one accident cannot inhere in many substrates. In your daily life, does the accident of 'attentiveness to Allah' inhere in your heart while you are simultaneously attentive to many worldly distractions? What would you need to remove for that single accident to inhere fully?
?
The concept of tenness is argued to be indivisible—it remains one essence even when the number is divided. Reflect on your own concept of 'sincerity' (ikhlas). Is it a single, indivisible reality in your actions, or do you allow it to fragment into motives? How will you protect its unity in your next act of worship?
?
If the accident of number cannot be divided without losing its nature, consider the accident of 'love for Allah'. Can it be divided among multiple objects and still remain true love for Him? What does this argument demand of your heart's attachment?

The Seventh Argument: Indivisibility of Qualities like Circularity
Presents the seventh argument against accident divisibility using circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument against the divisibility of accidents, using circularity to show that qualities like circularity cannot be divided into parts.

Core Idea
Circularity cannot be divided; if it were, parts would either be circles or fail to produce circularity.

This lesson presents the seventh argument, which targets the philosophers' claim that qualities like circularity are accidents. The argument demonstrates that such qualities cannot be divided along with their substrates. By examining the case of circularity, we see that any division leads to a contradiction or an indivisible accident inhering in a divisible substrate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Notice that the argument starts by stating that qualities like circularity are accidents according to the philosophers. This sets up what is being challenged.
2
Examine the first possibility
The accident could inhere wholly in each part. What would that imply? Each part of the circle would be a circle, which is impossible.
3
Examine the second possibility
The accident could divide into parts. Then the argument explores two subcases: either the part is itself circularity or not. Follow each subcase to its conclusion.
The subcases lead to either a contradiction or an indivisible accident in a divisible substrate.
4
Understand the final refutation
The argument concludes that all options lead to impossibility, so the original claim that such qualities are divisible accidents must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ، مثل الإستدارة والتثليث والتربيع . عند الفلاسفة : أعراض موجودة . فنقول : هيئة الإستدارة إن كانت عرضاً ، فذلك العرض إما أن يكون بتمامه قائماً بكل واحد من الأجزاء ، فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد من أجزاء الدائرة دائرة. هذا خلف. وإما أن ينقسم ذلك العرض إلى الأجزاء ، ويقوم بكل واحد من أجزاء الخط ، جزء من أجزاء ذلك العرض ، وهذا أيضاً باطل . لأن جزء الإستدارة إن كان في نفسه استدارة ، عاد ما ذكرناه من أنه يلزم أن يكون جزء الدائرة : دائرة . وإن لم يكن كذلك فحينئذ يكون القائم بكل واحد من أجزاء [ الخط ] شيئاً ، ليس هو في نفسه استدارة ، فعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء ، إن لم يحدث أمر زائد ، وجب أن لا تحصل الإستدارة ، وإن حصل . فذلك الحاصل إن كان منقسماً ، عاد التقسيم الأول ، وإن لم ينقسم فحينئذ يكون الحال [ غير منقسم ] ويكون المحل منقسماً . وذلك يبطل كلامهم
The seventh argument: Qualities specific to quantities, like circularity, triangularity, and squareness, are, according to the philosophers, existing accidents. So we say: The form of circularity, if it is an accident, then that accident is either wholly inhering in each one of the parts, which necessitates that each one of the parts of the circle be a circle—this is a contradiction. Or that accident divides into parts and a part of that accident inheres in each one of the parts of the line, and this also is false. Because if the part of circularity is itself circularity, we return to what we mentioned that it necessitates that the part of the circle be a circle. And if it is not so, then what inheres in each of the parts of the line is something that is not in itself circularity. Then upon the aggregation of those parts, if no additional thing occurs, then circularity must not obtain; and if it does obtain, then that obtained thing, if it is divided, the first division returns, and if it is not divided, then the state (الحال) is undivided while the substrate is divided. And that nullifies their statement.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which focuses on qualities specific to quantities, such as circularity (استدارة), triangularity, and squareness. The author notes that, according to the philosophers, these are accidents. The argument uses the example of circularity: the form (هيئة) of circularity is an accident. This accident must either (1) inhere wholly in each part of the circular line, which would make every part a circle—an impossibility—or (2) divide such that a part of circularity inheres in each part of the line. The second option is also false because: if the part of circularity is itself circularity, then the same problem arises (each part would be a circle); if it is not circularity, then the parts when combined would not produce circularity unless an additional thing arises. If that additional thing is divisible, we are back to the first division; if it is indivisible, then we have an indivisible accident (الحال) inhering in a divisible substrate, which contradicts the opponent's claim that accidents divide with their substrates. Thus, the argument refutes the divisibility of such qualities.

Key Terms
كيفيات
كميات
استدارة
تثليث
تربيع
عرض
هيئة
جزء
دائرة
خط
محل
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that since a circle's circumference can be divided into arcs, the quality of circularity itself can be divided into parts.
Correction: The argument shows that the quality of circularity is not divisible; dividing the line does not divide the quality. Any division leads to contradictions or an indivisible accident.

Checkpoint
In the seventh argument, what is the consequence if a part of the accident of circularity is not itself circularity?
A) The parts would still produce circularity when combined.
B) Circularity would not obtain unless an additional thing arises.
C) The accident would necessarily be a substance.
D) The accident would be divisible into infinite parts.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument demonstrates that qualities like circularity cannot be divided because division leads to contradictions or an indivisible accident in a divisible substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If circularity cannot be divided, what does that imply about your own understanding of the world around you? Are there things you take as divisible that might actually be indivisible in their essence? Will you begin to question the assumptions you make about the nature of reality?
?
The argument relies on logical deduction to refute a philosophical claim. When you hold a belief, do you subject it to the same rigorous logical scrutiny? Or do you accept things based on common opinion? Commit to examining one of your own beliefs this week with the same method.
?
The philosophers assumed qualities are accidents divisible with their subjects. The mutakallimun showed this leads to absurdity. In your life, are there assumptions you hold that, if pursued logically, would lead to contradictions? Will you have the courage to trace your assumptions to their end and abandon them if they lead to falsehood?

The Seventh Objection: Roundness and the Indivisible State
The author presents the seventh objection against the principle that the state divides with the locus, using roundness as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of roundness demonstrates that a state (accident) can be indivisible even when its locus is divisible, refuting the philosophers' principle.

Core Idea
Roundness cannot be divided across parts — proving states can be indivisible.

The author now attacks a key principle of the philosophers: that any state (accident) must divide when its subject divides. He uses geometric qualities like roundness to show a contradiction. This argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum, forcing us to accept that an indivisible state can exist in a divisible locus. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice that the author presents two options for how roundness could relate to the parts of a circle.
2
Identify the two options
The argument considers two possibilities: either the entire accident resides in each part, or it divides among the parts. Identify each and note the immediate problem with the first option.
3
Follow the second option in detail
If the accident divides, examine whether a part of roundness is itself roundness. Trace the two sub-cases and their consequences.
The key is the final conclusion: if an additional thing arises and is indivisible, then the state is indivisible while the locus is divisible.
4
Understand the final refutation
The conclusion that an indivisible state exists in a divisible locus directly contradicts the philosophers' principle. See how this invalidates their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، مثل الإستدارة والتثليث والتربيع. عند الفلاسفة : أعراض موجودة. فنقول : هيئة الإستدارة إن كانت عرضاً، فذلك العرض إما أن يكون بتمامه قائماً بكل واحد من الأجزاء، فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد من أجزاء الدائرة دائرة. هذا خلف. وإما أن ينقسم ذلك العرض إلى الأجزاء، ويقوم بكل واحد من أجزاء الخط، جزء من أجزاء ذلك العرض، وهذا أيضاً باطل . لأن جزء الإستدارة إن كان في نفسه استدارة، عاد ما ذكرناه من أنه يلزم أن يكون جزء الدائرة : دائرة . وإن لم يكن كذلك فحينئذ يكون القائم بكل واحد من أجزاء الخط شيئاً، ليس هو في نفسه استدارة، فعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء، إن لم يحدث أمر زائد، وجب أن لا تحصل الإستدارة، وإن حصل . فذلك الحاصل إن كان منقسماً، عاد التقسيم الأول، وإن لم ينقسم فحينئذ يكون الحال غير منقسم ويكون المحل منقسماً . وذلك يبطل كلامهم .
The seventh proof: The qualities specific to quantities, such as roundness, triangularity, and squareness. According to the philosophers: they are existing accidents. We say: If the form of roundness is an accident, then that accident is either entirely subsisting in each of the parts, so it necessarily follows that each of the parts of a circle is a circle. This is a contradiction. Or that accident divides into parts, and a part of that accident subsists in each of the parts of the line. This is also false. Because if a part of roundness is in itself roundness, then what we mentioned returns, that it necessarily follows that a part of the circle is a circle. And if it is not so, then that which subsists in each of the parts of the line is something that is not in itself roundness. So when those parts come together, if no additional thing occurs, then it is necessary that roundness does not obtain. And if it does obtain, then that which obtains: if it is divisible, the first division returns; and if it is not divisible, then the state would be indivisible and the locus would be divisible. And that invalidates their statement.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection against the philosophers' principle that "the state must divide with the division of its locus." The author examines qualities specific to quantities, such as roundness, triangularity, and squareness, which the philosophers consider to be accidents existing in bodies. The argument focuses on roundness (istidāra). Two possibilities are considered: First, if the accident of roundness is entirely present in each part of the circle, then each part would itself be a circle—an absurd conclusion (khulf). Second, if the accident divides so that a part of roundness inheres in each part of the line (the circumference), then that part of roundness must be examined: if it is itself roundness, the same absurdity recurs; if it is not, then when the parts come together, either no new thing arises (so roundness cannot appear) or something new arises. In the latter case, that new thing must be either divisible (leading to the same regress) or indivisible—contradicting the original claim that the state (here roundness) divides with its locus. Thus, the philosophers' position is invalidated.

Key Terms
استدارة
عرض
قسمة
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an accident can be partially present in each part of its subject, and the parts together produce the whole accident without any additional entity.
Correction: The author shows that if a part of roundness is not itself roundness, then simply bringing parts together cannot produce roundness unless a new indivisible state arises, which contradicts the principle that states must divide.

Checkpoint
In the seventh objection, what is the consequence if the accident of roundness is entirely present in each part of the circle?
A) Each part becomes a part of roundness.
B) Each part becomes a circle itself.
C) Roundness disappears.
D) The parts remain straight lines.

Key Takeaway
An indivisible state (like roundness) can exist in a divisible locus, disproving the philosophers' principle that every state must divide with its locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your faith as something that can be divided into parts—intellect, emotion, action—each functioning independently? How does the indivisibility of roundness in this argument challenge you to see tawhid as an indivisible state that must encompass your entire being?
?
The philosophers assumed every state must divide with its locus. In your life, do you assume that spiritual growth must be measurable or divisible into stages? What if sincerity (ikhlas) is an indivisible state that cannot be partially attained? How does that change your daily worship?

The Eighth Objection: Divisibility of Possibility and Necessity-Through-Another
The author presents the eighth objection regarding the divisibility of possibility and necessity-through-another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth objection uses the divisibility of accidental attributes like possibility to challenge the principle that states must divide with their locus.

Core Idea
If possibility divides, then parts of it would equal the whole—absurd.

The author now presents the final objection in a series targeting the claim that 'the state must divide with the division of its locus.' He turns to the attributes of possibility and necessity-through-another that characterize contingent bodies. By examining what happens if these attributes divide or not, he shows the principle leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases considered
Read the passage and find the two conditional scenarios: if the concepts do not divide with the locus, or if they do. Notice how the author sets up a disjunction.
The passage begins with 'إن لم تنقسم' and 'وإن انقسمت'.
2
Understand the absurdity of part equaling whole
If each part of possibility is itself possibility, then the part would be exactly like the whole in being possible. Reflect on why this is a problem: a part is not the whole.
This absurdity is the core of the objection.
3
Connect to the overall refutation
The author concludes that these eight objections together refute the principle that states must divide with their locus. Think about how this specific objection fits the pattern.
The phrase 'فهذه النقوض الثمانية' signals the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام ممكنة بذواتها، واجبة بغيرها. فكونها ممكنة بذواتها، واجبة بغيرها صفات عرضية خارجة عن الماهية. فنقول : هذه المفهومات إن لم تنقسم الإنقسام محلها، فقد بطل الكلام المذكور، وإن انقسمت فكل واحد من أجزاء الإمكان، ومن أجزاء الوجوب بالغير، إن كان في نفسه إمكاناً ووجوباً بالغير، لزم أن يكون الجزء مساوياً للكل . وإن لم يكن كذلك، فعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء، إما أن يحدث أمر زائد، أو . ونسوق التقسيم إلى آخره. فهذه النقوض الثمانية واردة على قوله : و الحال يجب أن ينقسم بإنقسام المحل، والله أعلم.
The eighth proof: There is no doubt that these bodies are contingent (mumkin) in themselves and necessary (wajib) through another. So their being contingent in themselves and necessary through another are accidental attributes (sifat 'aradiyya) external to the essence. We say: These concepts: if they do not divide with the division of their locus, then the mentioned statement is invalid. And if they do divide, then each of the parts of possibility (imkan) and each of the parts of necessity-through-another (wujud bil-ghayr), if it is in itself possibility and necessity-through-another, then it necessarily follows that the part is equal to the whole. And if it is not so, then when those parts come together, either an additional thing occurs, or [not]. And we carry the division to its end. So these eight objections are directed against his statement: 'the state must divide with the division of the locus.' And God knows best.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final objection against the principle that "the state must divide with the division of its locus." The author turns to the attributes of possibility (imkān) and necessity-through-another (wujūb bil-ghayr) that characterize contingent bodies. These are accidental attributes external to the essence. The argument proceeds by disjunction: either these concepts do not divide when their locus divides (contradicting the principle) or they do divide. If they divide, each part of possibility or necessity-through-another is itself possibility or necessity-through-another, making the part equal to the whole—an absurdity. If they do not have this property, then when the parts come together, either an additional thing arises (which would then face the same divisibility dilemma) or nothing arises, in which case the whole lacks the attribute. The author concludes by stating that these eight objections collectively refute the claim that the state must divide with the division of its locus.

Key Terms
إمكان
وجوب بالغير
صفة عرضية
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that possibility is not an accidental attribute but a necessary property of essence.
Correction: The author treats possibility and necessity-through-another as accidental (صفات عرضية) for the sake of refutation, accepting the opponent's framework.

Checkpoint
In the eighth objection, what is the consequence if each part of possibility is itself possibility?
A) The part would be equal to the whole in possibility.
B) The part would be necessary.
C) The part would be impossible.
D) The part would be divisible.

Key Takeaway
The eighth objection shows that if accidental attributes like possibility divide, then parts equal the whole, an impossible consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson presents a logical refutation that hinges on an absurd consequence. When you encounter a belief you hold that leads to an absurdity if followed to its conclusion, are you willing to abandon that belief? Or do you rationalize to avoid the discomfort?
?
The author treats contingency as an accidental attribute. Do you ever treat your own dependence on God as something external to your essence, as if it were peripheral rather than central to who you are?
?
The argument forces a choice between two branches, both leading to contradiction. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you avoid making a decisive choice because both options seem costly? How does this lesson’s rigorous logic challenge you to face such dilemmas?

First Division: Similar Parts and Accidental Indwelling
First part of the proof: similar parts lead to accidental indwelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the proof that if intellectual forms were bodily with parts similar in essence, their presence in the entire locus would be accidental.

Core Idea
If a form fully exists in a part, its presence in the whole is accidental.

Sanūsī begins his proof by considering the possibility that an intellectual form might be bodily and composed of parts that are similar in essence. He argues that if such a form could reside entirely in a part of a body, then its presence in the whole body would be an accidental attribute, not essential. This sets the stage for a deeper exploration of the nature of intellectual forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first division
Carefully read the Arabic passage describing the first division. Note how it introduces the idea that each part of the form suffices with a part of the locus.
This division assumes parts are alike in essence (متماثلة).
2
Understand the equality argument
The passage states that a part of the form is equal to the whole in complete essence. Why would that imply that the form can exist entirely in a part? Think about the principle: 'the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like.'
3
Grasp the conclusion about accidentality
If the form can exist wholly in a part, then its existence in the entire locus is not necessary but accidental. What does this imply about the form's relationship to the body?
This leads to the second division which shows the impossibility of the bodily nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسامها مغشاة بعوارض غريبة ، من جمع وتفريق ، ويكون في أقل بلاغ . هذا لفظ الإشارات وتفسيره . إن كل واحد من جزئها مكتفي بجزء من ذلك المحل ، وذلك الجزء من تلك الصورة مساوي لكلها في تمام الماهية ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ، فوجب أن يكون حلول كل تلك الصورة في جزء من ذلك المحل ممكناً . وإذا كان كذلك كان حلول كل تلك الصورة في كل ذلك المحل عارضاً غريباً . وفي أقل منه بلاغ . وأما القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين في أول الدليل : وهو أن يقال : إن تلك الصورة تنقسم إلى جزءين مختلفين في الماهية . فنقول : هذا محال . لأن الصورة العقلية لو كانت جسمانية ، لوجبت لها أجزاء بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة في الجسم، لكن تلك [ الانقسامات غير متناهية ] . فوجب أن تكون الأجزاء المختلفة الحاصلة لتلك الصورة العقلية غير متناهية . وهذا محال . لوجوه : الأول : إنه إذا كان كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء التي هي غير متناهية حالاً في جزء آخر من ذلك المحل ، فقد اختص كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الجسم بعرض مخالف للعرض الحال في الجزء الآخر ، وذلك يوجب تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل . والثاني : وهو أن تلك الكثرة سواء كانت متناهية ، أو غير متناهية فلا بد فيها من واحد ، فتلك الصورة العقلية إذا كانت مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية فقد حصل فيها جزء واحد ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور . الثالث : إنا نفرض أن الجزء الجنسي ، وقع في أحد النصفين . والجزء الفصلي وقع في النصف الثاني . فإذا غيرنا القسمة ، وجب أن ينتقل الجزء الجنسي من موضعه، إلى الموضع الثاني ، الحاصل بسبب الغرض الثاني من القسمة وأيضاً فإذا أوقعنا قسماً في قسم مثل هذا : فإنه يلزم أن يتنصف الجنس ، وإن لم يتنصف الفصل . وذلك محال . فهذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة على ما ذكره الشيخ .
Its division is covered by accidental attributes, of composition and separation, and it occurs in less than a moment. This is the wording of al-Ishārāt and its interpretation. Indeed, each one of its parts suffices with a part of that locus, and that part of that form is equal to the whole in complete essence, and the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like; so it must be that the indwelling of the entire form in a part of that locus is possible. And if this is so, then the indwelling of the entire form in the entire locus is an accidental occurrence. And in less than it is sufficient. As for the second of the two divisions mentioned at the beginning of the proof—which is to say that that form divides into two parts differing in essence—we say: this is impossible. For if the intellectual form were bodily, parts would necessarily be required for it according to the possible divisions in the body, but those divisions are infinite. So the different parts resulting from that intellectual form must be infinite. And this is impossible, for reasons: The first: that if each one of those infinite parts is indwelling in another part of that locus, then each part of that body becomes characterized by an accident different from the accident in the other part, and that necessitates the composition of the body from actually infinite parts. The second: that that multitude, whether finite or infinite, must have a unity; so if that intellectual form is composed of infinite parts, then a single part results within it, and then the aforementioned division returns. The third: we suppose that the generic part falls in one half and the specific part falls in the other half. Then if we change the division, the generic part must move from its place to the second place resulting from the second purpose of the division; also, if we place a division within a division like this, it necessitates that the genus be halved even if the species is not halved. That is impossible. This is the complete exposition of this proof according to what al-Shaykh mentioned.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the claim that intellectual forms (الصور العقلية) can be bodily. The proof is structured around two possible divisions of the form. The first division is into parts similar in essence; here it is argued that if the form exists in a part of the body, its existence in the whole body becomes accidental. The second division is into parts differing in essence; this is deemed impossible. The impossibility is demonstrated by three reasons: first, if the parts are infinite and each occupies a distinct part of the body, the body would be composed of actually infinite parts, which is absurd. Second, any multitude requires a unity, but if the form has infinite parts, it would itself have a part, leading to regress. Third, a specific hypothetical case involving generic and specific parts shows that such division would force the genus to be halved without the species, which is impossible. The passage concludes that this completes the exposition of the proof as given by al-Shaykh.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
الماهية
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if a form is divisible, it must have parts differing in kind.
Correction: The first division assumes parts are alike in essence (متماثلة), so the argument shows that even then, bodily indwelling leads to accidental existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first division, if a part of the intellectual form is equal to the whole in complete essence, what follows?
A) The form cannot exist in any part of the body.
B) The form can exist wholly in a part of the body.
C) The form must exist only in the whole body.
D) The form has no parts.

Key Takeaway
If an intellectual form were bodily with similar parts, its presence in the whole body would be merely accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you believe to be an essential part of your identity. If it were only accidentally attached to you, like a garment, would your soul still be intact? This lesson forces you to examine what is truly essential to your spiritual reality. Are you prepared to distinguish between what is accidental and what is essential in your own character?
?
The proof uses a hypothetical division to reveal a truth about the immaterial soul. When you face doubts about spiritual matters, do you entertain hypotheticals honestly, or do you avoid them? This lesson demands that you follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it challenges your assumptions. What hypothetical about your own soul are you afraid to consider?

Second Division: Differing Parts and Infinite Regress
Second division and three reasons for impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second part of the proof showing that if intellectual forms were bodily and had parts differing in essence, it would lead to infinite parts and multiple absurdities.

Core Idea
Bodily intellectual forms would require infinite diverse parts, which is impossible.

Having shown the problems with similar parts, the proof turns to the possibility of parts differing in essence (مختلفة في الماهية). This division is deemed impossible because it would force the form to have infinitely many parts. The text then gives three reasons why infinite parts are absurd, each targeting different aspects of the bodily hypothesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division passage
Read the Arabic from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end. Notice that it declares this division impossible and then lists three reasons.
The three reasons are introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand why infinite parts follow
The proof argues that if the form is bodily, it must have parts corresponding to all possible divisions of the body. Since there are infinite possible divisions, the form would have infinite parts differing in essence. Why is this problematic?
3
Analyze the three reasons
Each reason shows a contradiction: (1) body composed of actually infinite parts, (2) regress in the form itself, (3) halving genus without species. Work through each one carefully.
These are classic absurdities in kalām against infinite actual parts.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The passage concludes that this completes the proof according to al-Shaykh. How does the impossibility of the second division reinforce the overall argument?
Both divisions lead to contradictions, so the bodily nature is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسامها مغشاة بعوارض غريبة ، من جمع وتفريق ، ويكون في أقل بلاغ . هذا لفظ الإشارات وتفسيره . إن كل واحد من جزئها مكتفي بجزء من ذلك المحل ، وذلك الجزء من تلك الصورة مساوي لكلها في تمام الماهية ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ، فوجب أن يكون حلول كل تلك الصورة في جزء من ذلك المحل ممكناً . وإذا كان كذلك كان حلول كل تلك الصورة في كل ذلك المحل عارضاً غريباً . وفي أقل منه بلاغ . وأما القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين في أول الدليل : وهو أن يقال : إن تلك الصورة تنقسم إلى جزءين مختلفين في الماهية . فنقول : هذا محال . لأن الصورة العقلية لو كانت جسمانية ، لوجبت لها أجزاء بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة في الجسم، لكن تلك [ الانقسامات غير متناهية ] . فوجب أن تكون الأجزاء المختلفة الحاصلة لتلك الصورة العقلية غير متناهية . وهذا محال . لوجوه : الأول : إنه إذا كان كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء التي هي غير متناهية حالاً في جزء آخر من ذلك المحل ، فقد اختص كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الجسم بعرض مخالف للعرض الحال في الجزء الآخر ، وذلك يوجب تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل . والثاني : وهو أن تلك الكثرة سواء كانت متناهية ، أو غير متناهية فلا بد فيها من واحد ، فتلك الصورة العقلية إذا كانت مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية فقد حصل فيها جزء واحد ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور . الثالث : إنا نفرض أن الجزء الجنسي ، وقع في أحد النصفين . والجزء الفصلي وقع في النصف الثاني . فإذا غيرنا القسمة ، وجب أن ينتقل الجزء الجنسي من موضعه، إلى الموضع الثاني ، الحاصل بسبب الغرض الثاني من القسمة وأيضاً فإذا أوقعنا قسماً في قسم مثل هذا : فإنه يلزم أن يتنصف الجنس ، وإن لم يتنصف الفصل . وذلك محال . فهذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة على ما ذكره الشيخ .

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the claim that intellectual forms (الصور العقلية) can be bodily. The proof is structured around two possible divisions of the form. The first division is into parts similar in essence; here it is argued that if the form exists in a part of the body, its existence in the whole body becomes accidental. The second division is into parts differing in essence; this is deemed impossible. The impossibility is demonstrated by three reasons: first, if the parts are infinite and each occupies a distinct part of the body, the body would be composed of actually infinite parts, which is absurd. Second, any multitude requires a unity, but if the form has infinite parts, it would itself have a part, leading to regress. Third, a specific hypothetical case involving generic and specific parts shows that such division would force the genus to be halved without the species, which is impossible. The passage concludes that this completes the exposition of the proof as given by al-Shaykh.

Key Terms
القسم الثاني
الانقسام
غير متناهية
الجزء الجنسي
الجزء الفصلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that infinite parts are possible if they are merely potential.
Correction: The proof argues that the parts would be actual (already وجود بالفعل) because each occupies a distinct part of the body, leading to an actual infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, what would be the consequence of each infinite part of the form indwelling in a different part of the body?
A) The body would become indivisible.
B) The body would be composed of actually infinite parts.
C) The form would become simple.
D) The parts would merge into one.

Key Takeaway
The second division shows that a bodily intellectual form would entail infinite parts, leading to absurdities like an actually infinite body and logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your mind can grasp concepts like 'humanity' without any physical parts. After reading this proof, do you still believe your thoughts are merely brain activity? If not, how does this affect your view of your own intellect's dignity and responsibility?
?
If intellectual forms were bodily, they would be limited and divisible. Yet you can understand infinite concepts like Allah's attributes. Does this proof challenge you to rely more on your intellect as a gift from Allah, rather than reducing it to material processes? What practical change will you make today to honor your intellect?
?
The third reason shows that logical parts like genus and species cannot be physically moved or halved. When you try to categorize things in your life, do you sometimes treat them as rigid, physical boxes? How can this lesson free you to see concepts as immaterial and unified, leading to deeper understanding?

Refuting the Definition of Knowledge
Sanusi gives the first reason for the proof's weakness: its faulty definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Sanusi refutes the proof by attacking its foundational definition of knowledge as the presence of an equivalent form.

Core Idea
The first objection demolishes the proof by rejecting its theory of knowledge.

Imam al-Sanusi begins his refutation by striking at the very foundation of the previous proof. He declares that the proof is built on a flawed definition of knowledge—that knowledge is the presence of a form equivalent to the object in the knower's mind. He claims to have already disproven this definition in the logic section of this book. This lesson examines that first objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author asserts the weakness of the proof and immediately identifies the first flaw.
The author uses the word 'mabni' (built upon) to show this is the foundation.
2
Identify the definition being attacked
The author says the proof is based on a specific definition of knowledge. Find that definition and note it.
The definition is: 'knowledge is the obtaining of a form equivalent to the essence of the known in the essence of the knower.'
3
Understand the refutation
The author says he has already proven the corruption of this doctrine with decisive proofs in the beginning of the logic section. This is a reference to earlier arguments.
We need to trust that these proofs exist; the current lesson is the refutation itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذه الحجة في غاية الضعف لوجوه : الأول : إنه مبني على أن العلم بالشيء عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لماهية المعلوم في ذات العالم . وقد بينا في أول منطق هذا الكتاب بالدلائل القاهرة : فساد هذا المذهب . الثاني : نقول : هب أنا سلمنا لكم هذه القاعدة . فلم لا يجوز أن تلك الصورة تنقسم إلى جزءين متشابهين في الماهية - قوله : « فعلى هذا التقدير تكون تلك الصورة صورة غير مجردة ، قلنا : الكلام من وجهين : الأول : وهو أن هذا لازم عليكم أيضاً . وذلك : لأن هذه الصورة ، إذا كانت حالة في جوهر النفس الناطقة ، فهي صورة جزئية ، حالة في نفس جزئية ، وتقارنها سائر الأعراض الحالة في تلك النفس الجزئية . فإذا اعتبرنا تلك الصورة مع جملة هذه اللواحق، لم تكن صورة مجردة . بل كانت مقرونة بلواحق كثيرة . وذلك يمنع من كونها كلية
Know that this proof is extremely weak for reasons: The first: it is based on the claim that knowledge of a thing consists in the obtaining of a form equivalent to the essence of the known in the essence of the knower. We have already shown at the beginning of the logic of this book, with conclusive proofs, the corruption of this doctrine. The second: we say: suppose we grant you this principle; then why is it not permissible that that form divides into two parts similar in essence? — His statement: 'On this supposition, that form would be a non-abstract form.' We say: the discussion is from two aspects: The first: that this [conclusion] is also necessary upon you; that is, because this form, if it is indwelling in the substance of the rational soul, is a particular form, indwelling in a particular soul, and is accompanied by other accidents indwelling in that particular soul. So if we consider that form together with all these concomitants, it would not be an abstract form; rather, it would be connected with many concomitants. And that prevents it from being universal.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains the author's refutation of the proof presented in the previous lesson. The author declares the proof extremely weak and gives three reasons, though only the first two are fully stated. The first reason attacks the foundational premise that knowledge is the presence of a form equivalent to the object in the knower's essence; the author claims this has already been disproven. The second reason is more concessive: even if that premise were accepted, why could the form not divide into similar parts? The author then quotes an objection that such division would make the form non-abstract, and responds by noting that even on the opponent's view, the form in the rational soul is always particular and accompanied by accidents, so it is already non-abstract and cannot be universal. The third reason is cut off at the page break.

Key Terms
العلم
الصورة المساوية
المذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author rejects all proofs about intellectual forms.
Correction: The author only rejects the specific proof because it relies on a flawed definition of knowledge. He does not reject all proofs involving forms.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author gives for the weakness of the proof?
A) The proof assumes a false definition of knowledge.
B) The proof requires the form to be abstract.
C) The proof is based on an infinite regress.
D) The proof ignores the nature of the soul.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that a proof is only as strong as its foundational premises; if the definition of knowledge is wrong, the whole proof falls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you accept arguments simply because they are common or convenient, without checking if the key terms (like 'knowledge') are even defined correctly in the first place? Will you commit to examining the foundational premises of every belief you hold today?
?
The author points to earlier proofs he has already given. Are you someone who builds your faith on solid, reasoned foundations, or do you rely on weak proofs that you have never scrutinized? What concrete step will you take this week to verify a core belief using primary sources?
?
When a teacher or scholar says 'I have already proven this elsewhere,' do you trust them blindly, or do you go back and verify? In your own life, which doctrines do you accept without personal verification, and what will you do about it starting today?

The Second Objection: The Problem of Division and Abstractness
Sanusi presents the second objection and responds to the opponent's counter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection: even if the definition were accepted, the mental form could divide into similar parts without being abstract, and that the opponent's own view leads to non-abstractness.

Core Idea
Even granting the definition, the form could divide and still not be abstract, undermining the proof.

Imam al-Sanusi continues his refutation with a second objection that proceeds concessively. He says: suppose we grant you the definition of knowledge; even then, it is possible for the mental form to divide into two similar parts. The opponent objects that division would make the form non-abstract. But the author responds that, on the opponent's own view, the form in the rational soul is always particular and accompanied by accidents, so it is already non-abstract and cannot be universal. This lesson explores that dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the concessive 'هب أنا سلمنا لكم' (suppose we grant you).
This is a common dialectical technique: assuming the opponent's premise for argument's sake.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponent says: if the form divides into similar parts, then it would be a non-abstract form. Find that phrase.
The phrase is 'فعلى هذا التقدير تكون تلك الصورة صورة غير مجردة'.
3
Analyze the response
The author responds from two aspects, but only the first is given. The first aspect is a turning of the tables: the opponent's own view leads to the same consequence because the form in the soul is particular and accompanied by accidents.
The author argues that even without division, the form is already non-abstract due to its particularity and concomitants.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that because the form is always connected with many concomitants, it is not abstract and therefore cannot be universal. This undermines the proof's requirement.
The passage ends here; the second aspect and third objection are missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know that this proof is extremely weak for reasons: The first: it is based on the claim that knowledge of a thing consists in the obtaining of a form equivalent to the essence of the known in the essence of the knower. Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the author's response to the opponent's objection?
A) That the form cannot divide because it is abstract.
B) That the opponent's own view leads to the same consequence of non-abstractness.

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that even if the definition were sound, the mental form's particularity and accidents prevent it from being abstract and universal, which undercuts the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to have an 'abstract' understanding of a concept like justice or mercy, reflect: Is that understanding truly free from your personal attachments, experiences, and biases? How does this lesson's point about the form's particularity challenge your confidence in the purity of your own knowledge? What specific step can you take today to become more aware of your own biases in understanding Islamic teachings?
?
The opponent's objection backfired because it applied equally to his own view. Think of a criticism you have recently made about someone else's belief or practice. Could that same criticism apply to your own life? Identify one such criticism and write down how you might be falling into the same error. Commit to addressing it this week.
?
If your mental forms are always connected to your particular soul and its accidents, how does that affect your humility before Allah? Are you quick to assume that your understanding of a religious text is the 'universal' truth? Pray this week for the ability to recognize the limitations of your own perspective and to seek knowledge with genuine openness.
